_Thought for 32s_

I pushed the next layer, and I think the most useful new reduction is this:

> **Any hidden state that survives on the exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) lane cannot be a finite-order local analytic two-atom datum built from the visible shadow/germ.**

That is stronger than what we had before. Before, the residual question was “does the actual state factor through the visible quotient/shadow package?” Now we can say more precisely: if factorization fails, the failure cannot come from any finite-order local analytic invariant of the paired source jets. It would have to be genuinely nonlocal, or come from some source-level object not expressible as a local analytic functor of the visible package. The ingredients behind this are already in hand: local exact repeated-value pairs on the fixed-shear slice are exactly the involution graph \(t\leftrightarrow -t\); the quotient-visible branch is one-dimensional, governed by \(\widehat Q\) and \(\widehat{\mathcal D}=\widehat Q'\); the visible chamber geometry is a canonical adjacent merge tree with coherent ties; and the current explicit pointwise package has no second primitive highest-new generator beyond the \(r=q^{(7)}\) tower.    

Here is the attack.

Take a close exact repeated-value pair on the exact fixed-shear slice. By the involutive local visibility theorem, it is necessarily
\[
(t_1,t_2)=(t,-t),
\qquad
u=t^2,
\]
so the pair is already parameterized by the single quotient variable \(u\). There is no second local branch choice. 

Now suppose a candidate hidden datum \(H\) is assigned to such a pair. Assume only that \(H\) is:

1. local and finite-order;
2. analytic in the jets of the visible/frozen projective shadow or of the descended local germ;
3. invariant under the involution-exchange of the two atoms, since \((t,-t)\) and \((-t,t)\) represent the same unordered overlap pair.

Then \(H\) must descend to a function of the quotient jet at \(u=t^2\). The reason is parity.

Write any visible local field \(F(t)\) on the fixed-shear slice as
\[
F(t)=F_{\mathrm{even}}(t)+F_{\mathrm{odd}}(t),
\qquad
F_{\mathrm{even}}(-t)=F_{\mathrm{even}}(t),\quad
F_{\mathrm{odd}}(-t)=-F_{\mathrm{odd}}(t).
\]
For the actual visible shadow package on the fixed-shear slice, the structural content is even stronger: the frozen projective shadow is already even under \(t\mapsto -t\), so the basic visible state is itself quotient-descended. 

A finite-order local two-atom invariant built from the pair of jets at \(t\) and \(-t\) can only depend on:
- symmetric combinations of even jets, which are already functions of \(u=t^2\);
- symmetric combinations of odd jets, but these occur only through even products or squares, hence again as quotient-descended functions of \(u\).

Equivalently, once the pair is forced to be \((t,-t)\), every involution-symmetric local analytic jet polynomial in the two atom-jets is an even analytic function of \(t\), hence an analytic function of \(u=t^2\).

So the correct theorem shape is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{No finite-order local hidden datum lemma.}
}
\]

On the exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) slice, every involution-symmetric finite-order local analytic two-atom invariant built from the visible shadow/germ jets of a close exact repeated-value pair \((t,-t)\) descends to the quotient parameter \(u=t^2\). In particular, it is already quotient-visible.

This is not just plausible; it is exactly what the involution-graph theorem is buying you. The pair is not a general two-point configuration anymore. It is a single-point quotient configuration wearing a doubled source presentation.

That gives an immediate corollary:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if a hidden state survives on }K_v=0,\text{ it is not a finite-order local analytic pair invariant of the visible package.}
}
\]

This meshes perfectly with the same-tower audit. The current explicit pointwise highest-new package has already been shown to stay inside the \(r\)-tower, with no second primitive pointwise generator. So not only is there no new pointwise field; there is also no finite-order local analytic two-atom hidden invariant obtained by symmetrizing paired visible jets.  

This sharpens the earlier trichotomy. We can now strike one whole class of possibilities off the list:

- not a hidden branch-pairing ambiguity;
- not a hidden visible-chamber update;
- not a finite-order local analytic symmetric pair invariant of the visible package.

So if the actual transported overlap / finite-core state fails to descend, the failure must be one of these narrower types:

1. a genuinely nonlocal transport object, depending on continuation/history rather than local jets;
2. a provenance-sensitive source-level object not expressible as a local analytic functor of the visible shadow/germ;
3. a non-finite-order quantity.

That is a real advance, because it says the remaining hiddenness — if it exists — is much more exotic than “some local two-point datum we forgot.”

There is another useful consequence.

The nonconstant quotient branch is already governed by \(\widehat Q\), and local close branches occur precisely at odd-order zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\); the only global nonlocal quotient-visible obstruction left is the three-run / three-lobe inequality \(B_1\ge B_0+B_2\) on a compressed branch.   So if one also proves that the actual transported state is determined by finite-order local visible data near each close pair, then the entire exact fixed-shear lane would collapse all the way to the quotient-visible scalar problem. The only thing preventing that final collapse is precisely the missing nonlocal/state-functoriality step.

So my updated frontier is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The exact fixed-shear }K_v=0\text{ lane has no remaining local finite-order hidden datum.}
}
\]

What remains is not “more local geometry.” It is one of two fundamental tasks:

- either prove the actual transported state is local/analytic in the visible package, in which case the hidden-state problem is over;
- or explicitly construct the extra nonlocal/provenance-sensitive datum, which would identify the true residual obstruction.

Between those two, the best next attack is even clearer than before:

### Best next subattack
Try to prove a **locality theorem for the actual transported state**:
\[
\Sigma_{\mathrm{actual}}(t,-t)
=
\mathcal F\bigl(\text{finite jet of visible shadow/germ at }u=t^2\bigr)
\]
for some analytic \(\mathcal F\).

Because once that is proved, the no-finite-order-local-hidden-datum lemma above immediately forces quotient visibility, and the odd-merger uniqueness/coherence machinery finishes the regular exact fixed-shear lane.

So the attack has now tightened the bottleneck again. The missing step is no longer “show there is no hidden state” in general. It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{show that the actual transported overlap state is a local analytic functor of the visible package.}
}
\]

If that fails, the failure must itself exhibit a genuinely nonlocal or provenance-sensitive invariant, which would be extremely informative.

I’m pushing exactly on that locality theorem now. The clean route is to try to show that any admissible “actual transported state” is forced to be built from finite jet data at the quotient-visible pair, and if that fails, isolate the minimal mechanism by which nonlocal dependence could enter.

_Thought for 6s_

I pushed the locality theorem route further, and I think the next real structural gain is this:

> **If locality fails, the failure cannot first appear at a close exact pair. It must already be present as a nonlocal transport functional on a regular chamber family.**

That sounds modest, but it sharply changes the battlefield. It means the exact fixed-shear bottleneck is no longer “some mysterious extra datum attached to a close pair.” If a hidden datum exists, it must be carried along the regular branch *before* the close pair is formed.

Here is the attack.

Let \(\Sigma_{\mathrm{act}}\) denote the actual transported overlap / finite-core state on the exact fixed-shear lane. We want to prove
\[
\Sigma_{\mathrm{act}}(t,-t)=\mathcal F(\text{finite visible jet at }u=t^2),
\]
or else understand why not.

We already know three strong facts.

First, local exact repeated pairs on the fixed-shear slice are exactly involutive:
\[
(t_1,t_2)=(t,-t),
\qquad u=t^2.
\]
So the close-pair locus itself is one-dimensional and quotient-visible. There is no local partner ambiguity.  

Second, any involution-symmetric finite-order local analytic two-atom invariant built from the visible package descends to \(u=t^2\). So there is no finite-order local hidden pair invariant left.  

Third, on the regular quotient-visible branch, the chamber geometry is a canonical adjacent merge tree with unique continuation and coherent odd mergers. So the visible regular branch carries no monodromy.

Put these together and you get the following dichotomy.

Assume locality fails. Then there exist two families \(A,B\) of exact fixed-shear configurations and a close repeated pair parameter \(u_*\) such that
\[
\mathsf V_A(u_*)=\mathsf V_B(u_*),
\qquad
\Sigma_{\mathrm{act},A}(u_*)\neq \Sigma_{\mathrm{act},B}(u_*),
\]
where \(\mathsf V\) is the full visible local package at the pair: quotient germ, involution-visible shadow, regular chamber label, merger ancestry, and finite visible jets.

Now choose such a counterexample with minimal visible complexity and, within that, minimal parameter distance back along the regular branch.

Because no finite-order local hidden datum exists at the pair itself, the first difference between \(A\) and \(B\) cannot be created *at* \(u_*\) by the close-pair local model. Therefore the difference must already be present on the incoming regular chamber transport before the pair closes.

That yields the sharper principle:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Any nonlocal hidden state must be a transport invariant of regular chamber families, not a local pair invariant.}
}
\]

This is better than what we had before, because it removes the temptation to keep searching for more pair-level jets or collision coefficients.

So I attacked the regular transport side.

On a regular exact fixed-shear chamber, the visible data are: a connected quotient interval, the sign of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), its position in the merge tree, and the descended shadow data. The chamber is simply connected in parameter, and the visible chamber graph is a tree. Therefore any hidden transport invariant on such a chamber must be one of only two types:

1. **a continuous holonomy-like variable** transported along the chamber, even though the chamber itself has no visible monodromy; or  
2. **an external provenance label** inherited from how the chamber was created higher up and not forgotten by visible mergers.

But the tree structure already kills honest holonomy. There is no loop on which to accumulate phase. So type (1) cannot be ordinary monodromy/holonomy in the geometric sense.

That leaves only a more rigid possibility:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If locality fails, the hidden datum must be a provenance label on chambers, preserved by regular transport and possibly not erased by mergers.}
}
\]

This is the cleanest new reduction I can justify.

So the problem becomes: can there exist a nontrivial chamber label \(L\) such that

- \(L\) is constant along each regular chamber;
- \(L\) is not determined by visible chamber data;
- odd adjacent merger does not necessarily erase \(L\);
- yet \(L\) is compatible with involution symmetry and visible tie coherence?

Now we can attack that directly.

A merger-compatible provenance label would amount to an update rule
\[
L_{\mathrm{parent}}=\mathcal M(L_1,L_2;\text{visible local germ}),
\]
defined on adjacent child chambers. But because the visible merge tree is noncrossing and ties are coherent, this \(\mathcal M\) must be associative and symmetric on every visible subtree:
\[
\mathcal M(\mathcal M(L_1,L_2),L_3)=\mathcal M(L_1,\mathcal M(L_2,L_3)),
\qquad
\mathcal M(L_1,L_2)=\mathcal M(L_2,L_1).
\]

This turns the hidden-state problem into an algebra problem.

A nontrivial hidden provenance theory would require a commutative associative binary operation on labels that is invisible on visible data but still changes the eventual corrected state.

So I asked: what kinds of label algebras are compatible with the geometry?

There are only a few possibilities:

- **idempotent**: \(\mathcal M(L,L)=L\);
- **absorbing/collapsing**: \(\mathcal M(L_1,L_2)\) depends only on visible data, so labels are erased;
- **parity-like** or group-like: \(\mathcal M\) combines labels nontrivially.

But parity-like/group-like labels immediately run into a visibility problem. Since the close pair is \((t,-t)\) and the involution is shadow-neutral, any label that distinguishes the two children individually has to disappear under unordered pairing unless it descends to a symmetric chamber quantity. And once it descends to a symmetric chamber quantity, it is exactly the kind of finite local visible datum we already ruled out.

So a nontrivial provenance label cannot be attached to individual atoms in any way detectable at the close pair. It would have to live at the level of entire chamber history and yet survive all symmetric/associative adjacent mergers without ever becoming visible. That is a very severe restriction.

This suggests the next candidate theorem:

### No invisible finite label theorem
Assume the actual transported state on regular exact fixed-shear chambers is determined by a finite chamber label propagated by a symmetric associative merger rule and evaluated only at unordered close pairs. Then that label factors through the visible merge-tree data. In particular, no extra invisible finite provenance label survives.

I do not yet have a full proof, but the proof strategy is becoming clear:

- Because the chamber graph is a rooted tree, every chamber label is determined by labels at leaves plus repeated applications of \(\mathcal M\).
- Symmetry and associativity make the resulting parent label depend only on the multiset of leaf labels below that chamber.
- But an unordered close pair on the exact fixed-shear slice only sees the outer visible chamber interval and its local quotient/shadow data, not an ordered multiset of hidden leaves.
- Therefore any label affecting the close-pair state must either collapse to a visible symmetric function of the chamber subtree, or else it is not well-defined under visible tie coherence / involution symmetry.

This is very close to a contradiction for finite hidden labels.

So the updated frontier is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{A hidden state cannot be a local pair invariant, and likely cannot be a finite invisible chamber label either.}
}
\]

If that is right, then the only remaining serious possibility is much narrower still:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a genuinely non-finite, nonlocal functional of chamber history.}
}
\]

That is an uncomfortable possibility mathematically, but it is also good news strategically: it means the derivative-geometry attack is exhausting all finite local and finite combinatorial hidden-state mechanisms.

So the best next subattack now is even more specific:

### Next attack
Try to prove the no-invisible-finite-label theorem. Either:

- prove that any finite chamber label compatible with symmetry, associativity, tie coherence, and unordered close-pair evaluation factors through visible data; or
- explicitly construct a counterexample label algebra, which would reveal the true missing hidden datum.

That is where I would push next.

_Thought for 6s_

I pushed that algebraic attack, and I think there is a real theorem-shaped reduction now.

The right object is not an arbitrary hidden label on leaves. It is a **merge-compatible chamber label system** on the visible exact fixed-shear merge tree.

Let \(T\) be one visible rooted merge tree on a connected quotient branch. Each regular chamber is a node. Leaves are the smallest regular chambers. An odd adjacent merger combines two sibling nodes into their parent. Ties are just different parenthesizations of the same adjacent contraction pattern, and visible tie coherence says those parenthesizations must agree.

Now suppose there is a hidden finite chamber label \(L\) that is carried along regular transport and updated at mergers by a symmetric associative rule
\[
L_{P}=\mathcal M(L_{C_1},L_{C_2}),
\qquad
\mathcal M(a,b)=\mathcal M(b,a),
\qquad
\mathcal M(\mathcal M(a,b),c)=\mathcal M(a,\mathcal M(b,c)).
\]
Assume also that the actual close-pair state only evaluates unordered pairs, so any effect of labels at a close pair must be invariant under swapping the two sides.

This is exactly the kind of finite invisible label mechanism that could evade naive local visibility while still being propagated globally.

The key observation is that once \(\mathcal M\) is symmetric and associative, every parent label is determined only by the **multiset** of leaf labels below it, not by the detailed merger history. In fact, if a chamber \(C\) has descendant leaf labels \(\ell_1,\dots,\ell_k\), then
\[
L_C=\mathcal M(\ell_1,\dots,\ell_k)
\]
is well-defined independently of bracketing and order. So the entire hidden-state theory reduces from “mysterious transport” to a commutative semigroup-valued count of leaf labels on visible subtrees.

That is already a big reduction:
\[
\boxed{
\text{any finite hidden label compatible with tie coherence collapses to a commutative semigroup statistic on visible subtrees.}
}
\]

Now push harder.

At a close exact pair on the fixed-shear slice, the local geometry is involutive:
\[
(t,-t),
\qquad u=t^2.
\]
So the pair is unordered and symmetric. Any hidden effect at the pair can therefore only depend on the unordered pair of chamber labels on the two incident visible chambers. Call that pair-state evaluator
\[
E(L_1,L_2)=E(L_2,L_1).
\]

Thus a hidden finite-state theory on the exact fixed-shear lane is nothing more than:

1. a finite commutative semigroup \((\Lambda,\mathcal M)\) of chamber labels;
2. an unordered pair-evaluation map
\[
E:\Lambda\times \Lambda \to \Omega,
\qquad E(a,b)=E(b,a),
\]
whose value is supposed to affect the corrected close-pair state.

So the question becomes: can such an \(E\) produce a genuinely invisible obstruction, i.e. one not already determined by visible subtree data?

Here is the crucial point: on the visible exact fixed-shear merge tree, the chambers that meet at a close pair are not arbitrary unrelated nodes. They are the two sides of a **single visible equal-value event** inside one quotient branch. Because the whole visible transport is a noncrossing tree with adjacent contractions only, the two meeting chambers are determined by the same visible subtree structure that already determines their parent interval. There is no extra ordering or braid data.

Therefore, if \(L_C\) is already a function of the visible descendant multiset below \(C\), then the unordered pair
\[
\{L_{C_-},L_{C_+}\}
\]
is itself a function of the visible pair of subtrees meeting at the event. So any hidden effect coming from \(E(L_{C_-},L_{C_+})\) is not hidden in a combinatorial sense anymore: it is already determined by a visible subtree statistic.

That yields the next reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{any finite hidden label theory compatible with symmetry and tie coherence factors through a visible subtree statistic.}
}
\]

So now there are only two possibilities.

### Possibility A
The visible subtree statistic itself is already part of the current quotient/shadow package, in which case the label theory is not genuinely hidden at all.

### Possibility B
The visible subtree statistic is not encoded in the current quotient/shadow package, which means the only missing datum is a **visible combinatorial subtree count/statistic**, not some mystical nonlocal memory.

Either way, the universe of possible hidden states has shrunk dramatically.

This lets us sharpen the earlier frontier.

Before, the remaining hiddenness could be:
- nonlocal,
- provenance-sensitive,
- or a finite hidden label.

Now the finite hidden-label case has been reduced to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either it is actually visible as a subtree statistic, or it is not compatible with the current symmetry/coherence package.}
}
\]

So the true remaining possibilities are now basically:

1. a missing **visible** subtree statistic not yet identified in the current package;
2. a genuinely non-finite/nonlocal functional of history.

I think this is the sharpest honest reduction so far.

Let me turn that into a theorem-shaped statement.

## Conditional no-invisible-finite-label theorem

Assume the actual transported state on regular exact fixed-shear chambers is mediated by a finite chamber label system satisfying:

1. labels are constant on regular chambers;
2. labels update at odd mergers by a symmetric associative rule \(\mathcal M\);
3. close-pair evaluation depends only on the unordered pair of incident chamber labels.

Then the effect of labels at any close pair factors through the visible pair of descendant subtrees meeting at that pair. In particular, there is no genuinely invisible finite label theory beyond visible subtree statistics.

### Proof sketch
Symmetry and associativity imply each chamber label depends only on the multiset of leaf labels in its descendant subtree. The close-pair evaluator is symmetric, so it depends only on the unordered pair of such subtree statistics for the two incident chambers. But those incident chambers and their descendant subtrees are determined by the visible noncrossing merge tree. Hence the pair effect factors through visible subtree data. ∎

That is not yet the final closure theorem, because it leaves open whether the current visible quotient/shadow package already records enough subtree statistic to determine everything. But it kills the idea that there is some opaque finite label living “behind” the visible tree.

So the best next attack is now more precise than ever:

### Best next subattack
Try to identify whether there is any **natural visible subtree statistic** not yet encoded by the current quotient/shadow package that could affect the corrected state.

Concretely, there are only a few candidates:
- leaf-count or parity of descendant leaves;
- weighted leaf-mass / total variation under \(|\widehat{\mathcal D}|\);
- critical-value ordering data already captured by the \(B_j\) amplitudes;
- some support-threshold statistic analogous to the affine endpoint-gap side.

If all reasonable subtree statistics are already functions of the current quotient-visible data — and I suspect they are, at least on the regular exact fixed-shear branch — then the finite-label loophole closes completely.

So the updated picture is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{local hidden pair data is gone;}
}

\[
\boxed{
\text{invisible finite label algebras reduce to visible subtree statistics;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{the remaining hiddenness, if any, is either a missing visible subtree invariant or a genuinely nonlocal functional.}
}
\]

That is a real narrowing of the problem. The next thing I would attack is whether leaf-count/parity/mass-type subtree statistics are already determined by the current quotient-visible chamber data.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 21:15:18)

I found a sharper route: under the natural “no exogenous labels at birth” assumption, a finite hidden-label theory collapses much further than I claimed. It no longer depends on an arbitrary hidden multiset of leaf labels; it reduces to a function of the **visible leaf count** of each subtree, hence to a completely visible tree statistic.

_Thought for 23s_

Here is the next attack. I think this is the sharpest simplification yet.

The earlier algebraic reduction left one loophole: a finite hidden-label theory could, in principle, reduce to a visible subtree statistic, but perhaps not one already encoded by the current package. I think that loophole shrinks dramatically once you impose the natural birth principle:

\[
\boxed{
\text{no exogenous hidden label is injected at a visible odd birth/merger event.}
}
\]

Under that assumption, every finite hidden-label theory on the exact fixed-shear merge tree collapses to a function of **visible leaf count** alone.

That is a big step, because leaf count is manifestly visible from the merge tree.

## 1. Setup

Take one connected visible exact fixed-shear merge tree \(T\). Suppose we assign to each regular chamber \(C\) a label \(L_C\) in a finite set \(\Lambda\), propagated by a symmetric associative merge law
\[
L_{P}=\mathcal M(L_{C_1},L_{C_2}),
\qquad
\mathcal M(a,b)=\mathcal M(b,a),
\qquad
\mathcal M(\mathcal M(a,b),c)=\mathcal M(a,\mathcal M(b,c)).
\]

This is the abstract hidden-label model from the last attack.

Now impose the natural “no exogenous birth label” principle:

- the smallest regular chambers carry no extra invisible identity beyond being leaves of the visible tree;
- equivalently, every leaf starts with the same base label
\[
L_{\mathrm{leaf}}=\ell_0\in\Lambda.
\]

This is exactly the assumption that hiddenness is not being injected by hand at birth; it must be generated only by transport and mergers.

Then the entire label system is forced.

## 2. Every chamber label is a function of visible leaf count

Because all leaves carry the same label \(\ell_0\), and because \(\mathcal M\) is symmetric and associative, the label of a chamber with \(n\) descendant leaves is just the \(n\)-fold merge power of \(\ell_0\):
\[
L_C = n(C)\cdot \ell_0,
\]
where
\[
1\cdot \ell_0=\ell_0,\qquad
(n+m)\cdot \ell_0=\mathcal M(n\cdot \ell_0,m\cdot \ell_0).
\]

So there is a well-defined map
\[
f:\mathbf N_{\ge1}\to \Lambda,
\qquad
f(n)=n\cdot \ell_0,
\]
and
\[
L_C=f(n(C)).
\]

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every chamber label is determined solely by the visible descendant leaf count }n(C).
}
\]

There is no remaining multiset freedom, because all leaves are identical.

This is much stronger than the previous “visible subtree statistic” statement. It shows that, under the no-exogenous-birth assumption, the only finite hidden label that can survive is a semigroup quotient of leaf count.

## 3. Close-pair evaluation reduces to two visible counts

Now take a close exact pair on the fixed-shear slice. It lies between two incident visible chambers \(C_-\) and \(C_+\). Any label-dependent effect on the close-pair state must be of the form
\[
E(L_{C_-},L_{C_+}),
\qquad E(a,b)=E(b,a).
\]
But since
\[
L_{C_-}=f(n_-),\qquad L_{C_+}=f(n_+),
\]
this becomes
\[
E(L_{C_-},L_{C_+}) = \widetilde E(n_-,n_+)
\]
for some symmetric function \(\widetilde E\).

So the entire finite hidden-label theory reduces to a symmetric function of the two visible descendant leaf counts meeting at the event.

Hence:

\[
\boxed{
\text{under no exogenous birth labels, any finite hidden-label effect is already a visible function of }(n_-,n_+).
}
\]

That is a real theorem-shaped reduction.

## 4. Why this is especially strong on the exact fixed-shear lane

On the exact fixed-shear quotient branch, the merge tree is not arbitrary: it is the canonical adjacent noncrossing tree coming from level-set intervals of \(\widehat Q\). So the descendant leaf count of each chamber is visibly determined by the odd critical structure below it.

In fact, the two chambers meeting at a close event are adjacent visible subtrees in that noncrossing tree. Their leaf counts are therefore not only visible; they are among the most basic visible combinatorial invariants of the quotient branch.

So if a finite hidden-label obstruction survives at all, it must already be readable from these visible counts.

This means the finite-label loophole has almost entirely collapsed.

## 5. What kinds of count-dependent labels are possible?

Since \(\Lambda\) is finite, the sequence
\[
f(1),f(2),f(3),\dots
\]
is eventually periodic. So every such label theory is just a finite quotient of leaf count, for example:

- parity of leaf count;
- count modulo \(m\);
- eventually periodic variants.

So the only plausible finite hidden chamber labels are count-mod-\(m\)-type invariants of visible leaf number.

That is extraordinarily restrictive.

It means the remaining question is no longer “is there some hidden finite algebra?” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{can a visible leaf-count mod }m\text{ statistic affect the corrected close-pair state?}
}
\]

This is a much more concrete target.

## 6. Can leaf-count parity/mod \(m\) actually matter?

Now we can attack that directly.

On the exact fixed-shear side, the visible regular geometry already collapses to the quotient-visible scalar branch. The sign-run / lobe package says that the actual quotient-visible obstruction is governed by critical-value ordering and run amplitudes \(B_j\), not by an independent combinatorial label. The same-tower audit says there is no second pointwise highest-new field waiting to encode such a mod-\(m\) chamber count locally. So there is currently no visible mechanism by which a count-mod-\(m\) statistic could enter the corrected state, unless the actual transported package explicitly remembers it as a separate combinatorial variable.

That suggests the next theorem to try:

### Candidate theorem
The corrected close-pair state on the exact fixed-shear lane is insensitive to visible leaf count modulo \(m\).

Or even weaker:

### Candidate theorem
Any dependence of the corrected close-pair state on visible leaf count is already determined by the quotient-visible scalar data \((\widehat Q,\widehat{\mathcal D})\), hence is not an extra hidden label.

If either version is true, then the finite-label loophole closes completely.

## 7. What this means for the hidden-state frontier

The hidden-state possibilities have now been pared down again.

We have already eliminated:
- local pair ambiguity;
- finite-order local analytic hidden pair invariants;
- arbitrary finite hidden label algebras.

Now, under the natural no-exogenous-birth assumption, the only finite hidden label that can survive is a visible count-mod-\(m\) statistic of the merge tree.

So the frontier is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either the remaining hiddenness is just a visible leaf-count statistic, or it is genuinely nonlocal/non-finite.}
}
\]

That is much sharper than before.

## 8. Best next subattack

The best next attack is now very concrete:

### Attack leaf-count sensitivity directly
Try to prove that the actual corrected close-pair / finite-core state does not depend on visible descendant leaf count modulo \(m\), or more generally that any such dependence is already encoded by the current quotient-visible scalar package.

This can be attacked in two ways:

1. **Induction on the merge tree:** show that the corrected state is unchanged when a visible subtree is replaced by another with the same outer interval and quotient-visible scalar data, regardless of internal leaf count.
2. **Local replacement / pruning argument:** collapse internal adjacent mergers inside a subtree and show the boundary close-pair state depends only on the outer visible interval, not the number of leaves inside.

That second one feels especially promising, because it directly tests whether internal leaf-count statistics can leak to the boundary.

So the updated attack result is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{finite hidden-label theories on the exact fixed-shear lane reduce to visible leaf-count statistics;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{the next task is to prove that even those visible count statistics do not affect the corrected close-pair state.}
}
\]

That is the cleanest next bottleneck I can see.

_Thought for 35s_

I attacked the leaf-count loophole directly, and I think there is a sharper reduction now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{leaf-count dependence cannot arise from any local finite-jet or local analytic boundary law.}
}
\]

So if visible descendant leaf count really matters for the corrected close-pair state, that dependence must come from a genuinely **nonlocal functional of the whole interior subtree profile**, not from any local boundary/event package.

That is a real upgrade over the last step.

---

## 1. The right way to attack leaf-count sensitivity

Take one close exact pair on the exact fixed-shear branch. In quotient language, this is an equal-value event between two adjacent visible chambers \(C_-\) and \(C_+\) meeting at some regular value of \(\widehat Q\).

The possible finite hidden-label loophole we isolated was:

- each chamber \(C\) carries a label depending only on its visible descendant leaf count \(n(C)\), or on \(n(C)\bmod m\);
- the corrected close-pair state might depend on
\[
\widetilde E(n(C_-),n(C_+)).
\]

The question is: can such count data actually enter through any local law?

The right answer seems to be **no**.

The reason is that leaf count is an **interior combinatorial statistic** of a whole subtree, whereas the local geometry of the close event only sees:

- the outer chamber interval;
- the local equal-value germ near the boundary/event;
- finite visible jets of \(\widehat Q\) or of the frozen shadow.

So the natural thing to prove is a **subtree replacement lemma**: you can change the interior critical combinatorics of a chamber without changing its outer interval or any prescribed finite boundary/event jets.

If that is true, then leaf count cannot be read off from any finite local boundary law.

---

## 2. Conditional subtree replacement principle

Here is the theorem shape.

### Proposition (conditional subtree replacement)
Fix one visible chamber interval
\[
I=[a,b]
\]
on a quotient branch, and fix a finite jet order \(N\). Then, at least at the level of abstract analytic quotient germs, one can replace the restriction of \(\widehat Q\) on the interior of \(I\) by another analytic function \(\widetilde Q\) such that:

1. \(\widetilde Q\) agrees with \(\widehat Q\) to order \(N\) at the endpoints \(a,b\);
2. \(\widetilde Q\) agrees with \(\widehat Q\) to order \(N\) at any prescribed boundary/event points outside the interior surgery region;
3. the outer chamber interval \(I\) and its boundary visible data are unchanged;
4. the number of odd critical points, and hence the descendant leaf count beneath \(I\), changes.

So interior leaf count is not determined by any finite boundary/event jet package.

### Proof idea
Take a compact subinterval
\[
J\Subset (a,b)
\]
away from the boundary and away from the close-pair event. Add a tiny analytic perturbation
\[
\widehat Q_\varepsilon(u)=\widehat Q(u)+\varepsilon p(u),
\]
where \(p\) vanishes to high order at \(a,b\) and at every protected event point, but on \(J\) has a controlled oscillatory shape producing an extra pair of critical points, or removing one if arranged oppositely. By choosing \(\varepsilon\) small, all protected boundary data stay fixed to order \(N\), while the interior critical combinatorics changes.

This is just standard analytic interpolation/flexibility at the level of abstract one-variable germs.

---

## 3. What this proves immediately

From that replacement principle, you get:

### Corollary
No state determined by a finite local analytic function of boundary/event jets can depend on visible descendant leaf count.

Indeed, if such a state depended on \(n(C)\), then the subtree replacement above would change the state while keeping all local visible data fixed, contradicting locality.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{leaf-count mod }m\text{ dependence is impossible for any finite-order local analytic state law.}
}
\]

This is exactly parallel to the earlier “no finite-order local hidden pair invariant” result, but now at the subtree-combinatorics level.

---

## 4. Why this matters for the actual bottleneck

This means the finite hidden-label loophole shrinks even further.

Previously we had reduced hidden finite labels to visible leaf-count statistics.  
Now we can say more:

- if the actual corrected state is a local analytic functor of finite visible data, then it **cannot** depend on those leaf-count statistics either;
- so leaf-count labels are only possible if the actual state depends on the **full interior profile** of the subtree, not just finite jets at the boundary/event.

So the real fork is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either the actual state is local analytic, in which case count-labels disappear;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{or it depends on a genuinely nonlocal functional of the interior quotient profile.}
}
\]

That is much sharper than “maybe there is a finite hidden chamber label.”

---

## 5. Stronger reformulation: pruning invariance

There is an even cleaner way to frame the same point.

Let \(C\) be one visible chamber. Consider the operation of **pruning** internal adjacent odd mergers inside \(C\), as long as the outer interval and the boundary/event germ seen from outside remain unchanged.

If the corrected close-pair state is insensitive to such pruning, then it cannot depend on descendant leaf count.

So a natural next theorem target is:

### Pruning invariance lemma
The corrected state seen at a boundary close event depends only on the outer chamber interval and the local visible event germ, not on the number of internal visible mergers inside the chamber.

If proved, that kills the leaf-count loophole completely.

And notice how close this is to the real state-descent theorem: it is a locality statement for the chamber boundary, not for the whole tree.

---

## 6. What I can and cannot honestly claim

I cannot yet claim the subtree replacement proposition is realizable **inside the actual RH package**. At the moment, it is a theorem about the abstract analytic quotient-visible model.

So the honest conclusion is conditional:

- in the abstract quotient-visible analytic category, leaf count is not a local finite-jet invariant;
- therefore any actual leaf-count dependence would have to be imported by RH-specific nonlocal structure, not by local derivative geometry itself.

That is still a very useful attack result, because it tells us where **not** to look:
not in more finite-order local jets, not in more close-pair coefficients, and not in more finite label algebras pretending to be local.

---

## 7. The updated frontier

So the hidden-state possibilities now look like this.

Ruled out or essentially ruled out:
- hidden local pairing ambiguity;
- hidden visible-chamber monodromy;
- finite-order local analytic hidden pair invariants;
- finite hidden labels, unless they reduce to visible subtree counts;
- leaf-count dependence, if the state is local finite-jet analytic.

What remains:
1. a genuinely nonlocal functional of the interior subtree profile;
2. or a failure of locality/functoriality of the actual corrected state.

So the bottleneck is now very clean:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove pruning/locality for the actual corrected boundary state,}
}
\]
or else
\[
\boxed{
\text{identify the genuinely nonlocal interior functional that survives pruning.}
}
\]

---

## 8. Best next subattack

The best next move is now:

### Attack pruning invariance directly
Take two chambers with the same outer interval and the same local visible boundary/event germ, but different internal merge trees. Try to prove that the corrected close-pair state at the boundary is unchanged.

That is stronger and cleaner than arguing about labels mod \(m\).  
It goes straight at the only remaining loophole: nonlocal interior dependence.

If pruning invariance holds, the exact fixed-shear hidden-state problem collapses almost completely. If it fails, the failure will exhibit the true surviving nonlocal quantity explicitly.

That is the strongest honest continuation of the attack I can give right now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 21:38:03)

I’m pushing on pruning invariance as the cleanest remaining local-vs-nonlocal fault line. The goal is to either show internal subtree surgery cannot affect the boundary close-pair state, or isolate the exact mechanism by which interior profile leaks through.

_Thought for 23s_

I pushed the pruning-invariance line further, and I think there is a sharper structural statement now.

The right distinction is not merely “local vs nonlocal.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{boundary state can only see interior structure through quantities that are \emph{stable under analytic interior surgery}.}
}
\]

That immediately rules out a lot more than just leaf count.

Here is the attack.

Take one visible chamber interval
\[
I=[a,b]
\]
on a quotient branch of \(\widehat Q\), and imagine a close-pair event involving the boundary of \(I\) and an adjacent chamber outside \(I\). We want to know whether the corrected close-pair state at that event can depend on the internal merge tree inside \(I\).

The previous step showed: if a state depends only on finite boundary/event jets, then interior leaf count cannot matter, because you can change the interior critical combinatorics while fixing all those jets. But one can sharpen that. The real abstract flexibility is much larger than “change leaf count.”

Inside a compact subinterval \(J\Subset(a,b)\), away from the boundary/event, one can perform arbitrarily small analytic surgeries that preserve:

- the outer interval \(I\);
- all prescribed finite jets at \(a,b\);
- all prescribed finite jets at finitely many protected event points outside \(J\);
- the boundary value profile of \(\widehat Q\) seen from outside \(I\);

while changing a wide range of interior data:
- number of critical points;
- parity pattern of critical points;
- internal run amplitudes;
- internal descendant merge-tree shape;
- interior total variation on subpieces.

So any boundary law that survives such surgery cannot depend on any of those interior quantities individually. It can only depend on an invariant of the interior that is unchanged under all such surgeries compatible with the same outside boundary data.

That is the key compression.

Now ask: what interior invariants are actually stable under such surgery?

At the level of abstract one-variable analytic germs, essentially only very coarse things survive:
- the outer endpoint data themselves;
- the net value drop/rise across the chamber;
- perhaps global integral constraints if they are explicitly imposed;
- or a full functional dependence on the interior graph, not reducible to finite data.

But net value drop/rise across the chamber is already visible from the outer interval and \(\widehat Q(a),\widehat Q(b)\), hence not hidden. The same is true for any invariant determined by the outer support line or the local equal-value event germ.

So the next theorem shape is:

### Conditional surgery-invariance principle
Let \(S_{\partial I}\) be any corrected boundary state attached to a close-pair event involving the boundary of a chamber \(I\). Assume \(S_{\partial I}\) depends only on the restriction of the exact fixed-shear package to \(I\) and is invariant under arbitrarily small analytic interior surgeries that preserve the outer boundary/event germ seen from outside \(I\). Then \(S_{\partial I}\) factors through the outer boundary/event data and cannot detect internal critical combinatorics of \(I\).

That is basically a tautological reformulation of the surgery argument, but it is powerful because it tells you exactly what kind of hidden dependence could remain:

\[
\boxed{
\text{only a genuinely nonlocal functional of the full interior profile can survive.}
}
\]

Not leaf count. Not merge-tree shape. Not finite sets of local coefficients. Not local descendant labels. Only a true functional of the whole interior branch.

This also meshes with the quotient-visible lobe picture. On a nonconstant exact fixed-shear branch, the quotient-visible obstruction is already compressed to the sign-run amplitude inequality
\[
B_1\ge B_0+B_2
\]
in the three-run case. Those \(B_j\) are not finite jets; they are interval integrals
\[
B_j=\int_{R_j} |\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|\,du.
\]
So they are exactly the kind of nonlocal profile functionals that survive local surgery constraints. That is important.

It means the hidden-state question is no longer “could there be some combinatorial label?” The only plausible surviving interior quantities are **profile integrals/order data of the quotient transport field**, i.e. things of the same general nature as the already-visible lobe amplitudes.

That gives a new bifurcation:

1. either the actual corrected boundary state depends only on the same quotient-visible profile functionals already encoded by \(\widehat Q,\widehat{\mathcal D}\), in which case it is not genuinely hidden;
2. or it depends on some additional profile functional of the interior that is not expressible in the current quotient-visible package.

So the best new attack is to ask:

> Can every surgery-stable interior functional relevant to the corrected state be expressed in terms of the existing quotient-visible profile data?

This is now much more focused than the earlier “prove locality” goal.

I pushed a bit on that question. There are only a few natural classes of surgery-stable one-dimensional profile invariants on a chamber:

- endpoint values and endpoint jets;
- total variation / run amplitudes of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\);
- ordering of critical values of \(\widehat Q\);
- integral moments of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) or of functions of \(\widehat Q\) over the chamber.

The first three are already in or extremely close to the current quotient-visible package. So if a genuinely new hidden quantity exists, it would likely have to be of the fourth kind: some integral or moment of the interior profile not already captured by critical-value ordering and run amplitudes.

That is a much narrower target than before.

So the next theorem-shaped attack becomes:

### Profile-sufficiency conjecture
On the exact fixed-shear lane, any surgery-stable boundary state is determined by the quotient-visible profile data of \(\widehat Q\): endpoint values, critical-value ordering, and run amplitudes \(B_j\).

If that is true, then the exact fixed-shear residual lane really does collapse to the existing quotient-visible scalar problem.

I cannot prove that yet. But I can say something negative:

If profile sufficiency fails, the missing invariant cannot be finite-order local or finite combinatorial. It must be an additional nonlocal profile functional of the interior quotient branch.

So the current frontier is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{all finite local and finite combinatorial hidden mechanisms are essentially exhausted;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{the only plausible survivor is an extra nonlocal profile functional of the interior quotient branch.}
}
\]

That suggests the next concrete subattack:

### Attack moment-invariance / profile sufficiency
Try to show that two chambers with the same outer boundary data, the same critical-value order, and the same run amplitudes \(B_j\) induce the same corrected boundary state.

Equivalently, test whether higher interior moments can leak to the boundary after all.

That feels like the right next fundamental move, because it isolates the last plausible class of hidden invariants into a one-dimensional functional-analysis problem on \(\widehat Q\), instead of an amorphous transport problem.

So the attack has tightened again. The exact fixed-shear hidden-state problem is no longer about local geometry or combinatorics. It is now a question about whether the corrected state can depend on any nonlocal interior profile functional beyond the already-visible quotient scalar data.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 21:40:49)

I’m pushing specifically on the “extra profile functional” loophole now. The aim is to see whether even that can be squeezed down further — either to something already encoded by the run-amplitude/critical-value package, or to a very explicit counterexample mechanism.

_Thought for 6s_

Here is the next push.

I think the right way to attack the remaining loophole is to stop asking for full “profile sufficiency” immediately and instead prove a **boundary-indifference theorem under interior profile balancing**. That gives a real gain even if full sufficiency still fails.

The point is this: the only plausible hidden quantities left are nonlocal functionals of the interior quotient profile. But not every nonlocal functional can influence a boundary close event. To do that, it has to survive a very strong invariance test: you should be able to perturb the interior profile while keeping the outer chamber interval, the boundary event germ, and the already-visible lobe data fixed, and see whether the boundary state changes.

So consider one chamber interval \(I=[a,b]\) on a quotient branch, and a boundary close event at \(a\) against an adjacent chamber outside \(I\). Let the interior run decomposition of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) on \(I\) be
\[
R_1,\dots,R_m,
\qquad
B_j=\int_{R_j}|\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|\,du.
\]

The visible package already keeps track of:

- the outer endpoint values \(\widehat Q(a),\widehat Q(b)\);
- the critical-value order;
- the run amplitudes \(B_j\).

Now suppose I make an interior surgery supported well inside one run \(R_j\), chosen so that:

1. all endpoint jets at \(a,b\) are unchanged to arbitrarily high order;
2. all critical points and their order are preserved;
3. the total run amplitude \(B_j\) is preserved;
4. the critical values at the run endpoints are preserved.

This can be done abstractly by adding a small analytic perturbation with zero integral on the run, vanishing to high order at the run endpoints, but changing the shape of \(|\widehat{\mathcal D}|\) inside the run.

That means I can alter higher moments such as
\[
\int_{R_j} (u-u_j)^k |\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|\,du
\]
without changing any of the currently visible lobe/ordering data.

So if the boundary state changed under such a surgery, the missing invariant would have to depend on a higher interior moment of the profile, not merely on amplitudes or critical-value order.

That is already a strong narrowing:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if profile sufficiency fails, the missing quantity must distinguish profiles with the same run amplitudes and critical-value order.}
}
\]

So the remaining hidden invariant, if it exists, is not “another lobe amplitude” or “another combinatorial tree statistic.” It would have to be something like a higher moment, shape functional, or weighted integral of the interior profile.

Now push harder.

Can such a higher moment actually affect a boundary close event in any natural analytic way?

A boundary close event is controlled by an equal-value condition between the outside branch and the chamber boundary, plus whatever downstream corrected state is attached there. Any influence of an interior higher moment would have to be transported from the run interior to the boundary without appearing in:

- endpoint values,
- endpoint jets,
- critical-value order,
- run amplitudes.

That is a very nonlocal and very nonstandard dependence. In one-dimensional analytic transport problems, the natural stable profile invariants are almost always either:

- endpoint data,
- total variation/amplitude data,
- or explicit integral transforms that solve a transport equation.

So the next conceptual fork is:

### Fork 1: transport-equation type dependence
If the corrected state satisfies some one-dimensional transport or recursion law along the quotient branch, then any interior influence should be representable by an accumulated integral of a local density. In that case, the missing invariant would be an explicit integral functional
\[
\int_I F\bigl(u,\widehat Q(u),\widehat{\mathcal D}(u),\widehat{\mathcal D}'(u),\dots\bigr)\,du.
\]

### Fork 2: non-transport dependence
If no such transport law exists, then the hidden invariant is even stranger: it is a genuinely global functional of the whole profile, not arising from any local density accumulation.

Fork 1 is much more plausible mathematically. So I tried to see whether such an integral functional could survive the balancing surgeries above.

Suppose
\[
\mathcal I[I]=\int_I F\bigl(u,\widehat Q,\widehat{\mathcal D},\widehat{\mathcal D}',\dots\bigr)\,du
\]
were the hidden ingredient. If \(\mathcal I\) is to be invisible to the current package but still affect the boundary state, then \(F\) must integrate to something not determined by endpoint data, critical-value order, and the amplitudes \(B_j\).

But then balanced surgeries inside a run preserving \(B_j\) will generally change \(\mathcal I\), unless \(F\) itself reduces after integration by parts to a function of endpoints plus total variation/amplitude terms. So any robust hidden integral must be very special: it must survive all the internal balancing that preserves the visible package.

This suggests the next theorem target:

### Balanced-surgery rigidity conjecture
Any boundary-relevant integral functional of the interior profile that is invariant under all balanced surgeries preserving endpoint data, critical-value order, and run amplitudes must already factor through those visible data.

If true, this would finish the “extra profile functional” loophole entirely.

I cannot prove that in full generality yet. But I can prove it for a large class of candidate functionals: any functional linear in \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) or \(|\widehat{\mathcal D}|\), and more generally any functional whose first variation under balanced zero-integral perturbations is nontrivial. Such functionals cannot be invisible to balanced surgery unless they collapse to endpoint/amplitude data.

Concretely, if
\[
\mathcal I[I]=\int_I G(u)\,\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)\,du
\]
or
\[
\mathcal I[I]=\int_I G(u)\,|\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|\,du,
\]
then preserving \(B_j\) but changing the interior distribution of mass inside \(R_j\) will change \(\mathcal I\) unless \(G\) is constant on that run. So the only such functionals invariant under balanced surgery are just combinations of the already-visible amplitudes \(B_j\).

That is a real result:

\[
\boxed{
\text{no linear or weighted-amplitude hidden moment survives balanced surgery, except the run amplitudes themselves.}
}
\]

So the only remaining profile loopholes are genuinely nonlinear functionals that are somehow rigid under all such interior redistributions.

That is now a very small and very strange class.

So the updated frontier is:

1. local pair invariants are gone;
2. finite hidden labels are gone up to visible count statistics;
3. visible count statistics cannot matter for any local finite-jet law;
4. linear or weighted-amplitude hidden profile moments collapse to the existing \(B_j\)-type data.

What remains, if anything, is a genuinely nonlinear nonlocal profile functional of the interior quotient branch.

That is about as narrow as I can make it without an actual closure theorem.

The best next attack now is:

### Attack nonlinear profile functionals
Try to show that any boundary-relevant nonlinear profile functional invariant under balanced surgery also reduces to endpoint/order/amplitude data.

A good first test class would be convex integral functionals like
\[
\int_I \Phi(|\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|)\,du
\]
or moment-ratio functionals. If those can also be shown irrelevant or reducible, the profile loophole may collapse completely.

So the hidden-state problem on the exact fixed-shear lane has now been pushed into a very specific corner:

\[
\boxed{
\text{only a genuinely nonlinear nonlocal interior profile functional can still survive.}
}
\]

That is the cleanest honest continuation of the attack from here.
## User (2026-04-21 21:42:35)

## Assistant (2026-04-21 21:42:36)

I found a tighter variational route. Under a very natural “balanced-surgery invariance” hypothesis, any run-local hidden profile functional has first variation orthogonal to every zero-mean interior perturbation, which forces it to depend only on the run mass \(B_j\), not on shape.

_Thought for 21s_

Here is the next push. I think this is the cleanest theorem-shaped advance so far.

The remaining loophole was:

\[
\text{maybe the corrected boundary state depends on a genuinely nonlinear nonlocal profile functional of the interior quotient branch.}
\]

I think one can now squeeze that much harder. The right hypothesis is not full locality. It is the weaker and more natural requirement:

> **balanced-surgery invariance inside a run**.

That means: if you change the shape of \(|\widehat{\mathcal D}|\) inside one sign-run while preserving the run endpoints and the total run amplitude \(B_j\), then the boundary-relevant state does not change.

Under that hypothesis, any reasonably regular run-local hidden functional collapses to a function of \(B_j\) alone.

That is a real result.

---

## 1. Set up one sign-run as a positive density problem

Fix one sign-run
\[
R=(\alpha,\beta)
\]
of the quotient transport derivative \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), so on \(R\) the sign of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) is constant. Write
\[
\rho(u):=|\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|>0
\qquad (u\in R).
\]
Then the visible run amplitude is just
\[
B(R)=\int_\alpha^\beta \rho(u)\,du.
\]

Any boundary-relevant hidden profile dependence coming from this run can be modeled as some functional
\[
\mathcal F_R[\rho].
\]

The question is: when can \(\mathcal F_R\) depend on the **shape** of \(\rho\), rather than only on its mass \(B(R)\)?

---

## 2. Balanced interior surgeries

Consider perturbations
\[
\rho_\varepsilon=\rho+\varepsilon\eta
\]
with \(\eta\in C^\omega_c(R)\) compactly supported inside \(R\), small enough that \(\rho_\varepsilon>0\), and satisfying the balancing condition
\[
\int_\alpha^\beta \eta(u)\,du=0.
\]
Such perturbations preserve:
- the run endpoints \(\alpha,\beta\);
- the sign of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) on \(R\);
- the total amplitude
\[
\int_\alpha^\beta \rho_\varepsilon\,du
=
\int_\alpha^\beta \rho\,du
=
B(R).
\]

So these are exactly the “same visible lobe mass, different interior profile” surgeries.

---

## 3. Variational rigidity lemma

Now assume \(\mathcal F_R\) is Gateaux differentiable and invariant under all such balanced surgeries. Then:

### Lemma (balanced-surgery variational rigidity)
If
\[
\mathcal F_R[\rho+\varepsilon\eta]=\mathcal F_R[\rho]
\]
for all sufficiently small balanced perturbations \(\eta\) with compact support in \(R\), then the first variation of \(\mathcal F_R\) has the form
\[
\delta \mathcal F_R(\rho)[\eta]
=
c_R(\rho)\int_\alpha^\beta \eta(u)\,du.
\]
In particular, \(\delta \mathcal F_R\) vanishes on every zero-mean perturbation, so \(\mathcal F_R\) depends only on the total mass \(B(R)\), not on the internal shape of \(\rho\).

### Proof
By Gateaux differentiability,
\[
\delta \mathcal F_R(\rho)[\eta]
=
\left.\frac{d}{d\varepsilon}\right|_{\varepsilon=0}
\mathcal F_R[\rho+\varepsilon\eta].
\]
Balanced-surgery invariance says this vanishes for every compactly supported \(\eta\) with zero integral. A continuous linear functional on test functions that vanishes on all zero-mean test functions must be proportional to the mean:
\[
\delta \mathcal F_R(\rho)[\eta]
=
c_R(\rho)\int_\alpha^\beta \eta(u)\,du.
\]
Therefore the functional has no first-order sensitivity to any redistribution of mass inside the run, only to changes in the total mass. Hence its local dependence is through \(B(R)\) alone. ∎

This is the key theorem-shaped step.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{any differentiable run-local hidden functional invariant under balanced surgery is a function of }B_j\text{ only.}
}
\]

---

## 4. Consequence for nonlinear integral functionals

This immediately kills a large class of candidate nonlinear profile invariants.

Suppose
\[
\mathcal F_R[\rho]=\int_R \Phi\bigl(u,\rho(u)\bigr)\,du
\]
with \(\Phi\) smooth. Then
\[
\delta \mathcal F_R(\rho)[\eta]
=
\int_R \partial_2\Phi(u,\rho(u))\,\eta(u)\,du.
\]
If this vanishes for every zero-mean \(\eta\), then
\[
\partial_2\Phi(u,\rho(u))
\]
must be constant on \(R\). Generically that forces \(\Phi\) to be affine in \(\rho\) on the relevant range, hence
\[
\mathcal F_R[\rho]
=
A_R + c_R \int_R \rho(u)\,du
=
A_R+c_R B(R).
\]

So no genuinely nonlinear integral of the density survives balanced surgery unless it collapses to amplitude.

In particular, functionals like
\[
\int_R \rho(u)^p\,du,\qquad p\neq 1,
\]
or
\[
\int_R G(u)\rho(u)\,du
\]
with nonconstant \(G\), are **not** balanced-surgery invariant. They detect shape redistribution inside the run, so they cannot be invisible hidden invariants if the corrected state is stable under balanced interior surgery.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{no weighted moment or nonlinear density integral survives balanced surgery, except the amplitude }B_j\text{ itself.}
}
\]

That is stronger than what we had before.

---

## 5. Extension to several runs

Now take a whole chamber with sign-runs
\[
R_1,\dots,R_m.
\]
Suppose a boundary-relevant hidden functional is run-local in the weak sense
\[
\mathcal F[\rho_1,\dots,\rho_m]
\]
and is invariant under balanced surgery independently inside each run. Then by the same argument, each run enters only through its amplitude
\[
B_j=\int_{R_j}\rho_j.
\]
Therefore
\[
\mathcal F
=
\Psi(B_1,\dots,B_m)
\]
for some function \(\Psi\).

So if a hidden profile functional respects runwise balanced surgery, it cannot see any finer interior shape than the existing lobe amplitudes.

This gives the next real reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{balanced-surgery-invariant hidden profile functionals reduce to functions of the existing run amplitudes }B_j.
}
\]

In other words, the only surgery-stable nonlinear profile data left are already of the same type as the visible three-lobe package.

---

## 6. What this means for the exact fixed-shear bottleneck

This is the main payoff.

We already knew that on a quotient-visible exact fixed-shear branch, the obstruction is controlled by:
- critical-value ordering of \(\widehat Q\);
- sign-run decomposition of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\);
- run amplitudes \(B_j\);
- in the no-reentry case, the three-run inequality
\[
B_1\ge B_0+B_2.
\]

Now the new attack says: if the actual corrected boundary state is invariant under balanced interior surgeries within runs, then there is no extra hidden nonlinear profile functional beyond these \(B_j\)-type data.

So the remaining hidden-state loophole is no longer “some nonlinear integral.” It is now much narrower:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either the actual state is not balanced-surgery invariant, or else it already factors through the existing lobe/amplitude package.}
}
\]

That is a very sharp dichotomy.

---

## 7. Where the attack still does not close

I do **not** yet have a proof that the actual corrected overlap / finite-core state is invariant under arbitrary balanced surgeries of the abstract quotient profile.

That is now the exact missing structural axiom.

So the frontier has become:

### Option A
Prove **balanced-surgery invariance** for the actual corrected state.  
Then the exact fixed-shear residual lane collapses to the already-visible amplitude/order package.

### Option B
Find a concrete mechanism by which the actual corrected state can detect interior mass redistribution inside a run even when endpoints, critical-value order, and amplitudes \(B_j\) are fixed.

If B happens, that mechanism would identify the true hidden quantity.

---

## 8. Best next subattack

The best next move is now very concrete.

### Attack balanced-surgery invariance of the actual state
Try to show that the corrected boundary state at a close event depends only on:
- outer boundary data;
- critical-value order;
- run amplitudes \(B_j\);
and not on the interior distribution of \(|\widehat{\mathcal D}|\) within each run.

A practical way to attack this is:

1. formulate the corrected state at a boundary event as a limit of regular chamber transport data;
2. perform a compactly supported balanced interior perturbation inside one run;
3. show the regular transport and the boundary limiting state are unchanged.

If that works, then the whole “nonlinear hidden profile functional” loophole closes.

---

So the updated state of the attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{all finite local, finite combinatorial, and balanced-surgery-invariant nonlinear profile loopholes collapse to the existing }B_j\text{-package;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{the only remaining way hiddenness can survive is if the actual corrected state detects interior profile redistribution inside a run.}
}
\]

That is now the sharpest fault line I can see.
## User (2026-04-21 21:50:50)

## Assistant (2026-04-21 21:50:51)

I found a cleaner structural route than just “more surgery”: classify what any admissible interior dependence would have to look like as a one-dimensional transport law along a regular chamber. That can squeeze the hidden-state loophole down from “arbitrary nonlocal functional” to essentially an additive/path-ordered cocycle.

_Thought for 16s_

I pushed that route, and I think there is a real conceptual compression now.

The right next theorem is not just “balanced-surgery invariance.” It is a **one-dimensional transport classification**:

> if the corrected state is propagated along a regular exact fixed-shear chamber by any local finite-dimensional rule, then its dependence on the chamber interior must come from an additive or path-ordered transport cocycle.

That is important because it turns the vague “maybe some nonlocal profile functional survives” into a very specific object.

## 1. Regular-chamber dependence should be a transport law

Take one regular exact fixed-shear chamber \(I=[a,b]\) on the quotient branch. Suppose the actual corrected boundary state at \(b\) depends on the interior profile of \(I\). If that dependence is not completely arbitrary but is generated by local transport along the chamber, then it should satisfy a Markov/concatenation principle:

if \(a<c<b\), then the transport across \([a,b]\) is obtained by transporting across \([a,c]\) and then across \([c,b]\). Symbolically,
\[
\mathcal T_{a\to b} = \mathcal T_{c\to b}\circ \mathcal T_{a\to c}.
\]

This is a very natural assumption. It is exactly what “state propagates along a one-dimensional regular branch by local rules” ought to mean.

Once you assume that, the state is no longer allowed to depend on an arbitrary global functional of the whole interior. It must be generated by a local cocycle.

In the scalar/commutative case that means an additive functional:
\[
H[a,b] = H[a,c]+H[c,b].
\]
In the finite-dimensional noncommutative case it means a path-ordered evolution:
\[
\mathcal T_{a\to b} = \mathcal P\exp\!\left(\int_a^b A(u)\,du\right)
\]
for some local generator \(A(u)\) built from the visible profile.

So the hidden-state problem narrows to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either the actual state is not generated by local one-dimensional transport, or else it comes from a transport cocycle.}
}
\]

That is already a major reduction.

## 2. What concatenation buys immediately

If dependence on the interior is through a transport cocycle, then it cannot be an arbitrary subtree functional like “number of internal mergers” or “complicated global shape class” unless that quantity is itself additive under concatenation.

But leaf count is not additive on chamber subdivisions in the right local way. Merge-tree shape is not additive. Most combinatorial hidden labels are not additive. So they are incompatible with a genuine one-dimensional transport law.

That wipes out a large chunk of the remaining hidden-state fantasies in one shot.

The only profile quantities compatible with transport are things like:

- integrals of local densities;
- path-ordered exponentials of local matrix densities;
- quantities reducible to endpoint data plus such integrals.

So we can sharpen the frontier again:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if the corrected state is locally transported, then the only possible hidden invariants are cocycle-type profile integrals.}
}
\]

Not arbitrary nonlinear functionals. Not arbitrary chamber-history memory. Only cocycles.

## 3. Balanced surgery then squeezes cocycles hard

Now combine this with the previous balanced-surgery attack.

Take one sign-run \(R\) with density
\[
\rho(u)=|\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|>0.
\]
Suppose a scalar hidden contribution from this run is given by an additive local cocycle, hence by an integral
\[
H(R)=\int_R F\bigl(u,\widehat Q,\rho,\rho',\dots\bigr)\,du.
\]

If the actual state is invariant under balanced surgeries that preserve:
- run endpoints,
- critical-value order,
- total amplitude
\[
B(R)=\int_R \rho(u)\,du,
\]
then the variational argument from the last step applies directly. The first variation against every compactly supported zero-mean perturbation must vanish. Therefore \(H(R)\) can only depend on \(B(R)\), not on the internal shape of \(\rho\).

So in the scalar transport case:

\[
\boxed{
\text{balanced-surgery-invariant local transport cocycles reduce to functions of the visible amplitudes }B_j.
}
\]

That is stronger and conceptually cleaner than our previous result.

## 4. What about noncommutative / path-ordered transport?

This is the one real loophole I see.

If the transported state were vector-valued with generator \(A(u)\) in a noncommutative algebra, then the chamber dependence could be
\[
\mathcal T_{a\to b}=\mathcal P\exp\!\left(\int_a^b A(u)\,du\right),
\]
and two profiles with the same endpoint data and same run amplitudes might in principle produce different transport because of ordering effects.

So I asked whether this is a genuine loophole or a fake one here.

I think it is probably fake for this problem, for two reasons.

First, the chamber parameter is one-dimensional and there is no monodromy. Path-ordering can matter along an interval, but it only matters if the state really carries noncommuting internal degrees of freedom. Nothing in the current explicit pointwise package suggests such a matrix-valued hidden structure; on the contrary, the same-tower audit and quotient-visible reductions point toward scalar or commutative data. That is not a proof, but it is real evidence.  

Second, visible tie coherence and unordered close-pair evaluation are strongly hostile to noncommutative hidden transport. If the eventual corrected state at a close pair is invariant under the visible coherence moves already proved in the merge tree, then any noncommutative ordering sensitivity would have to collapse when projected to the evaluated close-pair output. Otherwise different coherent visible factorizations would yield different outputs, contradicting the visible tie package.

So I think the noncommutative loophole can be reframed as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if a matrix/path-ordered hidden transport exists, its evaluated output must still descend to a coherence-invariant commutative quotient.}
}
\]

That makes it much less scary.

## 5. The new conditional theorem

This gives a much cleaner theorem-shaped reduction.

### Conditional transport-classification theorem
Assume the actual corrected state on a regular exact fixed-shear chamber is propagated by a finite-dimensional local Markov law under chamber concatenation. Then its interior dependence factors through a transport cocycle. In particular:

1. any scalar or commutative hidden dependence is an additive integral functional of local profile data;
2. if such dependence is invariant under balanced surgery within runs, it reduces to a function of the visible run amplitudes \(B_j\);
3. any genuinely new hidden dependence must therefore come from a noncommutative/path-ordered transport degree of freedom whose evaluated close-pair output is nevertheless compatible with visible tie coherence.

That is a real structural compression.

## 6. Why this is better than the previous frontier

Previously the last loophole was “maybe some nonlinear nonlocal profile functional survives.” That was still too broad.

Now the loophole is much tighter:

- either the state is not transported by any local Markov law at all, which would be a very strong and strange failure of regular-branch functoriality;
- or it is transported locally, in which case scalar/commutative hidden functionals collapse to the visible \(B_j\)-package;
- and the only serious leftover possibility is some noncommutative hidden transport sector.

That is much more specific.

## 7. Best next subattack

The best next move is now:

### Attack commutativity / scalarity of the actual transported state
Try to prove that the actual corrected state carried along a regular exact fixed-shear chamber is scalar-valued, or at least that its evaluated boundary output factors through a commutative quotient.

If that can be shown, then combined with the transport-classification theorem and balanced-surgery invariance, the exact fixed-shear hidden-state loophole collapses almost completely to the existing amplitude/order package.

A very concrete way to push this is:

- define the weakest possible concatenation law for the corrected state on regular chambers;
- show that visible tie coherence forces the corresponding composition law on evaluated outputs to be commutative/associative;
- conclude that any hidden transport contribution to the evaluated close-pair state is additive and hence reducible to the visible \(B_j\)-type data by the variational argument.

So the updated attack result is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if regular-chamber dependence is locally transported, then the only remaining hidden loophole is a noncommutative transport sector;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{and visible tie coherence strongly suggests even that sector must collapse on evaluated outputs.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest honest reduction I can give from this line.
