## Assistant (2026-04-21 21:52:27)

I found a stronger route than just “maybe the transport is commutative.” Visible tie coherence plus unordered close-pair evaluation seem to force the **evaluated** transport algebra to collapse to a commutative monoid, even if an internal noncommutative transport existed upstream. That would largely kill the last serious loophole.

_Thought for 10s_

Here is the next push.

I think the last loophole can be squeezed further by separating two levels:

- the **internal** transport law along a regular chamber;
- the **evaluated output** that actually matters at a close pair.

Even if the internal transport were matrix-valued or path-ordered, the close-pair output is not arbitrary. It is subject to two very strong constraints:

1. **unordered evaluation** at the exact pair \((t,-t)\);
2. **visible tie coherence** of the adjacent merge tree.

Those two constraints look strong enough to force the **evaluated** transport algebra to become commutative, even if the internal transport is not.

That is the new attack.

---

## 1. Abstract the remaining loophole

Suppose on each regular chamber \(I\) there is some transport operator
\[
T(I)
\]
taking incoming hidden state to outgoing hidden state.

If transport is local and concatenative, then for adjacent subintervals
\[
I=[a,c],\qquad J=[c,b]
\]
we have
\[
T(I\cup J)=T(J)\,T(I).
\]
This is the usual one-dimensional transport law. It may be noncommutative at the operator level.

Now the close-pair state is not the operator \(T(I)\) itself. What matters is some evaluated quantity
\[
\mathcal E(T(I))
\]
or, more generally, a symmetric evaluation on the two incident chamber transports
\[
\mathcal E(T_-,T_+)=\mathcal E(T_+,T_-).
\]

The hidden loophole is: perhaps \(T\) lives in a noncommutative algebra, and \(\mathcal E\) detects something beyond the visible amplitude/order package.

The question is whether tie coherence permits that.

---

## 2. Tie coherence forces a coherence law on evaluated outputs

Take three adjacent child chambers \(A,B,C\) inside one visible subtree. There are two coherent visible ways to merge them to the same parent:

\[
((A\star B)\star C)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
(A\star(B\star C)).
\]

At the visible level these are already identified by the quotient merge-tree/tie package.

Therefore the evaluated output seen from outside must agree for both bracketings.

If we write the evaluated binary merge law schematically as
\[
X\odot Y
\]
for the effect of combining two adjacent visible chamber states on the eventual boundary output, then tie coherence forces
\[
(X\odot Y)\odot Z = X\odot (Y\odot Z).
\]

So the evaluated law is associative.

But there is more. Because the exact pair is unordered and the local involution \(t\leftrightarrow -t\) is shadow-neutral, the two incident sides at a close event are not ordered. So the evaluated law must also satisfy
\[
X\odot Y = Y\odot X.
\]

Thus the evaluated output algebra is not merely associative; it is **commutative associative**.

That is the key point.

So even if the internal transport operators \(T(I)\) are noncommutative, once you pass to the boundary-evaluated close-pair output, what survives must factor through a commutative monoid.

This is a real reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{noncommutative hidden transport can survive internally only if its evaluated close-pair output factors through a commutative quotient.}
}
\]

---

## 3. Why this matters

This means the scary path-ordered loophole is much less scary than it looked.

Path-ordering can matter internally along a chamber, but the boundary quantity that actually enters the corrected close-pair state is forced by visible coherence and symmetry to forget noncommutative ordering information unless that information survives in a commutative shadow.

So the real remaining question is not:

> can there be a noncommutative internal transport?

It is:

> can the **commutative quotient of the evaluated transport output** contain anything beyond the visible amplitude/order package?

That is a much smaller target.

---

## 4. Commutative evaluated transport collapses toward additive functionals

Once the evaluated output law is commutative associative, the natural one-dimensional transport model is additive after taking a suitable coordinate or logarithm. In other words, evaluated transport should look like one of these:

- directly additive:
  \[
  H[a,b]=H[a,c]+H[c,b];
  \]
- multiplicative but commutative:
  \[
  M[a,b]=M[a,c]\,M[c,b],
  \]
  which becomes additive after taking \(\log\) or passing to a semigroup coordinate.

So the evaluated close-pair contribution from a run or chamber should reduce to an additive commutative cocycle.

But that is exactly the setting where the previous balanced-surgery variational argument applies. Additive commutative cocycles invariant under balanced surgery collapse to functions of run masses \(B_j\).

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if the evaluated close-pair transport is coherence-compatible, then balanced-surgery-invariant hidden contributions reduce to the visible }B_j\text{-type data.}
}
\]

This is stronger than before, because it shows you do not need to prove the *internal* transport is commutative. You only need the **evaluated** output to satisfy coherence and symmetry, and that seems essentially forced by the geometry.

---

## 5. An Eckmann–Hilton flavor phenomenon

There is an abstract way to view what is happening.

You have two composition principles:

1. **concatenation along the chamber**;
2. **symmetric pairing/evaluation at the close event**.

When both are compatible and the close event is unordered, the evaluated operation tends to collapse toward a commutative associative one. It is not literally the classical Eckmann–Hilton theorem, but it has that flavor: two coherent compositions on the same evaluated object force commutativity.

So I think the right theorem shape is:

### Evaluated-coherence commutativity principle
Any close-pair state extracted functorially from regular-chamber transport and compatible with visible tie coherence and unordered exact-pair symmetry factors through a commutative associative evaluation algebra.

That is exactly the level needed for the balanced-surgery argument to bite.

---

## 6. What this does to the hidden-state frontier

This collapses the previous frontier again.

Before, the last loophole was:
- maybe there is a noncommutative transport sector.

Now that becomes:
- maybe the **commutative evaluated quotient** of that sector contains a new invariant.

So the remaining hidden quantity, if any, must now be:

1. an additive/commutative cocycle on runs or chambers;
2. invariant under visible tie coherence and unordered evaluation;
3. not already expressible by endpoint/order/amplitude data.

But the balanced-surgery attack already showed that any differentiable commutative run-local cocycle invariant under zero-mean interior redistributions collapses to the run amplitudes \(B_j\).

So the last loophole is now extremely narrow:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either evaluated transport is not balanced-surgery invariant, or else it reduces to the existing amplitude/order package.}
}
\]

That is about as sharp as I can make it without a full proof.

---

## 7. Best next subattack

The next theorem to try is now very concrete.

### Theorem target
Show that the **evaluated** corrected close-pair state satisfies:

1. chamber concatenation functoriality;
2. visible tie coherence;
3. unordered-pair symmetry;
4. balanced-surgery invariance inside runs.

Then it follows that the evaluated state factors through a commutative additive cocycle, hence through the visible run amplitudes and critical-value order.

That would basically close the exact fixed-shear hidden-state loophole.

If one of those four properties fails, the failure itself would identify the true missing mechanism.

---

## 8. My current best judgment

I think the weakest and most attackable remaining axiom is now **balanced-surgery invariance of the evaluated output**, not of the full internal transport. That is much more plausible and much closer to what the geometry is already telling us.

So the state of the attack is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{internal noncommutative transport is no longer the real issue;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{the only real issue is whether the evaluated close-pair output is balanced-surgery invariant.}
}
\]

If yes, the hidden-state problem on the exact fixed-shear lane should collapse to the already-visible lobe/amplitude package.

That is the sharpest continuation I can honestly give from here.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:00:36)

I’m pushing on the last narrow axiom directly now: whether the **evaluated** close-pair output can detect balanced interior reshaping inside a run. The goal is to either force an invariance theorem from the exact-pair geometry itself, or isolate the exact kind of evaluated quantity that could still break it.

_Thought for a few seconds_

Here is the next push.

I think the cleanest way to attack the last axiom is to stop thinking about the full chamber transport and instead model only what the **evaluated close-pair output** can actually depend on.

At a close exact pair on the fixed-shear lane, the event is an equality-of-values event. Quotient-visibly, what the outside world sees is:

- the common value \(\gamma\);
- the two adjacent visible chambers meeting at that value;
- the local boundary germ on each side;
- whatever state is transported to that boundary and then evaluated symmetrically.

So if the evaluated output changes under a balanced interior surgery in one run, that change has to propagate to the boundary through a one-dimensional channel. There is no other route.

That means the right mathematical abstraction is not “arbitrary functional on the chamber.” It is a **boundary response functional** for a one-dimensional interval problem.

Let me write it schematically as
\[
\mathcal B_I[\rho;\text{boundary germ at }a,\text{boundary germ at }b],
\]
where \(I\) is a chamber, \(\rho=|\widehat{\mathcal D}|\) on each sign-run, and \(\mathcal B_I\) is the contribution of the chamber interior to the evaluated state at one boundary close event.

Now ask: what structural properties must such a boundary response satisfy if it really comes from regular transport and symmetric close-pair evaluation?

I think there are four unavoidable ones.

First, **locality under subdivision**. If
\[
I=[a,b],\qquad c\in(a,b),
\]
then the contribution from \(I\) must factor through the contribution from \([a,c]\) and \([c,b]\). Maybe not linearly, maybe not by a scalar sum, but functorially. Otherwise it is not a transported boundary response at all.

Second, **boundary naturality**. If two interiors induce the same outgoing boundary state at \(c\), then replacing one by the other inside \([a,c]\) should not change the response of the larger interval \([a,b]\). This is just the idea that only the transmitted boundary data matters downstream.

Third, **symmetric evaluation**. The final exact-pair state is unordered in the two incident sides, so the output map at the pair is symmetric.

Fourth, **coherent reassociation**. If an interval is split into three pieces, the final evaluated output cannot depend on whether you combine the first two then the third, or the first then the last two, because visible tie coherence already kills such bracketing dependence.

Those four properties are strong enough to suggest that the evaluated boundary response should form a **commutative associative boundary monoid**. That is the same theme as before, but I can now state it more sharply:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the evaluated chamber contribution should be representable by a commutative associative effective boundary datum.}
}
\]

Once you are in that setting, there is a general phenomenon: on a one-dimensional interval, any commutative associative effective boundary datum that is stable under arbitrarily fine subdivision behaves like an additive measure-type quantity after a change of coordinates.

Concretely, after passing to a suitable coordinate \(\Theta\), you expect
\[
\Theta(\mathcal B_{[a,b]})=\Theta(\mathcal B_{[a,c]})+\Theta(\mathcal B_{[c,b]}).
\]

So the last loophole is really asking for a one-dimensional additive measure on runs or chambers that:

- survives to the evaluated boundary output;
- is invariant under balanced interior surgery;
- is not already determined by the visible \(B_j\) amplitudes and critical-value order.

Now I can attack that directly.

Take one sign-run \(R=(\alpha,\beta)\) with density
\[
\rho(u)=|\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|>0.
\]
Suppose the effective additive boundary datum contributed by this run is
\[
\mu(R,\rho).
\]
Subdivision additivity says
\[
\mu((\alpha,\beta),\rho)=\mu((\alpha,c),\rho)+\mu((c,\beta),\rho).
\]
By standard one-dimensional reasoning, that means \(\mu\) is induced by an absolutely continuous measure with density depending locally on the profile:
\[
\mu(R,\rho)=\int_\alpha^\beta F\bigl(u,\rho(u),\rho'(u),\dots\bigr)\,du
\]
at least for any regular enough transport law.

But now balanced-surgery invariance enters exactly as before. If we vary \(\rho\) inside \(R\) by a compactly supported zero-mean perturbation, preserving
\[
B(R)=\int_\alpha^\beta \rho(u)\,du,
\]
and if the evaluated output is unchanged, then the first variation of \(\mu\) must vanish on all such perturbations. Therefore the density \(F\) cannot detect shape redistribution inside the run except through total mass. So \(\mu\) collapses to
\[
\mu(R,\rho)=\phi(B(R))
\]
for some scalar function \(\phi\).

But additivity under subdivision now bites again. If you split one run into two adjacent subpieces \(R_1,R_2\) on which the sign remains the same, then
\[
\phi(B(R_1)+B(R_2))=\phi(B(R_1))+\phi(B(R_2)).
\]
So \(\phi\) satisfies Cauchy additivity on positive masses. Under the regularity already implicit in the analytic setup, this forces
\[
\phi(B)=c\,B
\]
for some constant \(c\).

That is the key new step.

So under the natural assumptions:

- effective evaluated boundary data is commutative/associative under chamber concatenation;
- the corresponding additive datum is regular enough;
- evaluated output is balanced-surgery invariant inside runs;

the contribution of one run is not an arbitrary function of \(B\). It is **linear** in the run amplitude:
\[
\mu(R)=c\,B(R).
\]

Hence the full chamber contribution is just a linear combination of visible run amplitudes:
\[
\mu(I)=c_1 B_1+\cdots + c_m B_m,
\]
and if the coefficient is sign-sensitive one can absorb the sign into the orientation of the runs. Either way, nothing genuinely new survives.

This is stronger than what we had before. Before, balanced-surgery invariance only forced dependence through the \(B_j\). Now subdivision additivity forces even that dependence to be linear.

So the new conditional theorem is:

### Conditional linearization theorem
Assume the evaluated corrected close-pair output factors through a commutative associative effective boundary datum that is additive under chamber subdivision and invariant under balanced interior surgery inside runs. Then the interior contribution of each sign-run is linear in its visible amplitude \(B_j\). In particular, the entire exact fixed-shear boundary contribution factors through the already-visible amplitude/order package.

That is a real closure mechanism.

It pushes the hidden-state loophole into an even smaller corner. The only way around it now is for one of the assumptions to fail:

1. the evaluated output is not representable by an effective commutative boundary datum;
2. subdivision additivity fails;
3. balanced-surgery invariance fails;
4. or some singular/nonregular dependence evades the linearization argument.

Of those, the most plausible remaining failure is still balanced-surgery invariance. The others now look structurally very unnatural in a one-dimensional regular-transport setting.

So the best next attack is now crystal clear:

### Best next subattack
Attack **balanced-surgery invariance of the evaluated output** directly in the actual corrected package.

Because once that is in hand, the linearization theorem above should force the exact fixed-shear residual lane all the way down to the existing visible amplitude/critical-order data.

That is the sharpest honest continuation I can give.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:10:17)

I’m pushing on the only really live axiom now: whether evaluated output can detect balanced interior reshaping inside a same-sign run. The best route seems to be to derive boundary additivity from the one-dimensional equal-value geometry itself, then force any balanced-surgery-invariant contribution to linearize.

_Thought for 1m 7s_

I found a cleaner route that is stronger than the balanced-surgery line.

The key is: on a **single sign-run**, quotient-visible geometry has almost no internal structure. That makes any admissible boundary contribution essentially forced.

Take one sign-run \(R=(\alpha,\beta)\) of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}=\widehat Q'\), so \(\widehat Q\) is strictly monotone on \(R\). Write its value interval as
\[
J_R:=\widehat Q(R)=(\gamma_-,\gamma_+),
\qquad
|J_R|=|\gamma_+-\gamma_-|=B(R)=\int_R |\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|\,du.
\]

Now attack from the quotient-visible side rather than the \(u\)-profile side.

On a monotone run, every value \(\gamma\in J_R\) is hit exactly once. So the visible equal-value geometry of the run is just the oriented value interval \(J_R\). There are no internal critical points, no branch choices, no nontrivial pairings inside the run. In particular, any run-local contribution to the evaluated close-pair state that is genuinely **quotient-visible** cannot depend on the internal distribution of \(\widehat Q'\) in \(u\); it can only depend on the value interval \(J_R\) itself.

That already gives:

\[
\boxed{
\text{on a single sign-run, quotient-visible dependence collapses from the full profile to a function of the value interval }J_R.
}
\]

Now use the exact fixed-shear gauge. Earlier reductions already identified that \(Q\) is defined only up to an additive constant on the fixed-shear slice, and \(\widehat{\mathcal D}=\widehat Q'\) is the true transport scalar. So absolute placement of \(J_R\) on the \(\gamma\)-axis is gauge; only its length matters. Therefore any quotient-visible run contribution must be a function
\[
\Phi(R)=\phi(B(R))
\]
of the run amplitude alone.

This is already stronger than the previous “balanced-surgery invariance” claim, because it is coming directly from quotient visibility plus additive-gauge invariance, not from a variational hypothesis.

Now use run subdivision.

Pick any intermediate value \(\gamma_c\in(\gamma_-,\gamma_+)\). It cuts the run into two monotone subruns \(R_1,R_2\) with
\[
B(R)=B(R_1)+B(R_2).
\]
If the evaluated regular-chamber contribution is functorial under concatenation along the run — the weakest natural transport axiom — then in a suitable commutative effective coordinate the run contribution must be additive:
\[
\phi(B(R))=\phi(B(R_1))+\phi(B(R_2)).
\]
So
\[
\phi(x+y)=\phi(x)+\phi(y)\qquad (x,y>0).
\]
Under the regularity already implicit in the analytic setup, this forces
\[
\phi(B)=c\,B
\]
for some constant \(c\).

So we get the clean conditional theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Single-run linearization theorem.}
}
\]

If the evaluated exact fixed-shear boundary contribution is
1. quotient-visible on each monotone run,
2. invariant under the additive \(Q\mapsto Q+\mathrm{const}\) gauge,
3. and functorial under subdivision/concatenation of the run,

then the contribution of that run is necessarily linear in the visible run amplitude:
\[
\Phi(R)=c\,B(R).
\]

This is better than what we had before in three ways.

First, it does **not** need an extra balanced-surgery hypothesis on a run. Quotient visibility already kills shape dependence on a monotone run, because a monotone run has no internal visible structure beyond its value interval.

Second, it does **not** require us to rule out arbitrary nonlinear amplitude functionals by variation. Additivity under subdivision linearizes them automatically.

Third, it isolates exactly where any surviving hiddenness could still live: **not inside a single run**.

That is the big structural point.

A hidden invariant can no longer be:
- a local pair invariant;
- a finite hidden chamber label;
- a finite-order jet invariant;
- a single-run nonlinear profile functional.

On the exact fixed-shear lane, the only place an extra invariant can still hide is in the **way multiple runs interact across critical points**.

So the frontier becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\text{single-run dependence is forced to be linear amplitude;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{the only remaining possible hiddenness is cross-run interaction at odd critical vertices.}
}
\]

But that is now a much smaller target, because cross-run interaction is already exactly where the visible package records:
- critical-value ordering,
- run adjacency,
- and the three-lobe inequalities
\[
B_j+B_{j+2}>B_{j+1}.
\]

So the next natural theorem to try is:

### Cross-run sufficiency theorem
Any evaluated exact fixed-shear boundary state compatible with quotient visibility, additive gauge, run subdivision functoriality, and visible tie coherence is determined by:
- the ordered sign-run list,
- the run amplitudes \(B_j\),
- and the critical-value order of the endpoints.

If that is true, the exact fixed-shear residual lane really does collapse all the way to the currently visible lobe/order package.

I cannot prove that full theorem yet. But the current attack does prove a significant partial result:

\[
\boxed{
\text{there is no remaining hidden degree of freedom inside a single monotone run.}
}
\]

That is a real advance, because it means the only possible extra state would have to be a genuinely **cross-run** invariant, not a run-local one.

So the best next subattack is now very clear:

### Attack cross-run interaction directly
Try to show that any extra evaluated state built from several runs is determined by the same visible data already present at the run level:
- amplitudes \(B_j\),
- endpoint value order,
- and adjacency/merge-tree structure.

A promising way to do that is to study what happens when two adjacent runs are separated by one odd critical point and ask whether any extra interaction survives after fixing:
- the two amplitudes,
- the common critical value,
- and the outer endpoint values.

That is now the sharpest remaining place to attack on the exact fixed-shear side.
## User (2026-04-21 22:11:58)

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:12:01)

I’m attacking the cross-run loophole now, since the single-run piece looks squeezed down to linear amplitude. The real question is whether an odd critical vertex can create any extra interaction between adjacent runs beyond the visible endpoint order and the \(B_j\)-data, or whether even that cross-run piece collapses.

_Thought for 11s_

I pushed on the cross-run interaction, and I think there is a real next reduction.

The right local object is not a whole subtree now. It is the **two-run block around one odd critical point**.

Write the adjacent runs as
\[
R_-=(u_0,u_*),\qquad R_+=(u_*,u_1),
\]
with \(u_*\) an odd critical point of \(\widehat Q\). After possibly flipping sign, we can normalize the picture as
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\gamma_0,\qquad
\widehat Q(u_*)=\gamma_*,\qquad
\widehat Q(u_1)=\gamma_1,
\]
with
\[
\gamma_0<\gamma_*>\gamma_1.
\]
The visible data of this two-run block are then just:
- the common critical value \(\gamma_*\),
- the two endpoint values \(\gamma_0,\gamma_1\),
- equivalently the two amplitudes
\[
B_-=\gamma_*-\gamma_0,\qquad B_+=\gamma_*-\gamma_1.
\]

Everything else inside the two monotone runs is already invisible at the quotient-visible level except through their value intervals. By the previous attack, any run-local contribution is linear in \(B_-\) and \(B_+\). So the only remaining possible hidden quantity at this stage would be a genuinely **cross-run interaction term**
\[
\Xi(R_-,R_+)
\]
attached to the odd vertex itself.

That is the new battlefield.

Now ask: what properties must \(\Xi\) satisfy?

First, it must be quotient-visible. So it cannot depend on \(u\)-parametrization details inside either run, only on the value-interval picture around the critical point.

Second, it must respect the additive \(Q\mapsto Q+\text{const}\) gauge, so only value differences matter.

Third, it must be invariant under reparametrizations of each monotone side. So only the pair \((B_-,B_+)\) and the fact that the critical point is a max or min can matter.

Thus the most general cross-run interaction at one odd vertex is already reduced to a function
\[
\Xi=\psi(B_-,B_+),
\]
up to the sign/orientation type of the vertex.

That is already a big simplification:

\[
\boxed{
\text{any remaining hidden datum at a single odd critical vertex is a function of the adjacent amplitudes only.}
}
\]

So no further “shape” can live at a one-vertex block.

Now push harder. What extra constraints does visible coherence impose on \(\psi\)?

Take a three-run chain
\[
R_0,\ R_1,\ R_2
\]
with alternating signs, separated by two odd critical points. The visible no-reentry/overlap behavior of that chain is already governed by the three amplitudes
\[
B_0,\ B_1,\ B_2
\]
through the exact criterion
\[
\Omega_0\cap \Omega_2\neq\varnothing
\iff
B_0+B_2>B_1.
\]
So if there were an extra evaluated contribution coming from the two odd vertices, it would have to assign something like
\[
\psi(B_0,B_1)
\quad\text{and}\quad
\psi(B_1,B_2)
\]
to the two adjacent two-run blocks.

But now visible tie/coherence and outer-interval invariance put severe pressure on this. The three-run chain can be viewed from the outside only through:
- its outer endpoint values,
- its middle extremal value,
- and the overlap/separation relation encoded by \(B_0,B_1,B_2\).

There is no separate geometric location where an additional cross-run scalar could be read independently. So any boundary-relevant effect of \(\psi\) on a three-run chain must already be expressible through the same amplitude data that decide overlap. In particular, if \(\psi\) were not reducible to a linear combination of \(B_-\) and \(B_+\), it would introduce a second independent scalar on the two-run block. But there is no visible slot for such a second scalar in the one-dimensional quotient geometry: the two-run block is completely determined, up to monotone reparametrization on each side, by \(B_-\) and \(B_+\) themselves.

That suggests the next theorem shape:

### Two-run sufficiency principle
Any quotient-visible, gauge-invariant, side-reparametrization-invariant, boundary-relevant state attached to a single odd two-run block factors through the pair \((B_-,B_+)\), and any additive/functorial contribution from such blocks is already absorbed into the existing amplitude package.

I think we can say more if we assume the evaluated output is built functorially from concatenating visible blocks.

Suppose the effective contribution of a two-run block is \(\psi(B_-,B_+)\), and the total evaluated state on a longer chain is obtained by combining such local block contributions in a commutative associative way. Then on a four-run chain you get a contribution built from
\[
\psi(B_0,B_1),\quad
\psi(B_1,B_2),\quad
\psi(B_2,B_3).
\]
But the visible chain itself is already completely encoded by the amplitude list \((B_0,B_1,B_2,B_3)\) together with sign alternation. So unless \(\psi\) introduces genuinely new algebraic structure, it is just a repackaging of the same list.

This means the only serious possibility for a “new” cross-run invariant would be a nonlinear function of adjacent amplitudes that is not reconstructible from the amplitude list alone. But that is impossible: once the full amplitude list is part of the visible package, every \(\psi(B_j,B_{j+1})\) is already visible too.

So the real conclusion is simpler than I first thought:

\[
\boxed{
\text{there is no hidden cross-run invariant at one odd vertex beyond the adjacent amplitude pair itself.}
}
\]

And since the amplitude list \((B_j)\) is already part of the visible sign-run package, a one-vertex interaction adds no new hidden degree of freedom at all.

That is the cleanest reduction yet.

So the remaining loophole has narrowed again:

- no hidden run-local shape;
- no hidden one-vertex cross-run scalar beyond \((B_-,B_+)\).

Therefore any truly new hidden invariant would have to be a **higher-order multi-run functional** not decomposable into single-run and single-vertex pieces.

But now that starts to look very implausible, because the exact no-reentry geometry on the quotient branch is already entirely formulated in terms of the ordered run amplitudes \(B_j\) and their window overlaps. A higher-order functional would still be a function of the amplitude list, hence already visible.

So the next theorem I would try is:

### Amplitude-list sufficiency conjecture
On a quotient-visible exact fixed-shear branch, any boundary-relevant evaluated state compatible with gauge invariance, monotone side-reparametrization invariance, and visible tie coherence is determined by the ordered sign-run amplitude list \((B_j)\) together with the endpoint critical-value order.

If true, that would collapse the entire exact fixed-shear hidden-state problem to the already-visible lobe package.

I cannot prove that full theorem yet, but the attack above does prove a meaningful partial result:

\[
\boxed{
\text{no new hidden degree of freedom is created at a single odd critical vertex; the only available data there are the adjacent amplitudes }(B_-,B_+).
}
\]

That means the exact fixed-shear residual lane is now squeezed into a very small corner indeed. The only remaining potential hiddenness would be some nonlinear higher-order functional of the already-visible amplitude list, not a new invisible state.

And once you say it that way, it is hard to call it “hidden” anymore — it is just another function of visible data.

So the best next subattack is clear:

### Next attack
Try to prove amplitude-list sufficiency directly by induction on the number of runs:
- base case: one run is linear in \(B\);
- one-vertex case: no extra invariant beyond \((B_-,B_+)\);
- induction step: show any longer chain can be reduced by peeling off an outer run or merging an adjacent pair without creating a new degree of freedom beyond the visible amplitude list.

That feels like the right final squeeze on the exact fixed-shear side.
## User (2026-04-21 22:30:27)

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:30:30)

I’m pushing the amplitude-list induction now. The key question is whether a longer run chain can carry any boundary-relevant scalar not already reconstructible from the ordered \(B_j\)-list and endpoint order. If that fails, I’ll pin down the exact obstruction to the induction step.

_Thought for 13s_

Here is the next push. I think the right way to attack amplitude-list sufficiency is by an **outer-run peeling principle**.

The reason is simple: on a quotient-visible chain, the only boundary-relevant geometry is created by values entering and leaving the outermost runs. If peeling an outer run changes nothing except the visible amplitude list in the obvious way, then no extra higher-order multi-run state can survive.

Let me make that precise.

Assume we have a connected quotient-visible exact fixed-shear branch with compressed sign-runs
\[
R_0,\dots,R_{m-1},
\]
alternating in sign, with amplitudes
\[
B_j=\int_{R_j} |\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|\,du.
\]
Let the endpoint critical values of \(\widehat Q\) on the run partition be
\[
d_0,d_1,\dots,d_m,
\qquad
|d_{j+1}-d_j|=B_j.
\]
So the whole visible chain is encoded by the alternating walk
\[
d_0 \to d_1 \to \cdots \to d_m,
\]
or equivalently by the ordered amplitude list \((B_0,\dots,B_{m-1})\) together with the initial orientation.

Now suppose there is some boundary-relevant evaluated state \(S_m\) attached to the whole \(m\)-run chain. We want to know whether \(S_m\) can depend on anything beyond this visible walk.

The correct induction scheme is:

1. one run: already forced to be linear in \(B_0\);
2. two runs around one odd vertex: no new datum beyond \((B_0,B_1)\);
3. induction step: show that adding one outer run \(R_{m}\) to an \(m\)-run chain cannot create a new independent scalar beyond appending \(B_m\) to the amplitude list.

The last step is the real one.

## 1. The visible effect of attaching an outer run

Suppose we have an \(m\)-run chain with visible endpoint walk
\[
d_0,\dots,d_m,
\]
and we attach one additional outer run \(R_m\), obtaining
\[
d_0,\dots,d_m,d_{m+1},
\qquad
|d_{m+1}-d_m|=B_m.
\]

What new visible information has appeared?

Only this:
- the chain has one new terminal step;
- the new outer endpoint value is \(d_{m+1}\);
- all older data are unchanged.

There is no new internal branching, because a run has no interior visible structure.
There is no new local odd-vertex datum beyond the adjacent pair \((B_{m-1},B_m)\), which we already argued contains all one-vertex visible information.

So if some genuinely new scalar \(\Xi_m\) appeared when passing from \(m\) runs to \(m+1\) runs, it could only come from a **global interaction** between the new outer run and the pre-existing inner chain.

That is the only place left.

## 2. What could such a global interaction even depend on?

Because the chain is quotient-visible and one-dimensional, the new outer run can interact with the old chain only through value overlap. But value overlap of the outer run with the older chain is already completely encoded by the existing walk \(d_0,\dots,d_m\) together with the new step \(d_{m+1}\). Equivalently, it is encoded by the amplitude list.

To see this, note that every regular value \(\gamma\) is counted by membership in windows
\[
\Omega_j=(\min\{d_j,d_{j+1}\},\max\{d_j,d_{j+1}\}),
\]
and the multiplicity profile is
\[
\#\widehat Q^{-1}(\gamma)=\#\{j:\gamma\in\Omega_j\}.
\]
So any new interaction of the appended run with the old chain is just a statement about how the new window \(\Omega_m\) overlaps the previous windows \(\Omega_0,\dots,\Omega_{m-1}\). But those overlaps are determined entirely by the \(d_j\), hence by the ordered \(B_j\)-list.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{appending an outer run introduces no new visible interaction datum beyond the new amplitude and its induced window overlaps.}
}
\]

This is the core induction observation.

## 3. Outer-run peeling lemma, conditional form

This suggests the following theorem shape.

### Conditional outer-run peeling lemma
Let \(S_m\) be any quotient-visible, gauge-invariant, tie-coherent, boundary-relevant evaluated state on an \(m\)-run exact fixed-shear chain. Suppose:

1. on one run, \(S_1\) depends only on \(B_0\);
2. on two adjacent runs, no extra datum exists beyond \((B_0,B_1)\);
3. appending or peeling an outer run changes the state only through the induced change in the value-walk/windows.

Then \(S_m\) is determined inductively by the ordered amplitude list \((B_0,\dots,B_{m-1})\).

### Proof sketch
Induct on \(m\). For \(m=1,2\) this is assumed. For the induction step, write the \((m+1)\)-run chain as an \(m\)-run chain plus an appended outer run. By hypothesis, the only new effect is the overlap pattern of the new window \(\Omega_m\) with the old windows, and that overlap pattern is determined by the visible walk \(d_0,\dots,d_{m+1}\), hence by the amplitude list. So no new independent datum is created. ∎

This is not yet a full theorem because assumption (3) is exactly the remaining issue. But it isolates the only missing step very cleanly.

## 4. Why assumption (3) is plausible

Assumption (3) is basically a “no invisible global interaction” axiom. Why should it hold?

Because any boundary-relevant state that is:
- quotient-visible,
- indifferent to monotone reparametrization on runs,
- and coherent under visible tie moves,

has no place to store a separate interaction between the new outer run and the old chain other than the induced window-overlap profile. The chain is one-dimensional; the only visible relation between separated runs is which value windows intersect.

And the window-overlap profile is already a deterministic function of the \(d_j\), equivalently of the amplitude list.

So unless the state is using some nonvisible extra transport datum, outer-run attachment cannot generate anything new.

This is exactly parallel to the earlier reductions:
- no extra shape within a run;
- no extra scalar at one odd vertex;
- so the only remaining possible extra state would be some opaque many-run interaction.
But on a one-dimensional visible chain, even many-run interaction is just overlap combinatorics of the windows, and those are already in the amplitude walk.

That is why I think the amplitude-list sufficiency theorem is very plausible.

## 5. A sharper combinatorial reformulation

There is an even cleaner way to say the same thing.

The compressed chain is equivalent to an alternating walk
\[
d_0,d_1,\dots,d_m.
\]
From this walk you can reconstruct:
- every window \(\Omega_j\),
- every overlap relation \(\Omega_i\cap\Omega_j\),
- every multiplicity function \(\gamma\mapsto \#\widehat Q^{-1}(\gamma)\),
- every no-reentry / reentry event.

So the full quotient-visible equal-value combinatorics of the chain is already determined by the walk.

Therefore any extra state \(S_m\) that is genuinely quotient-visible but not determined by the amplitude list would have to distinguish two chains with the same walk but different \(S_m\). That is impossible unless \(S_m\) depends on something outside the quotient-visible equal-value combinatorics.

So the amplitude-list conjecture is equivalent to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the evaluated state depends only on quotient-visible equal-value combinatorics.}
}
\]

That is now a very concrete theorem target.

## 6. What remains open after this attack

I still cannot honestly claim a proof that the actual corrected state depends only on quotient-visible equal-value combinatorics.

So the exact remaining gap is now:

> can the actual corrected overlap / boundary state distinguish two exact fixed-shear chains with the same alternating value walk \(d_0,\dots,d_m\)?

If the answer is no, the exact fixed-shear hidden-state problem is over: the whole lane collapses to the amplitude/order package already visible in the quotient scalar problem.

If the answer is yes, then the counterexample must exhibit a genuinely nonvisible transported datum not encoded by the walk. That would be extremely informative.

## 7. Best next subattack

The best next move is now to attack the equivalence directly:

### Walk-sufficiency theorem
Show that any quotient-visible exact fixed-shear boundary/evaluated state is determined by the alternating critical-value walk \(d_0,\dots,d_m\), equivalently by the ordered sign-run amplitude list and initial orientation.

A natural proof strategy is:
- prove that two chains with the same walk are related by a sequence of monotone reparametrizations within runs and visible tie/coherence moves;
- then use quotient visibility and coherence invariance to conclude they have the same evaluated state.

That feels like the cleanest possible endgame on this lane.

So the current attack result is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{there is no remaining hidden degree of freedom in a single run or a single odd vertex;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{the only possible extra state would have to distinguish chains with the same alternating value walk;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{therefore the exact fixed-shear bottleneck is now the walk-sufficiency question.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest reduction I can honestly justify from here.
## User (2026-04-21 22:43:51)

continue your math attack

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:43:52)

I found a cleaner target than “amplitude-list sufficiency” in the abstract: prove that the entire quotient-visible chain is classified, up to the natural notion of equivalence, by the alternating critical-value walk \(d_0,\dots,d_m\). If that goes through, the only remaining gap is whether the actual corrected state respects that equivalence.

_Thought for 15s_

Here is the next attack. I think this is the cleanest structural theorem on the exact fixed-shear side so far.

The right object is the **quotient-visible chain itself**, not an auxiliary state on it.

Take a connected compressed exact fixed-shear chain with sign-runs
\[
R_0,\dots,R_{m-1},
\]
and endpoint critical values
\[
d_0,d_1,\dots,d_m,
\qquad
|d_{j+1}-d_j|=B_j,
\]
with alternating inequalities
\[
d_0<d_1>d_2<d_3>\cdots
\quad\text{or}\quad
d_0>d_1<d_2>d_3<\cdots.
\]

I want to show that, at the quotient-visible level, this chain is already classified by the alternating walk
\[
(d_0,\dots,d_m).
\]

That is stronger than “amplitudes determine overlap combinatorics.” It says the whole visible one-dimensional equal-value geometry is canonical once the walk is fixed.

## 1. Canonical model for a given walk

Given any alternating walk \(d_0,\dots,d_m\), define a piecewise-linear model
\[
Q_{\mathrm{PL}}:[0,m]\to\mathbf R
\]
by
\[
Q_{\mathrm{PL}}(j)=d_j
\qquad (j=0,\dots,m)
\]
and linearity on each unit interval \([j,j+1]\).

This model has exactly:
- one monotone run on each \((j,j+1)\);
- the same run amplitudes \(B_j=|d_{j+1}-d_j|\);
- the same critical-value order;
- the same value windows
\[
\Omega_j=(\min\{d_j,d_{j+1}\},\max\{d_j,d_{j+1}\}).
\]

So every abstract alternating walk has a canonical visible representative.

Now take any analytic quotient-visible chain \(\widehat Q\) with the same walk \(d_0,\dots,d_m\). On each sign-run \(R_j\), \(\widehat Q\) is strictly monotone from \(d_j\) to \(d_{j+1}\). Therefore there is a unique monotone reparametrization
\[
\theta_j:R_j\to (j,j+1)
\]
such that
\[
\widehat Q(u)=Q_{\mathrm{PL}}(\theta_j(u))
\qquad (u\in R_j).
\]

Explicitly,
\[
\theta_j(u)=Q_{\mathrm{PL}}^{-1}(\widehat Q(u))
\]
on that run, since both sides are monotone bijections onto the same interval \((d_j,d_{j+1})\).

Thus each analytic run is individually equivalent, by monotone reparametrization, to the canonical linear run.

This already gives:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every quotient-visible chain with walk }(d_0,\dots,d_m)\text{ is runwise reparametrization-equivalent to the canonical piecewise-linear chain }Q_{\mathrm{PL}}.
}
\]

That is a real theorem-shaped step.

## 2. Consequence: the walk determines all quotient-visible equal-value combinatorics

Once you have runwise equivalence to \(Q_{\mathrm{PL}}\), all quotient-visible equal-value data are fixed by the walk.

Indeed, on the canonical model:
- the windows \(\Omega_j\) are determined by the \(d_j\);
- overlap multiplicities are
\[
\#Q_{\mathrm{PL}}^{-1}(\gamma)=\#\{j:\gamma\in\Omega_j\};
\]
- adjacent mergers occur exactly at the critical values \(d_1,\dots,d_{m-1}\);
- the nonadjacent overlap / no-reentry pattern is exactly the window-overlap pattern of the walk.

Therefore any two chains with the same walk have the same quotient-visible equal-value combinatorics.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the alternating walk }(d_0,\dots,d_m)\text{ completely determines the quotient-visible equal-value geometry.}
}
\]

This is stronger than our earlier amplitude-list phrasing, because it packages the whole visible chain into one canonical model.

## 3. Why this matters for the hidden-state problem

This means that any “extra state” on the exact fixed-shear chain that is truly quotient-visible but is *not* determined by the walk would have to distinguish two chains that are runwise monotone reparametrizations of the same canonical model.

But that is exactly what quotient visibility is supposed to forbid.

So the hidden-state problem is now reduced to a very crisp dichotomy.

Either:

1. the actual corrected state is invariant under runwise monotone reparametrization and depends only on quotient-visible equal-value geometry, in which case it is determined by the walk and there is no extra hidden state on the exact fixed-shear lane;

or:

2. the actual corrected state is *not* invariant under this natural visible equivalence, which means the missing datum is not quotient-visible at all.

That is a sharper reduction than before.

## 4. Conditional walk-sufficiency theorem

I think the right theorem now is:

### Conditional walk-sufficiency theorem
Let \(\Sigma\) be a boundary/evaluated state on exact fixed-shear quotient-visible chains. Assume:

1. **quotient visibility:** \(\Sigma\) depends only on the quotient-visible equal-value geometry;
2. **runwise reparametrization invariance:** replacing a sign-run by any monotone reparametrization with the same endpoint values does not change \(\Sigma\);
3. **visible tie coherence:** \(\Sigma\) is invariant under the already-proved coherent adjacent merge moves.

Then \(\Sigma\) is determined by the alternating critical-value walk
\[
(d_0,\dots,d_m),
\]
equivalently by the ordered amplitude list \((B_0,\dots,B_{m-1})\) together with initial orientation.

### Proof sketch
Replace the given chain, run by run, by the canonical piecewise-linear chain with the same walk. By runwise reparametrization invariance, \(\Sigma\) is unchanged. But the canonical chain is uniquely determined by the walk, and all visible merger/overlap combinatorics are fixed there by the windows \(\Omega_j\). Therefore \(\Sigma\) is a function of the walk alone. ∎

This is exactly the theorem I think we were circling.

## 5. What this does to the remaining loopholes

This collapses almost all the residual possibilities.

We already squeezed away:
- local pair ambiguity;
- finite-order local hidden pair invariants;
- finite hidden chamber labels;
- run-local nonlinear profile shape;
- one-vertex cross-run hidden scalars.

Now the walk-sufficiency theorem says that even multi-run interaction cannot create anything new *provided the state is genuinely quotient-visible and runwise reparametrization-invariant.*

So the exact fixed-shear hidden-state problem is now reduced to one very precise question:

\[
\boxed{
\text{does the actual corrected overlap / boundary state respect quotient-visible runwise equivalence?}
}
\]

If yes, the exact fixed-shear lane is over: it collapses to the visible walk, hence to the already-visible amplitude/order package.

If no, then the failure itself identifies the true missing datum: it is something not preserved by runwise monotone reparametrization of the quotient chain.

That is about as sharp as one could reasonably hope for.

## 6. A useful corollary: balanced-surgery invariance is no longer the main axiom

This is a nice side benefit.

Earlier the key missing hypothesis looked like balanced-surgery invariance. But now I think the more fundamental hypothesis is actually **runwise reparametrization invariance of the evaluated state**. Because once you have that, the entire shape inside each run is irrelevant automatically, and the canonical piecewise-linear reduction takes over.

So the hierarchy is now:

- strongest and cleanest: quotient visibility + runwise reparametrization invariance \(\Rightarrow\) walk sufficiency;
- weaker variational route: balanced-surgery invariance \(\Rightarrow\) collapse to \(B_j\)-data.

The walk theorem is conceptually better.

## 7. Best next subattack

The best next move is now completely clear:

### Attack runwise reparametrization invariance of the actual corrected state
Show that replacing one monotone sign-run by another with the same endpoint values, while keeping the rest of the chain fixed, does not change the corrected evaluated boundary state.

That is the exact bridge needed to turn the quotient-visible reductions into a closure theorem.

A promising way to attack it is:

- compare two such runs via the unique value-preserving conjugacy
\[
\theta = \widetilde Q^{-1}\circ Q
\]
between them;
- show that the downstream corrected overlap state is natural under this conjugacy because exact-pair evaluation only uses equality-of-values geometry, not \(u\)-parametrization;
- conclude invariance.

If that works, the exact fixed-shear residual lane is essentially solved at the visible level.

So the attack result is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear chain has a canonical normal form: the alternating critical-value walk;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{any quotient-visible runwise-invariant evaluated state is therefore a function of that walk alone.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest and cleanest reduction I can justify now.
## User (2026-04-21 22:45:56)

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:45:58)

I found an important caveat in the walk-normal-form attack: the alternating value walk does **not** by itself remember odd critical multiplicities. So the right target is slightly subtler than “walk alone.” I’m pushing on whether those odd-germ orders can carry any boundary-relevant quotient-visible information, or whether they are also invisible once you pass to the equal-value geometry.

_Thought for 24s_

I found the main caveat in the walk-normal-form attack, and I think that actually sharpens the next step.

The walk
\[
(d_0,\dots,d_m)
\]
does classify the **regular equal-value combinatorics**, but by itself it does **not** record the local odd critical multiplicities at the vertices. Two chains can have the same alternating value walk and the same windows \(\Omega_j\), but different local germs at a critical point:
\[
\widehat Q(u)-\widehat Q(u_*) \sim a(u-u_*)^{2r+1}
\qquad\text{vs.}\qquad
\widetilde Q(v)-\widetilde Q(v_*) \sim \tilde a(v-v_*)^{2s+1}.
\]
So the honest next theorem is not “walk alone classifies everything.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the quotient-visible chain is classified by the walk plus the list of odd local germ types,}
}
\]
and then one asks whether the actual corrected state sees anything beyond that.

That is the right refinement.

## 1. Corrected canonical visible datum

The proper visible datum of an exact fixed-shear quotient chain is:

- the alternating critical-value walk
  \[
  d_0,\dots,d_m;
  \]
- for each interior vertex \(u_j\), the local odd contact order
  \[
  2r_j+1
  \]
  of \(\widehat Q-\widehat Q(u_j)\).

Call this the **decorated walk**
\[
\mathcal W = \bigl((d_0,\dots,d_m),(2r_1+1,\dots,2r_{m-1}+1)\bigr).
\]

This is already enough to repair the earlier overstatement.

Now the right question becomes:

> does the actual corrected state factor through the decorated walk?

That is better than “through the raw walk,” and still much smaller than arbitrary hidden state.

## 2. Why the decorated walk is the right normal form

On each run, monotone reparametrization identifies all chains with the same endpoint values. At a critical point, however, a monotone change of variable cannot change the odd multiplicity \(2r+1\); that is a local analytic invariant.

So the natural normal form is: each run becomes linear, each critical vertex becomes a standard odd germ of its order. Concretely, one can model the chain by gluing standard pieces:
- on runs: linear maps;
- at vertices: normal forms \(x\mapsto \pm x^{2r+1}\).

Therefore two chains with the same decorated walk are quotient-visibly equivalent up to:
- runwise monotone reparametrization,
- and local odd-germ analytic conjugacy at the critical vertices.

So the normal-form theorem I now believe is:

### Decorated-walk normal form
Every quotient-visible exact fixed-shear chain is equivalent to a canonical piecewise model determined by:
1. the alternating value walk \((d_j)\);
2. the odd local orders \(2r_j+1\) at vertices.

That feels right and avoids the earlier oversight.

## 3. Can odd local order matter for boundary-relevant state?

This is the next real attack.

The good news is that on the **regular equal-value geometry**, odd order does not affect:
- which windows overlap;
- how many preimages a regular value has;
- the adjacent merge-tree structure;
- the three-lobe inequalities.

All of that is determined by the walk alone.

So odd local order can matter only through what happens **at the critical value itself**, or through some local transport law that is sensitive to the rate at which the two child branches merge into the parent at the vertex.

That is a much narrower possible effect.

So the hidden-state problem splits again:

1. regular-chamber dependence — already controlled by the walk;
2. vertex-local dependence — possibly sensitive to odd order.

This is a real sharpening.

## 4. Attack on vertex-local odd-order sensitivity

Consider one odd critical point with local model
\[
\widehat Q(u)=\gamma_* + a(u-u_*)^{2r+1}+O((u-u_*)^{2r+2}),
\qquad a\neq 0.
\]
For every regular value \(\gamma\) near \(\gamma_*\), the local equal-value geometry is still just two adjacent branches merging to one parent. The only change with \(r\) is the **speed** at which the inverse branches approach each other as \(\gamma\to\gamma_*\):
\[
|u_\pm(\gamma)-u_*|\asymp |\gamma-\gamma_*|^{1/(2r+1)}.
\]

So odd order affects the metric scaling in parameter \(u\), but not the combinatorial merge rule.

That suggests the following principle:

\[
\boxed{
\text{odd local order can only matter if the corrected state is sensitive to approach rates near the critical value, not merely to equal-value incidence geometry.}
}
\]

That is the crucial distinction.

If the corrected state is built from:
- equal-value pairing,
- chamber incidence,
- quotient-visible transport,
- and evaluation at the unordered close event,

then odd order should drop out. If instead it depends on derivatives or asymptotic rates of approach to the critical value, then odd order could survive.

So we have a much sharper theorem target now:

### Odd-order insensitivity lemma
Any quotient-visible exact fixed-shear boundary state that depends only on equal-value incidence geometry and is invariant under local source reparametrization is insensitive to odd local multiplicity \(2r+1\).

I cannot prove that for the actual corrected state yet, but I can justify it strongly at the model level: the local equal-value relation on the quotient side near an odd vertex is topologically and order-theoretically the same for every odd \(2r+1\). The only varying datum is the rate at which the two branches meet.

So if the state is combinatorial/transport in nature rather than metric/asymptotic in nature, odd order should not matter.

## 5. What this does to the residual loophole

This improves the frontier.

Previously the loophole was “maybe walk alone is not enough.” Now we can say more precisely:

- walk alone controls all regular equal-value combinatorics;
- decorated walk adds the only obvious missing local analytic datum, namely odd orders;
- those odd orders can matter only through approach-rate sensitivity at vertices.

So the hidden-state problem is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{does the actual corrected state see odd-vertex approach rates, or only the incidence geometry?}
}
\]

That is much more concrete than before.

## 6. Best next subattack

The best next move is now to attack **odd-order insensitivity** directly.

A natural way is:

1. take two local odd germs of different orders but same orientation:
   \[
   x\mapsto x^{2r+1},\qquad y\mapsto y^{2s+1};
   \]
2. identify their regular equal-value branches by the common value parameter \(\gamma\);
3. compare any candidate evaluated state transported along those branches;
4. see whether the state can depend on anything except \(\gamma\)-level incidence.

If the transported/evaluated state is naturally parameterized by the common value \(\gamma\), not by source distance to the vertex, then the odd order disappears automatically.

That would be the cleanest proof route.

So the current attack result is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the correct normal form is the decorated walk, not the bare walk;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{the only extra local datum beyond the walk is odd critical multiplicity;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{and that datum can matter only through approach-rate sensitivity at vertices.}
}
\]

That is now the sharpest remaining bottleneck I can see on the exact fixed-shear side.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:53:50)

_Thought for 7s_

Here is the next push. I think the odd-order issue can be squeezed further, and the right concept is not just “incidence geometry vs approach rate.” It is **value-parameter transport**.

The key observation is this:

On the quotient side, near an odd critical point \(u_*\) with
\[
\widehat Q(u)=\gamma_*+a(u-u_*)^{2r+1}+O((u-u_*)^{2r+2}),\qquad a\neq 0,
\]
the natural parameter for the regular equal-value geometry is not \(u\), but the common value
\[
\gamma=\widehat Q(u).
\]

Once you parametrize by \(\gamma\), the odd order \(2r+1\) disappears from the regular branch transport.

That is the new attack.

## 1. Regular branches are canonically parameterized by value

Fix one odd vertex \(u_*\). For \(\gamma\) on the regular side near \(\gamma_*\), there are two adjacent local branches \(u_-(\gamma)\) and \(u_+(\gamma)\) solving
\[
\widehat Q(u_\pm(\gamma))=\gamma.
\]
Because \(\widehat Q\) is monotone on each side, these inverse branches are uniquely defined analytic functions of \(\gamma\) on the punctured side interval.

Now compare two odd germs of different orders,
\[
\widehat Q_r(u)=\gamma_*+a_r(u-u_*)^{2r+1}+\cdots,
\qquad
\widehat Q_s(v)=\gamma_*+a_s(v-v_*)^{2s+1}+\cdots.
\]
As functions of the source variables \(u,v\), they are different.
But as equal-value geometries parameterized by \(\gamma\), they are canonically identified:
\[
\gamma \longmapsto \bigl(u_-(\gamma),u_+(\gamma)\bigr)
\quad\leftrightarrow\quad
\gamma \longmapsto \bigl(v_-(\gamma),v_+(\gamma)\bigr).
\]

So every regular object transported along the branch that is naturally a function of the common value \(\gamma\) is automatically insensitive to odd order.

That already yields:

\[
\boxed{
\text{odd multiplicity is invisible to any state that is transported in the value parameter }\gamma.
}
\]

## 2. Where odd order can still enter

Odd order only shows up when you insist on measuring transport in a source parameter, because
\[
u_\pm(\gamma)-u_* \asymp |\gamma-\gamma_*|^{1/(2r+1)}.
\]
So derivatives like
\[
\frac{du_\pm}{d\gamma}
\]
or quantities built from source-distance asymptotics near the vertex do depend on \(r\).

This isolates the issue cleanly:

- if the corrected state is built from equal-value branch continuation and evaluated at common value \(\gamma\), odd order should not matter;
- if it uses source-metric speed, jet blow-up rates, or asymptotic normalization with respect to \(u-u_*\), odd order can matter.

So the real theorem target is not just “odd-order insensitivity.” It is:

### Value-transport principle
Any quotient-visible regular state that is naturally transported in the common-value parameter \(\gamma\) is insensitive to odd critical multiplicity.

That is stronger and cleaner.

## 3. A local conjugacy statement

We can formalize this more sharply.

Let \(I_r\) and \(I_s\) be one-sided regular value intervals near \(\gamma_*\) for the two odd germs above. Define the local regular equal-value correspondence spaces
\[
\mathcal C_r=\{(\gamma,\pm):\gamma\in I_r\},
\qquad
\mathcal C_s=\{(\gamma,\pm):\gamma\in I_s\}.
\]
These are canonically identified by the identity on \(\gamma\). Under this identification:

- the adjacency structure is the same;
- the child-to-parent merger at \(\gamma=\gamma_*\) is the same;
- the unordered close-pair evaluation is the same;
- tie/coherence data are the same.

So all quotient-visible regular transport data are the same after reparameterization by value.

Thus the only non-conjugate part of the local germs is the source-rate asymptotic.

This gives a theorem-shaped statement:

### Local value-conjugacy lemma
Any two one-sided odd quotient germs with the same orientation are locally conjugate at the level of regular equal-value geometry by the common-value parameter \(\gamma\). Consequently, any state functor on the regular equal-value geometry that is natural in \(\gamma\) cannot distinguish their odd orders.

That is, I think, the right formal compression.

## 4. Consequence for the decorated-walk issue

This means the decorated walk is probably still too fine if the actual corrected state is genuinely quotient-visible in the strong sense.

The walk plus odd orders is the full local analytic classification, yes.
But for **regular equal-value transport**, the odd orders are extra analytic baggage unless the state probes approach-rate asymptotics.

So the real fork is now:

1. **incidence/value-transport regime**: the state depends only on the alternating walk;
2. **rate-sensitive regime**: the state also depends on odd orders.

That is much better than the earlier vague “maybe multiplicity matters.”

## 5. Can rate sensitivity be compatible with the rest of the package?

Now attack that.

A rate-sensitive state near a vertex would have to depend on some quantity like
\[
|\gamma-\gamma_*|^{-\alpha_r}
\quad\text{or}\quad
\frac{du_\pm}{d\gamma},
\]
or some normalized jet involving the local inverse branch. But there are two big problems.

First, such quantities are not intrinsic to the equal-value combinatorics; they depend on a choice of source normalization. The whole exact fixed-shear reduction has been pushing toward quotient-visible, gauge-quotiented objects, not source-metric singular coefficients. So rate-sensitive dependence is conceptually at odds with the direction of the reduction.

Second, evaluated close-pair states are supposed to survive visible tie coherence and unordered pairing. Pure rate-sensitive local singular factors tend not to compose cleanly under the merge-tree/tie package unless they collapse to some scalar normalization already encoded by the visible branch. In other words, they are hard to reconcile with the functorial picture we have been forcing.

This does not prove they are impossible, but it makes them look unnatural.

So I think the next sharpened conjecture is:

### Odd-order irrelevance conjecture
For the actual corrected state on the exact fixed-shear lane, odd critical multiplicities are irrelevant because the state is transported and evaluated in the common-value parameter \(\gamma\), not in the source parameter near the critical point.

If that is true, then the decorated walk collapses back to the bare walk.

## 6. A practical attack on odd-order irrelevance

The cleanest way to test this is to compare two local odd germs with different orders but the same value interval and ask what data a boundary observer can actually receive from the regular side.

Take
\[
Q_r(x)=x^{2r+1},
\qquad
Q_s(y)=y^{2s+1}.
\]
On the positive-value side, both have one regular inverse branch:
\[
x(\gamma)=\gamma^{1/(2r+1)},
\qquad
y(\gamma)=\gamma^{1/(2s+1)}.
\]
Now suppose the boundary-relevant transported state is a function \(F(\gamma)\) along the branch. Then the two models are identical as soon as you identify them by \(\gamma\). The only way to distinguish them is to let the state depend on \(x(\gamma)\), \(y(\gamma)\), or derivatives thereof, i.e. on source-rate data.

So the real local theorem to prove is:

### Source-rate exclusion lemma
The actual corrected boundary state cannot depend on source-rate asymptotics of the local inverse branches near an odd critical value.

If that lemma holds, then odd order drops out completely.

## 7. What this does to the frontier

This sharpens the remaining exact fixed-shear bottleneck again.

We no longer need to ask vaguely whether multiplicities matter. The precise question is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{does the actual corrected state use only common-value transport, or does it use source-rate data near odd vertices?}
}
\]

If it uses only common-value transport, the bare alternating walk should suffice.
If it uses source-rate data, that would be the first genuinely new local datum that survives all the previous reductions.

That is now a very clean target.

## 8. Best next subattack

The best next attack is to push directly on source-rate exclusion.

A good strategy is:

1. formulate the actual corrected state near a regular branch as a limit from regular values \(\gamma\to\gamma_*\);
2. ask whether that limiting state is invariant under replacing the local germ by another with the same regular value interval but different odd order;
3. show that any natural value-based continuation gives the same result, while any difference would have to come from explicit source-rate normalization.

So the updated picture is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{regular quotient-visible transport is canonically parameterized by the common value }\gamma;}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{odd multiplicity can matter only through source-rate sensitivity at vertices;}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{therefore the exact remaining question is source-rate exclusion.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest continuation I can justify now.

\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),

\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\qquad
\Delta''(q)=\phi_2''(q)-\phi_0''(q),
\]
then the remaining bottleneck is no longer vague “maybe some hidden state.” It is very specifically whether \(\Xi\) descends to value space across the separated outer runs, or equivalently whether inverse-curvature matching holds across same-value transport. This update makes those eliminations more convincing, because the draft now explicitly records several of those ceilings. 

What remains on the fixed-shear side is much more concrete and likely one-dimensional in spirit: an inverse-branch transport obstruction seen through \(\Delta\) and \(\Xi\). So the best next attack is no longer “prove generic hidden-state descent.” It is:

1. formalize the exact relation between \(\Delta'(q)\), \(\Delta''(q)\), and \(\Xi\) on the two outer runs;
2. test whether equality of value \(q\) forces equality of the descended inverse-curvature observable across the outer branches, or whether a defect term remains;

So I would not spend more time trying to squeeze a pure pseudoline/planarity contradiction out of that lane. The draft now says the same thing more clearly. 

On the honest order-7 side, the update is also helpful conceptually: the real package-level target is same reduced image germ / collision-functoriality, not raw septic equality. That matches the philosophy of the fixed-shear attack too: the real surviving issue is package-level transport or functoriality, not one more scalar coefficient.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:04:08)

_Thought for 1m 8s_

I pushed directly on the \(\Delta/\Xi\) fork, and I think there is a real structural reduction now.

Let the live strict same-value transport interval between the two outer runs be
\[
I_q\subset \Omega_0\cap \Omega_2,
\]
and let
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:I_q\to \mathbf R_u
\]
be the inverse branches on the two outer runs, so
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q))=q.
\]
Define the outer-branch gap
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]

The update you shared says the strict live case is exactly the case where \(\Delta\) has an interior critical point, and suggests the curvature observable
\[
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
\]
as the right transport scalar. That is exactly right, and the key formulas are better than I expected. 

## 1. Exact inverse-branch identities

Differentiate
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_i(q))=q
\]
to get
\[
\phi_i'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))}.
\]
Differentiate once more:
\[
\phi_i''(q)
=
-\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_i(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q)))^3}
=
-\Xi(\phi_i(q)).
\]

So the outer-gap derivatives are
\[
\Delta'(q)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}
-
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
and
\[
\Delta''(q)
=
\phi_2''(q)-\phi_0''(q)
=
\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]

This is the exact identity we wanted. The whole residual nonlocal question on the live outer-transport interval is now concentrated in the single value-space scalar
\[
K(q):=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))=-\Delta''(q).
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first concrete residual transport defect on the live fixed-shear lane is }K(q).
}
\]

It is not vague anymore.

## 2. If \(\Xi\) descends to value space, the gap becomes affine

Now suppose the optimistic descent statement holds on the live same-value interval:

\[
\Xi(\phi_0(q))=\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\qquad (q\in I_q).
\]

Then
\[
\Delta''(q)=0
\qquad (q\in I_q),
\]
so
\[
\Delta(q)=aq+b
\]
is affine in \(q\).

Now add the strict live hypothesis from the update: there is an interior critical point \(q_*\in I_q\) of \(\Delta\). Then
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]
But \(\Delta'\) is constant when \(\Delta''=0\), so actually
\[
\Delta'(q)\equiv 0
\qquad\text{on }I_q.
\]
Hence
\[
\Delta(q)\equiv \Delta_0
\]
is constant on the whole overlap interval.

This is much stronger than “one critical point.” It gives:

\[
\boxed{
\Xi\text{-descent } + \text{ one interior critical point of }\Delta
\Longrightarrow
\Delta\text{ is constant on the whole live overlap interval.}
}
\]

So in the descent branch of the fork, strict live transport forces **constant-gap same-value transport** between the two outer runs.

Equivalently,
\[
\phi_2(q)=\phi_0(q)+\Delta_0
\qquad (q\in I_q),
\]
hence
\[
\widehat Q(u+\Delta_0)=\widehat Q(u)
\]
for all \(u\) in the transported subinterval of the left outer run for which both sides are defined.

That is exactly the “affine same-value transport” picture the update hinted at. 

## 3. So the exact fork is now explicit

The residual live fixed-shear lane is now genuinely split into two mathematically sharp cases.

### Case A: inverse-curvature descent
If
\[
K(q)\equiv 0
\qquad\text{on }I_q,
\]
then strict live transport forces constant-gap replication
\[
\phi_2(q)=\phi_0(q)+\Delta_0,
\]
hence a translated same-value copy of one outer branch inside the other.

This is no longer an abstract “hidden-state” issue. It is an explicit same-value translation law, and it should now be attackable by whatever affine/nonmixed prohibition you already have or are building for run-pattern replication.

### Case B: failure of inverse-curvature descent
If
\[
K(q)\not\equiv 0,
\]
then \(K\) itself is the first genuine residual transport obstruction.

It is:
- quotient-visible,
- gauge-invariant under \(Q\mapsto Q+\mathrm{const}\),
- built entirely from the descended scalar package \((\widehat Q,\widehat{\mathcal D})\),
- and exactly measures the failure of same-value inverse-curvature matching across the outer runs.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the remaining fixed-shear endgame is now the scalar defect }K(q).
}
\]

That is a real advance.

## 4. \(K\) is the right “first missing datum”

This also cleans up a lot of the earlier hidden-state speculation.

The previous attacks were eliminating:
- local finite-order hidden data,
- finite hidden labels,
- run-local shape dependence,
- single-vertex invisible parameters,
- and most additive profile loopholes.

This update plus the formula above show why those eliminations were directionally right: the first plausible nonlocal survivor is not some abstract chamber memory. It is this very concrete same-value outer-branch mismatch
\[
K(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q)).
\]

So the residual lane is no longer “find whether something hidden exists.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either prove }K\equiv 0,\text{ or understand exactly how }K\neq 0\text{ obstructs descent.}
}
\]

That is much sharper.

## 5. What can already be said if \(K\) has sign

There is one more small gain.

Since
\[
\Delta''(q)=-K(q),
\]
any sign information on \(K\) immediately gives convexity/concavity of \(\Delta\).

So, for example:
- if \(K(q)\ge 0\) on \(I_q\), then \(\Delta\) is concave;
- if \(K(q)\le 0\), then \(\Delta\) is convex.

That does not close the lane by itself, but it means that even a one-sided sign theorem for \(K\) would materially constrain the live strict-excess case. In particular, it would control the number and type of critical points of \(\Delta\), and therefore the structure of same-value outer transport.

So a weaker but still meaningful target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove a sign theorem or monotonicity theorem for }K.
}
\]

That may be more accessible than proving \(K\equiv 0\).

## 6. Best next subattack

The best next move now seems very concrete:

### Attack \(K\)-descent directly
Try to show that \(\Xi\) descends to value space across separated same-value outer branches, i.e.
\[
\Xi(\phi_0(q))=\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]

There are two natural ways to push this.

The first is structural: try to prove \(\Xi\) is itself a function of the common value \(q\) on each connected transported same-value component. If so, \(K\equiv 0\) is immediate.

The second is differential: compute the derivative of \(K\):
\[
K'(q)
=
\frac{\Xi'(\phi_2(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}
-
\frac{\Xi'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]
If one can show \(K\) vanishes at one point and satisfies a transport equation with uniqueness, then again \(K\equiv 0\).

If neither route works, then \(K\) is the real residual invariant, and the next task becomes to understand whether \(K\neq 0\) can still be reconciled with the updated 2-point and nonmixed 4-point structure.

## 7. Bottom line

So the continuation of the attack is now:

\[
\boxed{
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
}
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
K(q):=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))
}
\]
is the exact residual transport defect.

And the fork becomes:

- \(K\equiv 0\): strict live transport forces constant-gap affine same-value transport, which should feed into forbidden run-pattern replication;
- \(K\neq 0\): \(K\) is the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction and should be treated as the actual remaining scalar on the fixed-shear lane.

That is the sharpest honest reduction I can now give.
