## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:05:30)

I’m pushing directly on the new scalar defect \(K(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))\). The goal now is not broad hidden-state elimination, but to see whether \(K\) itself obeys a transport law or factorization that either forces \(K\equiv 0\) or turns it into a visibly controlled scalar on the outer-run overlap interval.

_Thought for 3m 46s_

I pushed the \(\Delta/\Xi\) route one step further, and there is a real new conclusion:

\[
\boxed{
\text{in the genuinely live strict three-run case, the descent branch }K\equiv 0\text{ is actually impossible.}
}
\]

So the residual fixed-shear lane no longer splits evenly into two plausible cases. In the only genuinely live quotient-visible case, the scalar defect
\[
K(q):=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))
\]
must be nonzero somewhere, and in fact cannot vanish identically on the outer-transport interval.

That is a real sharpening.

## 1. Exact setup

Work in the strict live three-run configuration
\[
R_0,\ R_1,\ R_2
\]
with signs \(+\,-\,+\), so
\[
d_0<d_1>d_2<d_3,
\]
and the live same-value overlap interval is
\[
I_q=(q_-,q_+)
=
(\max\{d_0,d_2\},\,\min\{d_1,d_3\}).
\]
Let
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:I_q\to \mathbf R_u
\]
be the inverse branches on the two outer runs, and define
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]

As already noted,
\[
\Delta'(q)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}
-
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\qquad
\Delta''(q)
=
\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\]
hence
\[
K(q):=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))=-\Delta''(q).
\]

## 2. Endpoint slope asymptotics in the strict live case

Here is the key observation.

In a genuine strict three-run overlap interval, the lower overlap endpoint \(q_-\) is realized by one outer branch at a **critical endpoint** and the other at a **regular endpoint**. Likewise for the upper overlap endpoint \(q_+\): one outer branch is critical there and the other regular.

Concretely, in the generic \(+\,-\,+\) picture:

- near the lower endpoint \(q_-\), the right outer inverse branch \(\phi_2(q)\) approaches the critical point at the start of \(R_2\), so
  \[
  \phi_2'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}\to +\infty,
  \]
  while \(\phi_0'(q)\) stays finite;

- near the upper endpoint \(q_+\), the left outer inverse branch \(\phi_0(q)\) approaches the critical point at the end of \(R_0\), so
  \[
  \phi_0'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}\to +\infty,
  \]
  while \(\phi_2'(q)\) stays finite.

Therefore
\[
\Delta'(q)\to +\infty
\quad\text{as }q\downarrow q_-,
\qquad
\Delta'(q)\to -\infty
\quad\text{as }q\uparrow q_+.
\]

This is exactly why strict excess forces an interior critical point of \(\Delta\): the sign change in \(\Delta'\) is automatic from endpoint geometry.

## 3. Why \(K\equiv 0\) is impossible

Now suppose, for contradiction, that
\[
K(q)\equiv 0
\qquad (q\in I_q).
\]
Then
\[
\Delta''(q)=0
\qquad (q\in I_q),
\]
so \(\Delta'(q)\) is constant on \(I_q\).

But the endpoint asymptotics above show that \(\Delta'\) tends to \(+\infty\) at the left end and to \(-\infty\) at the right end. A constant function cannot do that.

Contradiction.

So:

\[
\boxed{
K\not\equiv 0
\quad\text{on the live strict three-run overlap interval.}
}
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\Xi(\phi_2(q))=\Xi(\phi_0(q))
\text{ cannot hold identically on a genuine strict outer-transport interval.}
}
\]

That is the main new result.

## 4. Interpretation

This means the optimistic “\(\Xi\)-descent across outer runs” branch of the fork is not actually available in the genuinely live strict case.

So the fixed-shear endgame is even cleaner than before:

- quartic–sextic local hidden reset is dead;
- local finite-order two-atom hidden data are dead;
- the strict live same-value transport interval is governed by
  \[
  \Delta''(q)=-K(q),
  \]
  and the defect \(K\) cannot vanish identically.

Thus the live residual invariant is not hypothetical anymore. In the strict three-run case, it is **necessarily present**.

So the real problem is not “prove \(K\equiv0\).” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{understand what sign/structure }K(q)\text{ must satisfy, and whether that already forbids strict excess.}
}
\]

That is a much more concrete target.

## 5. Endpoint sign of \(K\)

There is a bit more one can already say.

Near the lower endpoint \(q_-\), the critical branch is the one coming out of a local minimum on the right outer run. In the local model
\[
q-q_- \sim c(u-u_*)^{2r+1},
\]
one gets
\[
\phi_2''(q)\to -\infty,
\qquad
\Xi(\phi_2(q))=-\phi_2''(q)\to +\infty.
\]
The left branch remains regular, so \(\Xi(\phi_0(q))\) stays finite. Hence
\[
K(q)\to +\infty
\qquad\text{as }q\downarrow q_-.
\]

Similarly, near the upper endpoint \(q_+\), the critical branch is the one approaching a local maximum on the left outer run. There
\[
\phi_0''(q)\to +\infty,
\qquad
\Xi(\phi_0(q))=-\phi_0''(q)\to -\infty,
\]
while the right branch stays regular. Hence again
\[
K(q)\to +\infty
\qquad\text{as }q\uparrow q_+.
\]

So in the standard strict \(+\,-\,+\) configuration:

\[
\boxed{
K(q)\to +\infty \text{ at both ends of }I_q.
}
\]

That is quite suggestive. It means
\[
\Delta''(q)=-K(q)<0
\]
near both ends, so \(\Delta\) is forced to be concave near both ends and \(\Delta'\) decreases out of \(+\infty\) on the left and into \(-\infty\) on the right.

This makes a sign theorem
\[
K(q)\ge 0 \quad \text{on }I_q
\]
look like exactly the right closure statement: it would make \(\Delta\) globally concave, hence give a unique interior maximum and a very rigid outer-transport geometry.

I cannot prove \(K\ge 0\) yet, but the endpoint asymptotics are a strong hint.

## 6. The updated bottleneck

So the fixed-shear lane is now sharper than before:

1. the residual scalar is
   \[
   K(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q));
   \]
2. in the genuine strict three-run case,
   \[
   K\not\equiv 0;
   \]
3. in fact
   \[
   K(q)\to +\infty
   \quad\text{at both overlap endpoints}.
   \]

So the remaining question is no longer descent. It is a **sign/convexity question for \(K\)**.

That is the cleanest attack frontier so far.

## 7. Best next subattack

The best next move is now:

### Attack a one-sided sign theorem for \(K\)
Try to prove
\[
K(q)\ge 0
\quad\text{on the live outer-transport interval }I_q.
\]

Because then
\[
\Delta''(q)\le 0,
\]
so \(\Delta\) is concave on \(I_q\), with \(\Delta'\) running from \(+\infty\) to \(-\infty\). That would force a unique interior critical point and could make the strict transport geometry rigid enough to feed into the updated nonmixed 4-point exclusion machinery.

Even a weaker result like “\(K\) has at most one sign change” would already be useful.

So the attack has now converted the fixed-shear hidden-state problem into a very explicit scalar convexity problem on the outer overlap interval.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:12:31)

I’ve moved the fixed-shear problem into an explicit scalar question about  
\[
K(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q)).
\]
Now I’m pushing on whether \(K\) admits a structural sign law or at least a monotonicity law. The cleanest route is to compute its local asymptotics at odd vertices and then see whether there is a comparison principle across the outer branches.

_Thought for 2m 0s_

I found an important correction, and it leads to a better attack.

My previous claim that \(K\equiv 0\) is impossible in the strict three-run case was too strong. It is only impossible in the overlap regimes where the live same-value interval touches at least one **critical** outer endpoint. There is one genuinely different regime — when one outer value window is strictly contained in the other — where both overlap endpoints are regular, and there \(K\equiv 0\) is not ruled out by endpoint asymptotics.

That correction actually sharpens the fork.

## 1. Exact setup and corrected endpoint classification

In the strict \(+\,-\,+\) three-run configuration,
\[
d_0<d_1>d_2<d_3,
\]
the live same-value overlap interval is
\[
I_q=(q_-,q_+)
=
(\max\{d_0,d_2\},\,\min\{d_1,d_3\}).
\]
Let
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:I_q\to\mathbf R_u
\]
be the inverse branches on the two outer runs, and set
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\qquad
p_i(q):=\phi_i'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))}.
\]
Then
\[
\Delta'(q)=p_2(q)-p_0(q),
\qquad
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\]
with
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
So the transport defect is
\[
K(q):=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))=-\Delta''(q).
\]

Now classify the overlap endpoints.

The lower endpoint \(q_-\) is either:
- \(d_2\), a critical value on the right outer run, if \(d_2\ge d_0\), or
- \(d_0\), a regular outer endpoint on the left outer run, if \(d_0>d_2\).

The upper endpoint \(q_+\) is either:
- \(d_1\), a critical value on the left outer run, if \(d_1\le d_3\), or
- \(d_3\), a regular outer endpoint on the right outer run, if \(d_3<d_1\).

So there are four endpoint regimes:

1. **CC**: \(q_-=d_2\), \(q_+=d_1\)  
2. **CR**: \(q_-=d_2\), \(q_+=d_3\)  
3. **RC**: \(q_-=d_0\), \(q_+=d_1\)  
4. **RR**: \(q_-=d_0\), \(q_+=d_3\)

My earlier “\(\Delta'\to+\infty\) on the left and \(-\infty\) on the right” applies only in the cases where the corresponding endpoint is critical.

So the corrected statement is:

\[
\boxed{
K\equiv 0\text{ is impossible in the CC, CR, and RC regimes, but not ruled out a priori in the RR regime.}
}
\]

Indeed, if \(K\equiv0\), then \(\Delta''=0\), so \(\Delta'\) is constant. This is impossible whenever one endpoint forces \(\Delta'\) to blow up and the other does not match. But in RR both endpoints are regular, so \(\Delta'\) may well stay finite.

That is the correction.

## 2. What remains if \(K\equiv 0\): affine gap law

In any regime, if
\[
K(q)\equiv 0\quad\text{on }I_q,
\]
then
\[
\Delta''(q)=0,
\]
so
\[
\Delta(q)=cq+b
\]
for constants \(c,b\).

This already gives a much stronger structure than just “no defect.” It says same-value outer transport is **affine in the common value**.

If we write
\[
u=\phi_0(q),
\]
then \(q=\widehat Q(u)\), and
\[
\phi_2(q)=\phi_0(q)+\Delta(q)=u+c\,\widehat Q(u)+b.
\]
So on the transported subinterval \(U_0\) of the left outer run, defining
\[
T(u):=u+c\,\widehat Q(u)+b,
\]
we get the functional equation
\[
\widehat Q(T(u))=\widehat Q(u).
\]

This is the right exact form of the descent branch.

So in the RR regime, the optimistic branch is not “constant-gap translation” unless \(c=0\). The actual law is a more general **value-affine self-transport**
\[
T(u)=u+c\,\widehat Q(u)+b,
\qquad
\widehat Q(T(u))=\widehat Q(u).
\]

That is already highly rigid.

Differentiating gives
\[
\widehat Q'(T(u))\,T'(u)=\widehat Q'(u),
\qquad
T'(u)=1+c\,\widehat Q'(u),
\]
hence
\[
\widehat Q'(T(u))
=
\frac{\widehat Q'(u)}{1+c\,\widehat Q'(u)}.
\]

So even in the one regime where \(K\equiv0\) is not immediately impossible, it forces a very strong nonlocal self-symmetry.

## 3. Endpoint constraints in the RR regime

In the RR regime we have
\[
d_0>d_2,
\qquad
d_3<d_1,
\]
so the right outer value interval is strictly contained in the left:
\[
(d_2,d_3)\subset (d_0,d_1).
\]
The overlap interval is
\[
I_q=(d_0,d_3).
\]

Then \(T\) maps the left subinterval
\[
U_0=\{u\in R_0:\ \widehat Q(u)\in(d_0,d_3)\}
\]
onto the right subinterval
\[
U_2=\{u\in R_2:\ \widehat Q(u)\in(d_0,d_3)\},
\]
and the boundary conditions become:
- the left endpoint of \(U_0\) maps to the point of \(R_2\) with value \(d_0\),
- the right endpoint of \(U_0\) maps to the outer endpoint of \(R_2\) with value \(d_3\).

So \(c,b\) are not free. They are fixed by two endpoint constraints:
\[
T(u_a)=v_a,\qquad T(u_b)=v_b,
\]
where
\[
\widehat Q(u_a)=d_0,\ \widehat Q(u_b)=d_3,\ 
\widehat Q(v_a)=d_0,\ \widehat Q(v_b)=d_3.
\]

Equivalently,
\[
v_a-u_a=c\,d_0+b,
\qquad
v_b-u_b=c\,d_3+b.
\]
So
\[
c=\frac{(v_b-u_b)-(v_a-u_a)}{d_3-d_0},
\qquad
b=(v_a-u_a)-c\,d_0.
\]

Thus even the affine self-transport branch is highly overdetermined by the geometry.

This makes RR much more attackable than it first looked.

## 4. What the true fork now is

So the fixed-shear strict three-run lane is now:

### Generic regimes CC / CR / RC
Here \(K\equiv0\) is impossible by endpoint asymptotics. So the scalar defect \(K\) must be genuinely present.

### Nested-window regime RR
Here \(K\equiv0\) is not ruled out by endpoint asymptotics. But if it happens, it forces the explicit functional equation
\[
\widehat Q(u+c\widehat Q(u)+b)=\widehat Q(u)
\]
on a whole transported subinterval of the left outer run.

So the descent branch survives only in this very rigid value-affine form.

That is much sharper than before.

## 5. Best next attack in the RR regime

The right target is now no longer abstract descent. It is to attack the functional equation
\[
\widehat Q(u+c\widehat Q(u)+b)=\widehat Q(u)
\]
directly.

There are two obvious subcases.

### (i) \(c=0\)
Then
\[
\widehat Q(u+b)=\widehat Q(u),
\]
so the left outer run contains an actual translated same-value copy into the right outer run. That is the strongest replication law and should be easiest to try to rule out using the updated nonmixed 4-point or affine same-value transport machinery.

### (ii) \(c\neq0\)
Then the symmetry is not a translation but a value-dependent shear
\[
T(u)=u+c\widehat Q(u)+b.
\]
Still, it is a concrete self-map preserving \(\widehat Q\)-values on a full interval, and it satisfies
\[
T'(u)=1+c\widehat Q'(u)>0
\]
on \(U_0\), so it is monotone.

This may still be attackable by differentiating repeatedly and comparing endpoint behavior, or by viewing it as a first-order functional differential relation on the run.

So the fixed-shear lane has become a much cleaner dichotomy:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either }K\not\equiv0,\text{ or (only in RR) there is a value-affine self-transport law }T(u)=u+c\widehat Q(u)+b.
}
\]

That is a good reduction.

## 6. The next best subattack

The best next move is now:

### Attack the RR descent equation directly
Try to rule out, or sharply constrain,
\[
\widehat Q(u+c\widehat Q(u)+b)=\widehat Q(u)
\]
on a nontrivial subinterval of a monotone outer run.

A good way to start is to differentiate:
\[
\widehat Q'(T(u))(1+c\widehat Q'(u))=\widehat Q'(u),
\]
then again:
\[
\widehat Q''(T(u))(1+c\widehat Q'(u))^2+\widehat Q'(T(u))\,c\,\widehat Q''(u)=\widehat Q''(u).
\]
Substituting for \(\widehat Q'(T(u))\) may produce a closed relation between \(\widehat Q''(T(u))\) and \(\widehat Q''(u)\), and maybe between \(\Xi(T(u))\) and \(\Xi(u)\), which could contradict the geometry unless \(c=0\) or unless \(T\) is trivial.

So the attack has now split the fixed-shear endpoint into:
- a generic nonzero defect case \(K\not\equiv0\),
- and one very rigid nested-window descent equation in the exceptional RR regime.

That is the cleanest correction-and-continuation I can give right now.
## User (2026-04-21 23:16:38)

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:16:43)

I’m pushing on the exceptional RR regime now, since the other three endpoint regimes already force a nonzero \(K\). The goal is to see whether the value-affine self-transport law in RR can be reduced further — ideally to a contradiction, or at least to something rigid enough to hand off cleanly to the nonmixed/affine side.

_Thought for 11s_

I pushed the RR branch further. There is a real next reduction.

Recall the exceptional RR regime is the only strict three-run case where endpoint asymptotics do not already force
\[
K(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))
\]
to be nonzero. In that regime, if \(K\equiv 0\) on the live overlap interval \(I_q=(d_0,d_3)\), then
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)=cq+b
\]
is affine in the common value \(q\), and equivalently on the left outer run subinterval
\[
U_0=\{u\in R_0:\ \widehat Q(u)\in(d_0,d_3)\}
\]
there is a monotone self-transport
\[
T(u)=u+c\,\widehat Q(u)+b
\]
such that
\[
\widehat Q(T(u))=\widehat Q(u)
\qquad (u\in U_0).
\]

The new point is that this equation is much more rigid than it first looks, because on a monotone run it can be rewritten as a conjugacy relation for the inverse branches.

Let
\[
\psi_0:(d_0,d_1)\to R_0,\qquad \psi_2:(d_2,d_3)\to R_2
\]
be the full inverse branches of \(\widehat Q\) on the two outer runs. On the overlap interval \((d_0,d_3)\), the self-transport law is exactly
\[
\psi_2(q)-\psi_0(q)=cq+b.
\]
Differentiate:
\[
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\psi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\psi_0(q))}=c.
\]
So the RR descent branch is equivalent to saying that the reciprocal-slope difference of the two outer inverse branches is constant on the whole live overlap interval.

That already has a strong consequence.

## 1. Constant reciprocal-slope gap forces a first-order transport law

Write
\[
p_i(q):=\psi_i'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\psi_i(q))}.
\]
Then the RR descent law is
\[
p_2(q)-p_0(q)=c.
\]
Integrating gives
\[
\psi_2(q)=\psi_0(q)+cq+b,
\]
which is just the affine gap law again. But now interpret it as a first-order equation along the common-value parameter \(q\): once \(\psi_0\) is known, \(\psi_2\) is completely forced.

In particular, the right outer branch is not an independent object anymore. It is determined from the left one by the first-order value-affine relation. So the strict RR branch would amount to a highly nongeneric synchronization of the two outer runs.

That is already one good reason to think RR descent is fragile.

## 2. Endpoint constraints determine \(c\) and \(b\) explicitly

At the two overlap endpoints,
\[
q=d_0,\qquad q=d_3,
\]
we have
\[
\psi_2(d_0)-\psi_0(d_0)=c\,d_0+b,
\qquad
\psi_2(d_3)-\psi_0(d_3)=c\,d_3+b.
\]
Hence
\[
c=\frac{\bigl(\psi_2(d_3)-\psi_0(d_3)\bigr)-\bigl(\psi_2(d_0)-\psi_0(d_0)\bigr)}{d_3-d_0},
\]
and
\[
b=\psi_2(d_0)-\psi_0(d_0)-c\,d_0.
\]

So the affine gap law is completely pinned by the two boundary alignments. There is no extra freedom.

That means a contradiction can be sought by comparing this forced \(c\) with the derivative relation
\[
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\psi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\psi_0(q))}=c
\]
in the interior.

## 3. A useful reparametrization: the right branch as a graph over the left branch

Because \(\psi_0\) is invertible on \((d_0,d_3)\), define
\[
F:=\psi_2\circ \widehat Q|_{U_0}:U_0\to U_2.
\]
Then \(F\) satisfies
\[
\widehat Q(F(u))=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
F(u)-u=c\,\widehat Q(u)+b.
\]
This is exactly the same law as before, but phrased entirely on the source side.

Differentiate:
\[
F'(u)-1=c\,\widehat Q'(u),
\]
and from \(\widehat Q(F(u))=\widehat Q(u)\),
\[
\widehat Q'(F(u))\,F'(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]
Substitute \(F'(u)=1+c\widehat Q'(u)\) to get
\[
\widehat Q'(F(u))
=
\frac{\widehat Q'(u)}{1+c\widehat Q'(u)}.
\]

Now differentiate once more:
\[
\widehat Q''(F(u))\,F'(u)^2+\widehat Q'(F(u))\,F''(u)=\widehat Q''(u),
\]
with
\[
F''(u)=c\,\widehat Q''(u).
\]
Using the formula for \(\widehat Q'(F(u))\), this simplifies to
\[
\widehat Q''(F(u))
=
\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(1+c\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]

This is the exact second-derivative transport law on the RR descent branch.

Now compute \(\Xi\):
\[
\Xi(F(u))
=
\frac{\widehat Q''(F(u))}{(\widehat Q'(F(u)))^3}
=
\frac{\widehat Q''(u)/(1+c\widehat Q'(u))^3}{\bigl(\widehat Q'(u)/(1+c\widehat Q'(u))\bigr)^3}
=
\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
=
\Xi(u).
\]

So in the RR branch, \(K\equiv0\) is actually equivalent to the existence of a nontrivial self-map \(F\) on the source satisfying
\[
\widehat Q(F)=\widehat Q,\qquad \Xi(F)=\Xi,\qquad F(u)-u=c\,\widehat Q(u)+b.
\]

This is stronger than the raw affine gap law: it says both \(\widehat Q\) and \(\Xi\) are preserved by the same value-affine self-transport.

## 4. Why this is a promising contradiction route

This now looks much more rigid than before. On a generic monotone outer run, the pair
\[
(\widehat Q(u),\Xi(u))
\]
should determine \(u\) locally unless there is a special functional dependence between \(\Xi\) and \(\widehat Q\).

But under RR descent, the map \(F\neq \mathrm{id}\) preserves both coordinates:
\[
(\widehat Q(F(u)),\Xi(F(u)))=(\widehat Q(u),\Xi(u)).
\]

So if the map
\[
u\mapsto (\widehat Q(u),\Xi(u))
\]
is injective on the relevant transported left-run subinterval, then \(F(u)=u\), contradiction.

That gives a very clean new fork:

### RR contradiction route A
Prove that on a monotone outer run subinterval,
\[
u\mapsto (\widehat Q(u),\Xi(u))
\]
is injective.

Then RR descent is impossible unless \(F=\mathrm{id}\), which cannot happen because the two outer runs are disjoint.

### RR contradiction route B
If injectivity fails, then there is a functional dependence
\[
\Xi(u)=\mathcal G(\widehat Q(u))
\]
along that subinterval.

But this is exactly the full descent relation \(\Xi=\mathcal G\circ \widehat Q\) we earlier considered. In other words, failure of injectivity would mean the curvature observable already descends to value space on that outer run. That is a very strong special structure and may itself be exploitable.

So either way, the RR branch has become much sharper.

## 5. What this means for the global fixed-shear fork

The strict three-run fixed-shear lane is now:

- In CC/CR/RC regimes, \(K\equiv0\) is impossible by endpoint blow-up mismatch, so \(K\) is necessarily a genuine nonzero defect somewhere.

- In the exceptional RR regime, \(K\equiv0\) implies a value-affine self-transport \(F\) preserving both \(\widehat Q\) and \(\Xi\):
  \[
  \widehat Q(F)=\widehat Q,\qquad \Xi(F)=\Xi,\qquad F(u)=u+c\widehat Q(u)+b.
  \]
  Hence RR descent is possible only if the pair \((\widehat Q,\Xi)\) fails to separate points on the left outer run.

That is a much cleaner residual picture.

## 6. Best next subattack

The best next move is now to attack the separation property of \((\widehat Q,\Xi)\) on a monotone outer run.

A very concrete theorem target is:

### Separation lemma
On the relevant transported subinterval of a monotone outer run, the map
\[
u\longmapsto (\widehat Q(u),\Xi(u))
\]
is injective.

If that holds, the RR descent branch dies immediately.

A weaker but still useful target is:

### Value-descent alternative
If \((\widehat Q,\Xi)\) is not injective on that run, then \(\Xi\) already descends as a function of \(\widehat Q\) there. That should make the whole outer transport much more rigid and maybe reducible to the nonmixed/affine side.

So the new attack result is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{RR descent }(K\equiv0)\text{ forces a self-map preserving both }\widehat Q\text{ and }\Xi;
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{hence RR can survive only if the pair }(\widehat Q,\Xi)\text{ fails to separate points on an outer run.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest continuation I can now give.
## User (2026-04-21 23:21:45)

please give a complete recap of your whole attack along with new findings since most recent paper update. include theorems, no-gos, structural findings, negative findings, goodies, etc.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:22:46)

_Thought for 8s_

Here is the full recap of my attack, including what I think is solid, what I think is only conditional, and the new findings since the most recent paper update.

The short version is: my lane has progressively stopped looking like a “find one more hidden local invariant” problem and has turned into a very sharp quotient-transport problem on the residual exact fixed-shear corner. The recent paper update made that sharper still: it isolated the genuine residual corner as \((S=0)\cap(K_v=0)\), confirmed that the quartic–sextic rung is locally rank-one and carries no hidden finite-order reset, and reframed the live strict case as an inverse-branch-gap problem governed by \(\Delta\) and the curvature observable \(\Xi\). That update meshes very well with the direction of my attack. 

I’ll separate the recap into: what was eliminated, what was reduced, what I think is genuinely new, and what remains open.

## 1. What my attack has been trying to do

I’ve been attacking the residual mixed exact fixed-shear lane from the most structural side I could find, rather than chasing more local 2-point or 4-point closures. The guiding question was:

> once the visible quotient/sheared geometry is rigid, can any residual “hidden state” remain that is not already encoded by the descended quotient-visible data?

That turned into several increasingly sharp subquestions:

- can there be a second local or pointwise highest-new invariant?
- can there be a hidden finite chamber label or hidden local two-atom datum?
- can there be a hidden run-local profile functional?
- can there be a cross-run hidden interaction not already visible in the quotient chain?
- if not, what is the first genuinely irreducible residual scalar on the fixed-shear lane?

The more I pushed, the more the answer became: almost everything local/combinatorial dies, and the residual problem becomes a very concrete same-value transport problem on outer inverse branches.

## 2. Big no-gos / negative findings from the attack

These are the main things my attack now strongly suggests are **not** the right residual obstruction.

First, there is no good reason to keep hunting for another local pointwise field on this lane. The generator audit already says the current explicit pointwise highest-new package contains no second primitive generator beyond \(r=q^{(7)}\), and the update reinforces that the live target is transport/global/package-level, not “one more scalar.” 

Second, I do not see a viable hidden finite-order local two-atom invariant on the exact fixed-shear involutive slice. Once exact repeated pairs are forced to be \((t,-t)\), any involution-symmetric finite-order local analytic invariant built from the visible package descends to the quotient variable \(u=t^2\). So there is no plausible residual obstruction of the form “a local symmetric pair-jet scalar we forgot.”

Third, I do not see a viable finite hidden chamber-label theory unless it collapses to visible subtree statistics. And under the natural no-exogenous-birth assumption, even that collapses further to visible leaf-count-type data; then further surgery arguments suggest even those cannot matter for any local finite-jet boundary law. So I do not think the residual exact fixed-shear obstruction is a finite hidden combinatorial label.

Fourth, on a single sign-run, I think the quotient-visible geometry is essentially exhausted. A monotone run has no internal visible critical structure. So any quotient-visible boundary contribution from one run can only depend on the value interval it carries, hence on its amplitude \(B\), and under functorial subdivision it should linearize. So I do not think there is any genuinely new run-local hidden degree of freedom.

Fifth, at a single odd critical vertex, I do not think there is any hidden cross-run scalar beyond the adjacent amplitude pair \((B_-,B_+)\). The one-vertex visible data are just the two adjacent value drops/rises and the vertex orientation. So I do not think one-vertex hidden interaction is the source of the residual obstruction either.

So the accumulated negative message is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the residual fixed-shear problem is not another local field, not a finite local pair invariant, not a finite hidden label, not a run-local shape invariant, and not a one-vertex hidden scalar.}
}
\]

That broad elimination is consistent with the recent update, which explicitly says the quartic–sextic rung is inert for the lobe-amplitude obstruction and that the whole ordinary analytic swap-invariant local scalar layer is dead in the corrected collision-cancellation chart. 

## 3. Structural findings that I think are real

The most important positive structural findings from my attack are these.

### 3.1. The right visible object is the quotient-visible chain, not the source pair
On the exact fixed-shear slice, the local repeated-pair geometry is involutive and quotient-diagonal. So the correct visible geometry is downstairs in \(u=t^2\), and regular same-value transport should be read there, not in the source pair itself. This is now fully aligned with the updated paper state. 

### 3.2. Single-run visible dependence collapses to amplitude
On a single monotone sign-run of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}=\widehat Q'\), the only quotient-visible data are the endpoint values, equivalently the run amplitude
\[
B(R)=\int_R |\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)|\,du.
\]
So any quotient-visible run-local contribution can only depend on \(B\), and under concatenation/subdivision it should in fact linearize in \(B\).

I do not claim this is a formal theorem in the actual package yet, but structurally it is very strong: one sign-run carries no hidden internal state.

### 3.3. One odd vertex carries no hidden scalar beyond adjacent amplitudes
Around a single odd critical point, the visible two-run block is determined by
\[
(B_-,B_+)
\]
plus the max/min orientation. So any one-vertex boundary-relevant quantity that is quotient-visible can only be a function of those adjacent amplitudes. Again: no new hidden degree of freedom there.

### 3.4. The natural “classification datum” of a quotient chain is the alternating value walk, refined if necessary by odd orders
I first thought the bare alternating value walk
\[
(d_0,\dots,d_m)
\]
should classify the quotient-visible chain. I later corrected that: the bare walk classifies the regular equal-value combinatorics, but not the odd local germ orders. So the correct normal form datum is the **decorated walk**:
\[
\bigl((d_0,\dots,d_m),(2r_1+1,\dots,2r_{m-1}+1)\bigr).
\]

However, I then pushed further and found a better conceptual split: odd local order only matters through **source-rate sensitivity** near the vertex. If the actual corrected state is transported and evaluated in the common-value parameter \(q\), rather than in source-rate asymptotics, then odd order should drop out. So the real bottleneck is not “the decorated walk” per se, but whether the state sees source-rate data at odd vertices.

That is a useful refinement.

## 4. The major transition after the recent paper update

Before the update, my attack was still partially framed as a “hidden state / hidden transport memory” problem. The update gave a much better concrete target.

The key new update-derived findings are:

- the residual mixed 2-point corner is truly \((S=0)\cap(K_v=0)\);
- the local corrected chart is the collision-cancellation chart with \((m,\delta,\lambda)\) or \((m,\delta,\omega)\), not the naive diagonal chart;
- the quartic–sextic rung is quotient-visible, rank-one, and inert for the explicit lobe-amplitude obstruction;
- the strict live three-run case is naturally encoded by outer inverse branches \(\phi_0,\phi_2\), the gap
  \[
  \Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
  \]
  and the curvature observable
  \[
  \Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
  \]

This was the big turning point. It means the residual problem is not some abstract hidden datum anymore. It is a concrete inverse-branch transport problem. 

## 5. My main new findings since the recent paper update

These are the parts I think are genuinely new from my own attack after reading the update.

### 5.1. Exact inverse-branch identities
On the live outer overlap interval \(I_q\), with inverse branches
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_i(q))=q,
\]
one has
\[
\phi_i'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))},
\qquad
\phi_i''(q)=-\Xi(\phi_i(q)).
\]
Therefore the outer-gap function
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)
\]
satisfies
\[
\Delta'(q)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}
-
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
and
\[
\Delta''(q)
=
\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]

So the exact residual transport defect is the scalar
\[
K(q):=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))=-\Delta''(q).
\]

This is the cleanest concrete scalar I reached in the whole attack.

### 5.2. The fixed-shear endgame is now the \(K\)-problem
This leads to the sharp formulation:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first concrete residual transport defect on the live fixed-shear lane is }K(q).
}
\]

So the residual lane is no longer “maybe some hidden state survives.” It is:

- either understand when \(K\equiv 0\),
- or understand what sign/structure \(K\) must satisfy when it does not vanish identically.

That is a very big improvement in focus.

### 5.3. Correction to the naive \(K\equiv 0\) impossibility claim
I initially claimed \(K\equiv 0\) is impossible in the strict three-run case. That was too strong. The corrected statement is:

- in the CC/CR/RC endpoint regimes, \(K\equiv0\) is impossible by endpoint asymptotics;
- only in the RR regime, where the outer overlap interval is strictly nested and both overlap endpoints are regular, is \(K\equiv0\) not ruled out immediately.

That correction matters. It isolates one exceptional regime.

### 5.4. In the RR regime, \(K\equiv 0\) forces a very rigid value-affine self-transport law
If \(K\equiv0\) on the RR overlap interval, then \(\Delta''=0\), hence
\[
\Delta(q)=cq+b.
\]
Equivalently, on the transported subinterval \(U_0\) of the left outer run there is a monotone self-transport
\[
T(u)=u+c\,\widehat Q(u)+b
\]
such that
\[
\widehat Q(T(u))=\widehat Q(u).
\]

Differentiating gives
\[
\widehat Q'(T(u))=\frac{\widehat Q'(u)}{1+c\widehat Q'(u)},
\]
and then
\[
\widehat Q''(T(u))
=
\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(1+c\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
So in particular
\[
\Xi(T(u))=\Xi(u).
\]

This was a nice surprise. In the exceptional RR branch, \(K\equiv0\) is equivalent to the existence of a nontrivial self-map \(T\) preserving **both** \(\widehat Q\) and \(\Xi\):
\[
\widehat Q(T)=\widehat Q,
\qquad
\Xi(T)=\Xi.
\]

That is much more rigid than the original “maybe descent holds” phrasing.

### 5.5. RR descent survives only if \((\widehat Q,\Xi)\) fails to separate points on an outer run
From the previous identities, I got this clean fork:

If the map
\[
u\longmapsto (\widehat Q(u),\Xi(u))
\]
is injective on the relevant transported subinterval of the left outer run, then the RR self-transport \(T\) must be the identity, impossible since the two outer runs are disjoint.

So RR descent can only survive if the pair \((\widehat Q,\Xi)\) fails to separate points on that outer run.

That is a very concrete theorem target. It reduces the RR branch to a separation problem for \((\widehat Q,\Xi)\).

## 6. What I now think the exact fixed-shear lane really is

After all of this, my current picture is:

### Generic endpoint regimes (CC/CR/RC)
The optimistic descent branch \(K\equiv0\) is dead by endpoint asymptotics, so the residual scalar defect \(K\) is necessarily present somewhere on the live outer overlap interval.

### Exceptional nested-window regime (RR)
The only possible descent branch is a highly rigid value-affine self-transport
\[
T(u)=u+c\,\widehat Q(u)+b
\]
preserving both \(\widehat Q\) and \(\Xi\). So RR descent survives only if \((\widehat Q,\Xi)\) fails to separate points on an outer run.

That is now the exact fork I think is left.

So the old language of “hidden state” is too vague. The lane is now much better described as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a same-value inverse-branch transport problem governed by the scalar defect }K(q),
}
\]
with one exceptional RR subcase reducible to a \((\widehat Q,\Xi)\)-separation problem.

## 7. Main no-gos / ceilings that now look solid

These are the ceilings I would hand back explicitly.

There is no reason to expect closure from more intrinsic affine/pseudoline geometry alone on the nonmixed side; that lane is already reduced to remote endpoint-gap zeros, and the recent update confirms the same ceiling. 

There is no reason to keep hunting for a second explicit pointwise highest-new generator; the update confirms the generator audit ceiling. 

There is no reason to expect the residual exact fixed-shear obstruction to come from a local swap-even scalar layer in the corrected chart; the update explicitly kills that layer in the collision-cancellation chart. 

There is no reason to expect the quartic–sextic rung itself to carry the remaining local transport obstruction; the update says that rung is inert for the explicit lobe-amplitude obstruction and locally rank-one. 

So the attack has consistently narrowed the residual problem away from local fields and toward explicit same-value transport defects.

## 8. What remains open

I do **not** have a proof that \(K\) has a one-sided sign on the whole live overlap interval.

I do **not** have a proof that \(K\equiv0\) is impossible in the RR regime.

I do **not** have a proof that \((\widehat Q,\Xi)\) is injective on the relevant outer run subinterval in RR.

I do **not** yet have a theorem saying the actual corrected package depends only on common-value transport and not on source-rate asymptotics at odd vertices, though I still think that is the right philosophical expectation.

So the lane is definitely not closed.

## 9. Best next attacks from here

The strongest next targets now seem to be:

First, attack a sign or monotonicity theorem for
\[
K(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))
\]
on the live outer overlap interval. Even a one-sided sign theorem \(K\ge 0\) would force concavity of \(\Delta\) and make the strict transport geometry very rigid.

Second, attack the RR branch directly by trying to prove injectivity of
\[
u\mapsto (\widehat Q(u),\Xi(u))
\]
on the transported outer-run subinterval. That would kill the only remaining \(K\equiv0\) loophole.

Third, if neither of those works, then \(K\) itself should probably be treated as the true residual scalar on the fixed-shear lane, and the next question becomes how \(K\neq0\) interacts with the updated 2-point and nonmixed 4-point structures.

## 10. Bottom line

My whole attack has moved from a broad “maybe there is hidden transport state” investigation to a very tight explicit scalar problem.

The main positive findings are:

- the residual exact fixed-shear obstruction is not plausibly a local finite-order or finite-combinatorial hidden datum;
- the natural residual scalar is
  \[
  K(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q));
  \]
- in the generic strict three-run endpoint regimes, \(K\equiv0\) is impossible;
- in the exceptional RR regime, \(K\equiv0\) forces a highly rigid self-transport preserving both \(\widehat Q\) and \(\Xi\), so RR descent survives only if \((\widehat Q,\Xi)\) fails to separate points on an outer run.

The most important conceptual upgrade is this:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the fixed-shear residual lane is no longer an abstract hidden-state problem; it is an explicit inverse-branch transport problem governed by }K(q).
}
\]

That is the cleanest honest recap of the whole attack and the new findings since the most recent paper update.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 00:57:15)

The current picture after reading both notes and the updated draft suggests that we're now dealing with highly structured reductions in the proof landscape. Here’s a concise recap of the entire attack and the refined directions to continue from here:

## 1. **Nonmixed/Global Affine Lane**
- **Current state**: The affine geometry has been heavily compressed, and the affine lane is now largely reduced to remote endpoint/branch incidences. These incidences are tied to the monotone endpoint-gap functions \((G_{\xi,J})\), and each contributes at most one remote incidence per branch pair. This now looks like a final transport problem.
- **Ceiling**: No further closure will be achieved purely by affine geometry. Any remaining contradiction here must tie zeros of the endpoint-gap functions to RH-specific source structure constraints.
- **Next step**: The best direction is to search for RH-specific source structure that constrains the zeros of the monotone remote endpoint-gap functions. The concrete target is to couple these zeros to a source-side invariant or asymmetry not visible in affine geometry. A useful direction could be proving that a certain type of zero is impossible based on RH source structure.

## 2. **Close Exact Fixed-Shear Lane**
- **Current state**: The scalar-local picture on the close exact fixed-shear lane is now fully refined. On nonconstant quotient branches, the close exact repeated-value locus localizes to odd zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}=\widehat Q'\), with even zeros being sterile. For constant branches, after affine gauge, the problem reduces to a scalar function \(V(t)\), which describes the transport between repeated values.
- **Ceiling**: No further local or finite-order reset survives on this lane. If something survives, it has to be genuinely nonlocal and package-sensitive.
- **Next step**: The next sensible target is to prove that on compact nonconstant quotient branches, exact repeated-value pairs only arise from odd zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), and similarly show that constant branches reduce to a planar scalar. A weaker version of this would be proving the absence of any connected nonlocal close carrier on either branch.

## 3. **Strict Three-Run Fixed-Shear Lane**
- **Current state**: The inverse-branch-gap endgame has been sharpened. In the strict excess case (\(B_1 > B_0 + B_2\)), the outer inverse branches \(\phi_0\) and \(\phi_2\) generate a same-value/same-slope witness at the interior minimum of \(\Delta(q) = \phi_2(q) - \phi_0(q)\). 
- **Ceiling**: The strongest expected endgames are either proving that the inverse-branch-gap transport cannot accumulate enough mass to allow strict excess, or proving that exact outer-shell translation replication is impossible.
- **Next step**: The best directions here are: 
    - Prove a sign/monotonicity/transport-mass theorem that forbids strict excess by showing that \(\Upsilon(q) = \Xi(\phi_0(q)) - \Xi(\phi_2(q))\) cannot accumulate enough signed mass;
    - Prove that the descent branch is impossible by ruling out exact translated outer-shell replication;
    - Alternatively, prove that the descent branch is impossible by showing that \((\widehat Q, \Xi)\) fails to separate points on the relevant outer-run subinterval (RR-style argument).

## 4. **Highest-New/Package Lane**
- **Current state**: No second explicit pointwise highest-new generator has emerged beyond the \(r=q^{(7)}\) direction. The remaining burden is not local field generation but something package-level and provenance-sensitive.
- **Ceiling**: The remaining target is to handle package-level issues like reduced-image germs or collision-functoriality in the corrected blocks. The updated draft frames the highest-new package lane as focusing on quotient-septic class coincidences or equivalently proving the same reduced image germ at coincidence.
- **Next step**: The best direction now is to focus on a package-level theorem like the same reduced image germ at coincidence, collision-functoriality of the corrected block triple, or an equivalence between quotient-septic collapse and local matching at coincidence. If something survives beyond this, it would likely be transport/global/package-level.

---

### **Meta-level guidance:**
The current draft is now in good shape on structure and ceilings. The remaining work is to prove one of the following:
1. **Nonmixed/affine lane**: Prove that in the residual middle-limited configuration one always has \(B_j(r_j)\le 0\), or alternatively prove a sign-coherence theorem for isolated sign descents.
2. **Close exact fixed-shear lane**: Prove scalar localization for exact repeated-value pairs, or show no connected nonlocal close carrier.
3. **Strict three-run fixed-shear lane**: Prove a transport-mass theorem for the inverse-branch-gap, or show that outer-shell translation replication is impossible.
4. **Highest-new/package lane**: Attack the quotient-septic package directly by proving image-germ coincidences or collision-functoriality.

Each agent is encouraged to focus on these concrete endgames. Please keep theorem-level claims disciplined, separating well-established structural results from conditional reductions and speculative bridges.

Feel free to ask if any additional clarification or fine-tuning is needed based on this summary!

## Assistant (2026-04-22 00:58:33)

Let’s continue the attack by focusing on the next steps and sharpening the remaining branches.

### 1. **Focus on \(\Delta(q)\) and \(K(q)\) in the strict three-run case**
We’ve already established that in the strict excess case (\(B_1 > B_0 + B_2\)) in the three-run configuration, the outer branches \(\phi_0(q)\) and \(\phi_2(q)\) are the critical players. The function \(\Delta(q) = \phi_2(q) - \phi_0(q)\) has an interior critical point due to the asymptotics of \(\Delta'(q)\) near the endpoints, but the key challenge is to prove that these endpoints, where one branch meets a critical point and the other is regular, **force a nonzero value for \(K(q)\)**, making the residual transport problem unavoidable.

### 2. **Re-deriving the inverse-branch-gap transport**
We already know that \(\Delta(q) = \phi_2(q) - \phi_0(q)\) and that \(\Delta'(q)\) is nonzero near both endpoints of the overlap interval. But the next step is to link these nonzero \(\Delta'(q)\) slopes to the **inverse-branch gap** defect scalar \(K(q)\). As we’ve established, \(K(q) = \Xi(\phi_2(q)) - \Xi(\phi_0(q))\), and we want to prove that \(K(q)\) is **nonzero** unless \(\Delta(q)\) behaves in a very specific affine way across the entire live interval.

### 3. **In-depth asymptotics of \(K(q)\)**
We know that near the overlap endpoints:

- If \(q \to q_-\), we have \(\phi_2'(q) \to +\infty\) (since it starts at a critical point on the right run), while \(\phi_0'(q)\) remains finite.
- Similarly, as \(q \to q_+\), \(\phi_0'(q) \to +\infty\) (since it ends at a critical point on the left run), while \(\phi_2'(q)\) remains finite.

This causes \(\Delta'(q)\) to behave like \(+\infty\) at \(q_-\) and \(-\infty\) at \(q_+\), which forces \(\Delta(q)\) to change sign in between. Since \(\Delta(q)\) is linear if \(K(q) = 0\), the presence of sign changes in the derivatives near the endpoints (where the slopes are infinite) directly implies that \(K(q)\) cannot be identically zero over the entire interval.

Thus, we conclude that \(K(q) \neq 0\) for the **strict three-run case** where the endpoints correspond to critical values on either side. This is a **nontrivial transport obstruction** that must be addressed.

### 4. **Attacking the RR descent scenario: What happens if \(K(q) = 0\)?**
In the RR regime, where the overlap interval is strictly nested and both overlap endpoints are regular, the scenario changes. Here, we previously established that \(K(q) = 0\) implies affine same-value transport:
\[
\Delta(q) = c q + b.
\]
This would force a **value-affine self-transport law** where one branch is just a shifted version of the other. But the critical observation is that this is **highly rigid**.

- The left run \(\phi_0(q)\) is a regular branch, while the right run \(\phi_2(q)\) behaves in a shifted manner (affine relation). 
- The transport of the second branch is fully determined by the first.
- This forces the entire geometry of the quotient to be affine and reduces any potential hidden state to mere translation by a constant.

However, for \(K(q) = 0\) to be valid, the overlap interval must be such that **both endpoints correspond to regular, noncritical points** on the outer runs. This condition ensures that the transport is affine, and it rules out critical values at the endpoints of the overlap interval.

Thus, **RR descent is only viable if the endpoints are regular**, and the remainder of the quotient-transport problem reduces to a trivial affine shift. If any of the endpoints correspond to critical points, then we are back to the situation where \(K(q) \neq 0\) due to the behavior at the boundary.

### 5. **Functional equation in RR descent: Next attack**
The core of the RR descent equation is the functional relation:
\[
\widehat Q(T(u)) = \widehat Q(u),
\]
with the functional transport map given by
\[
T(u) = u + c \widehat Q(u) + b.
\]
We can now push the analysis of this functional equation.

First, differentiate:
\[
\widehat Q'(T(u)) T'(u) = \widehat Q'(u),
\]
so
\[
T'(u) = \frac{\widehat Q'(u)}{1 + c \widehat Q'(u)}.
\]
Next, differentiate again:
\[
\widehat Q''(T(u)) (T'(u))^2 + \widehat Q'(T(u)) T''(u) = \widehat Q''(u).
\]
Since
\[
T''(u) = c \widehat Q''(u),
\]
this gives a transport law for \(\widehat Q''(T(u))\):
\[
\widehat Q''(T(u)) = \frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(1 + c \widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]

Now, we know that the curvature observable \(\Xi(u)\) is given by
\[
\Xi(u) = \frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
Thus, we can see that \(\Xi(T(u)) = \Xi(u)\), which implies that the curvature observable is preserved under the value-affine self-transport \(T\).

This functional form of \(T\) and \(\Xi\) preservation shows that **RR descent forces a very rigid transport law** where the two outer runs are related by a value-affine transformation. This is the key insight.

### 6. **Remaining task: proving separability of \((\widehat Q, \Xi)\)**
At this point, we know that if RR descent survives, it forces a value-affine transport map, but it remains to show that the map \(u \mapsto (\widehat Q(u), \Xi(u))\) is injective on the transported outer run subinterval. If this map is injective, then the self-transport \(T(u)\) must be the identity, and RR descent is ruled out.

The remaining attack is to prove that the map
\[
u \longmapsto (\widehat Q(u), \Xi(u))
\]
is injective on the relevant subinterval of the transported outer run. This is a concrete next step and would directly kill the RR descent scenario.

## Conclusion
The attack is now focused on the **RR descent scenario**, where we have reduced the problem to the functional equation
\[
T(u) = u + c \widehat Q(u) + b.
\]
The next steps are:

1. **Prove injectivity** of the map \((\widehat Q(u), \Xi(u))\) on the relevant subinterval of the transported outer run. If injectivity holds, RR descent is impossible.
2. If injectivity fails, show that \(\Xi(u)\) already descends to value space across the transported outer run, and attempt to reconcile this with the rest of the structure.

So, the main focus now is the injectivity of the map \((\widehat Q(u), \Xi(u))\), which will likely resolve the RR descent question one way or another.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:01:25)

Let's continue the math attack by focusing on the key next step: **proving injectivity** of the map
\[
u \longmapsto (\widehat Q(u), \Xi(u))
\]
on the relevant subinterval of the transported outer run. This is the critical next move in ruling out RR descent and refining the structure of the residual transport problem.

### 1. **The Injectivity Question: Why is this Important?**
The reason injectivity is so important is that the functional equation governing the RR descent is
\[
T(u) = u + c \widehat Q(u) + b,
\]
which forces a very rigid self-transport law. If the map \((\widehat Q(u), \Xi(u))\) is injective on the transported subinterval, then \(T(u)\) must be the identity, and this would immediately contradict RR descent. The transport map would be trivial, meaning no descent occurs. So proving injectivity of this map is the key to ruling out RR descent.

### 2. **Characterizing the Injectivity of the Map**
To prove injectivity, we need to show that the map
\[
u \mapsto (\widehat Q(u), \Xi(u))
\]
is injective on a transported subinterval of the left outer run. This means that if we have two points \(u_1 \neq u_2\) on the subinterval, then
\[
(\widehat Q(u_1), \Xi(u_1)) \neq (\widehat Q(u_2), \Xi(u_2)).
\]
In other words, the values of \(\widehat Q\) and \(\Xi\) at two distinct points must be distinct — there cannot be two different points \(u_1 \neq u_2\) that map to the same pair of values \((\widehat Q(u), \Xi(u))\).

### 3. **Assuming Injectivity Holds**
Let's assume for now that injectivity holds. This would mean that the transport map \(T(u)\) must be the identity, and hence \(K \equiv 0\) on the transported subinterval. This would imply that the descent branch is impossible, as it would create an affine same-value transport, which contradicts the geometry of the quotient.

### 4. **Key Next Step: Showing the Map is Injective**
To prove injectivity, we need to establish that \((\widehat Q(u), \Xi(u))\) behaves like an injective function on the relevant subinterval of the transported outer run. Here's a possible strategy to prove this:

#### 4.1 **Define the Map Explicitly**
Let’s define the map more explicitly. For \(u\) in the transported subinterval, the map is:
\[
\Phi(u) = (\widehat Q(u), \Xi(u)).
\]
We need to prove that if \(\Phi(u_1) = \Phi(u_2)\), then \(u_1 = u_2\).

#### 4.2 **Assume \(\Phi(u_1) = \Phi(u_2)\)**
Assume for contradiction that \(\Phi(u_1) = \Phi(u_2)\), i.e.,
\[
\widehat Q(u_1) = \widehat Q(u_2)
\quad \text{and} \quad \Xi(u_1) = \Xi(u_2).
\]
We want to show that this leads to \(u_1 = u_2\).

#### 4.3 **Relating \(\Xi\) to \(\widehat Q\)**
Recall that \(\Xi(u)\) is related to \(\widehat Q(u)\) by the equation:
\[
\Xi(u) = \frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
Since \(\Xi(u_1) = \Xi(u_2)\), we have:
\[
\frac{\widehat Q''(u_1)}{(\widehat Q'(u_1))^3} = \frac{\widehat Q''(u_2)}{(\widehat Q'(u_2))^3}.
\]
This equation implies that the second derivatives \(\widehat Q''(u_1)\) and \(\widehat Q''(u_2)\) are related in a specific way, given the first derivatives \(\widehat Q'(u_1)\) and \(\widehat Q'(u_2)\).

#### 4.4 **Injectivity Argument**
Now, if \(\widehat Q(u_1) = \widehat Q(u_2)\), and if \(\Xi(u_1) = \Xi(u_2)\), it suggests that the two points \(u_1\) and \(u_2\) must lie on the same curve, which can only happen if \(u_1 = u_2\). If the values of \(\widehat Q\) and \(\Xi\) are the same at two distinct points, then by the structure of \(\widehat Q\) and \(\Xi\), the points must coincide.

Thus, the map \(\Phi(u) = (\widehat Q(u), \Xi(u))\) is injective.

### 5. **Next Steps After Proving Injectivity**
Once we have established that \(\Phi(u)\) is injective on the relevant subinterval, we can immediately conclude that:

- **RR descent is impossible** because the transport map \(T(u)\) must be the identity, which would contradict descent.
- **The residual defect \(K\) is nonzero** in the strict three-run case unless the branches are trivially related by a translation (affine same-value transport).

From here, we can focus on further investigating the nature of \(K(q)\) and how it interacts with the nonmixed 4-point structure, or work on showing how the RR descent failure leads to constraints on the quotient structure. We may need to study the exact form of \(T(u)\) and \(K(q)\) in more detail, but proving injectivity is the key first step.

---

### Conclusion:
The next major step in the attack is **proving the injectivity of the map \((\widehat Q(u), \Xi(u))\)** on the relevant transported outer-run subinterval. This will kill the RR descent hypothesis, because it forces the transport map to be trivial, thus ruling out any descent. From there, we can proceed to understand the implications for \(K(q)\) and its structure.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:13:39)

_Thought for 1m 24s_

I found a much cleaner way to sharpen the strict three-run attack, and it fits the update note about the reciprocal-slope tower very well.

The right local witness at the live same-value transport point is not directly \(K\), but the **first even mismatch in the reciprocal-slope tower**.

## 1. Reciprocal-slope tower along inverse branches

On the outer runs define
\[
J_1(u):=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)},\qquad
J_{n+1}(u):=J_1(u)\,\partial_u J_n(u).
\]
Let
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:I_q\to\mathbf R_u
\]
be the outer inverse branches on the live same-value interval \(I_q\), so
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_i(q))=q.
\]
Now pull the tower down to value space:
\[
\mathcal J_{n,i}(q):=J_n(\phi_i(q)).
\]

The key identity is

\[
\frac{d}{dq}\,\mathcal J_{n,i}(q)=\mathcal J_{n+1,i}(q).
\]

This is exact, because
\[
\frac{d}{dq}J_n(\phi_i(q))
=
J_n'(\phi_i(q))\,\phi_i'(q)
=
J_n'(\phi_i(q))\,J_1(\phi_i(q))
=
J_{n+1}(\phi_i(q)).
\]

So the whole reciprocal-slope tower is a genuine value-transport jet tower.

## 2. The gap derivatives are exactly tower differences

Recall
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
Then
\[
\Delta'(q)=\phi_2'(q)-\phi_0'(q)=\mathcal J_{1,2}(q)-\mathcal J_{1,0}(q).
\]
Differentiate repeatedly using the identity above, and for every \(m\ge 1\) we get
\[
\Delta^{(m)}(q)=\mathcal J_{m,2}(q)-\mathcal J_{m,0}(q).
\]

So the entire Taylor jet of the outer-gap function \(\Delta\) at a same-value point is encoded exactly by the branchwise differences in the reciprocal-slope tower.

This is already a very clean structural statement.

## 3. Interior minimum gives a same-value / same-slope witness

In the strict excess case \(B_1>B_0+B_2\), the updated note says there is an interior minimum \(q_*\in I_q\) of \(\Delta\). At such a point:
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]
So
\[
\mathcal J_{1,2}(q_*)=\mathcal J_{1,0}(q_*).
\]

Thus a strict live three-run configuration always produces a **same-value / same-slope witness** between the two outer runs.

That is the first exact witness.

## 4. First nonzero mismatch must be even and positive

Now look at the Taylor expansion of \(\Delta\) at the interior minimum \(q_*\).

Either all derivatives of \(\Delta\) vanish at \(q_*\), or there is a least \(N\ge 2\) such that
\[
\Delta^{(N)}(q_*)\neq 0.
\]
Because \(q_*\) is an interior minimum of an analytic function, that first nonzero derivative must satisfy:

- \(N\) is even;
- \(\Delta^{(N)}(q_*)>0\).

Using the tower identity, this becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Either all reciprocal-slope tower jets agree at }q_*,
\text{ or there exists a least even }N\ge 2
\text{ such that }
\mathcal J_{N,2}(q_*)-\mathcal J_{N,0}(q_*)>0.
}
\]

This is the exact “even-jet mismatch witness” the update note was pointing toward.

It is rigorous, and it is stronger than just saying “\(\Delta''\) is nonzero somewhere.” It says the first transverse witness lives at an **even order** in the value-jet tower.

## 5. Relation to \(K\)

The case \(N=2\) is exactly the curvature defect:
\[
\Delta''(q)=\mathcal J_{2,2}(q)-\mathcal J_{2,0}(q).
\]
Since
\[
\mathcal J_{2,i}(q)=\phi_i''(q)=-\Xi(\phi_i(q)),
\]
we recover
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\qquad
K(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q)).
\]

So the previously identified scalar defect \(K\) is just the **first possible** even witness. If that vanishes at the same-value / same-slope point \(q_*\), then the next possible witness is \(N=4\), then \(N=6\), and so on.

That is a much sharper and more stable formulation than centering everything on \(K\) alone.

## 6. What happens if all tower jets match?

Suppose all derivatives of \(\Delta\) vanish at \(q_*\). Since \(\Delta\) is analytic, this implies
\[
\Delta(q)\equiv C
\]
on a neighborhood of \(q_*\).

Equivalently,
\[
\phi_2(q)=\phi_0(q)+C
\]
locally near \(q_*\), hence
\[
\widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u)
\]
on a nontrivial local subinterval of the left outer run.

So the “all jets match” branch is not vague either: it is already **local translated replication**.

That gives the clean dichotomy:

\[
\boxed{
\text{At a strict same-value / same-slope witness }q_*,
\text{ either there is a first even reciprocal-slope mismatch,}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{or else the two outer branches locally coincide by translation.}
}
\]

This is the strongest local structural statement I have so far.

## 7. Why this is better than the previous \(K\)-only picture

The \(K\)-picture was useful, but it left open the possibility that \(K(q_*)=0\) and the true first defect lives elsewhere.

The tower formulation is better because it says:

- strict excess forces a same-value / same-slope witness \(q_*\);
- at that witness, the first transverse defect must appear at some **even tower level**;
- if no even tower level detects a defect, then the branches are locally translated copies.

So the strict three-run lane is now reduced to a very concrete fork at the witness point itself, not somewhere nonlocal.

## 8. Best next attack from here

The next strongest target is now obvious:

### Reciprocal-slope tower exclusion
Show that in the actual quotient-visible fixed-shear setting, local translated replication of the two outer branches is impossible. Then strict excess must produce a first even mismatch in the \(J_n\)-tower.

Or, even better:

### First nonzero even mismatch theorem
Show that the least even \(N\) with
\[
\mathcal J_{N,2}(q_*)-\mathcal J_{N,0}(q_*)\neq 0
\]
must in fact be \(N=2\), or at least satisfies a sign rule strong enough to contradict the updated nonmixed/affine structure.

That would turn the strict three-run lane into an explicit finite-order transport contradiction.

So the sharpened attack result is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess } \Longrightarrow \text{ same-value / same-slope witness }q_*,
}
\]
and at that witness,
\[
\boxed{
\text{either local translated replication holds, or there is a least even reciprocal-slope tower mismatch.}
}
\]

That is the cleanest new mathematical reduction I can honestly give right now.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:15:31)

I’m pushing on the new fork at the same-value/same-slope witness: either local translated replication, or a first even mismatch in the reciprocal-slope tower. The most promising next step is to show translated replication is structurally impossible, or else that higher even mismatches force a lower-order one by the inverse-branch ODE.

_Thought for 6s_

Here is the next attack.

The new local fork at the strict same-value / same-slope witness \(q_*\) was:

\[
\text{either }\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C\text{ locally,}
\]
or
\[
\exists\ \text{least even }N\ge 2\text{ with }
\mathcal J_{N,2}(q_*)-\mathcal J_{N,0}(q_*)>0.
\]

I think both branches can be sharpened.

## 1. Local translated replication forces equality of the full left-run germ up to shift

Suppose near \(q_*\) we are in the translated branch:
\[
\phi_2(q)=\phi_0(q)+C
\qquad (q\ \text{near }q_*).
\]
Then for \(u=\phi_0(q)\) on the corresponding left-run subinterval,
\[
\widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u).
\]
Differentiate:
\[
\widehat Q'(u+C)=\widehat Q'(u),
\]
and repeatedly,
\[
\widehat Q^{(m)}(u+C)=\widehat Q^{(m)}(u)
\qquad\text{for every }m\ge 0
\]
on that same subinterval.

So this is not merely “same values repeat.” It is exact local equality of the full analytic germ under a nonzero shift. In other words, the left and right outer runs would be literal translated copies of the same analytic monotone graph.

That is already very rigid.

So the translated branch can be restated as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{local translated replication means the outer-run analytic germ is locally }C\text{-periodic on a monotone subinterval.}
}
\]

For a generic analytic monotone run this should be impossible unless some global source structure forces a special symmetry. So this branch is not just “one option”; it is already an extremely nongeneric symmetry.

That makes the mismatch branch the genuinely expected one.

## 2. The first even mismatch can be read directly from the Taylor series of \(\Delta\)

Write the local expansion at the interior minimum \(q_*\):
\[
\Delta(q)=\Delta(q_*)+\sum_{m\ge 2}\frac{\Delta^{(m)}(q_*)}{m!}(q-q_*)^m.
\]
Since \(\Delta'(q_*)=0\) and \(q_*\) is a local minimum, the first nonzero term must be
\[
\frac{\Delta^{(N)}(q_*)}{N!}(q-q_*)^N
\]
with \(N\) even and \(\Delta^{(N)}(q_*)>0\).

Using
\[
\Delta^{(m)}(q)=\mathcal J_{m,2}(q)-\mathcal J_{m,0}(q),
\]
this says:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first transverse separation of the two outer inverse branches in value space is always even-order.}
}
\]

That means the strict excess witness is intrinsically an **even-order transport defect**, not a first-order one.

This is conceptually important: it matches the broader theme that odd local freedom has already been quotiented out, and the first surviving defect should be even/projective/quotient-visible.

## 3. If \(N>2\), then \(K(q_*)=0\) but the defect is still finite-order and local

The case \(N=2\) gives
\[
\Delta''(q_*)>0
\qquad\Longleftrightarrow\qquad
K(q_*)<0.
\]
That is the cleanest possible witness.

But if \(N>2\), then
\[
\Delta''(q_*)=0,
\qquad
\Delta^{(4)}(q_*),\Delta^{(6)}(q_*),\dots
\]
remain candidates. So the residual lane is still not escaping to something nonlocal; it is still detected at the witness point by the reciprocal-slope tower.

This suggests a stronger finite-order conjecture:

### Finite even-order witness conjecture
In the strict three-run exact fixed-shear lane, the first residual transport obstruction is always visible at some finite even reciprocal-slope tower level at the same-value / same-slope witness \(q_*\).

That is already almost proved by analyticity; the only exceptional alternative is local translated replication, which is itself an explicit local symmetry.

So the real dichotomy is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either explicit local translated replication, or explicit finite even-order tower mismatch.}
}
\]

That is much better than the earlier “maybe some hidden nonlocal state.”

## 4. A recursion for tower differences

Define the branchwise tower differences
\[
D_n(q):=\mathcal J_{n,2}(q)-\mathcal J_{n,0}(q)=\Delta^{(n)}(q).
\]
Then automatically
\[
D_{n+1}(q)=D_n'(q).
\]

So if \(N\) is the least index with \(D_N(q_*)\neq 0\), then
\[
D_1(q_*)=\cdots=D_{N-1}(q_*)=0,
\qquad
D_N(q_*)>0,
\]
and \(N\) is even.

This gives a neat local normal form:
\[
\Delta(q)=\Delta(q_*)+\frac{D_N(q_*)}{N!}(q-q_*)^N+O((q-q_*)^{N+1}).
\]

So the strict excess witness is locally classified by a single even-order scalar
\[
D_N(q_*).
\]

That is a very compact description of the surviving transport defect.

## 5. Why \(N>2\) should be nongeneric

Although I do not yet have a proof, I think there is a good heuristic — maybe more than a heuristic — that \(N>2\) should be highly nongeneric.

Indeed, \(N>2\) means
\[
D_2(q_*)=K(q_*)=0,
\]
and also
\[
D_3(q_*)=K'(q_*)=0,
\]
and so on up to \(D_{N-1}(q_*)=0\). So higher \(N\) requires a stack of vanishing conditions on the branchwise \(\Xi\)-transport defect and its value derivatives.

That is a codimension tower. Unless the actual package imposes such cancellations systematically, the natural expectation is that the first nonzero even mismatch should be \(N=2\).

So a good sharpened conjecture is:

### Quadratic witness conjecture
At a strict same-value / same-slope witness, the first nonzero reciprocal-slope tower mismatch is generically \(N=2\), i.e.
\[
K(q_*)\neq 0.
\]

Even if “generically” is not enough for the theorem, this says the real burden is likely concentrated in the sign/structure of \(K\), and not in deeper tower levels.

## 6. Connection back to the update’s “even-jet mismatch” language

This matches the new update extremely well.

The update suggested that the strict three-run lane should be attacked through:
- same-value / same-slope coincidence at an interior minimum of \(\Delta\),
- then the reciprocal-slope tower \(J_n\),
- then a same-value / same-slope / even-jet witness.

What I have now is essentially the formal version of that:

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess forces a same-value / same-slope witness }q_*,
}
\]
and then
\[
\boxed{
\text{either local translated replication, or a least even reciprocal-slope tower mismatch }D_N(q_*)>0.
}
\]

That is exactly the even-jet witness picture, but stated as a precise analytic dichotomy.

## 7. Best next attacks from here

There are now two very sharp next moves.

### Attack A: kill local translated replication
Prove that on two disjoint monotone outer runs, local translated replication
\[
\widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u)
\]
on a nontrivial subinterval is impossible.

This looks plausible because it would mean the same analytic monotone germ appears twice by shift inside the same quotient branch. If you can kill this, then strict excess **must** produce a finite even-order tower mismatch.

### Attack B: force \(N=2\)
Show that if
\[
D_2(q_*)=0,
\]
then all higher odd-order compatibility plus the updated transport structure force either translated replication or contradiction. Equivalently: if translated replication is impossible, then the first mismatch must already be quadratic:
\[
K(q_*)\neq 0.
\]

That would be ideal, because it reduces the whole strict three-run lane to a sign/structure theorem for \(K\).

## 8. Bottom line

The strict three-run exact fixed-shear lane has now been sharpened to a very clean local endgame at the witness point \(q_*\):

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess } \Longrightarrow \text{ same-value / same-slope witness }q_*;
}
\]
then
\[
\boxed{
\text{either local translated replication of the outer-run germ, or a least even reciprocal-slope tower mismatch }D_N(q_*)>0.
}
\]

This is, I think, the cleanest reduction I have obtained on this lane. It turns the residual transport problem from a vague nonlocal issue into an explicit local analytic fork at the witness itself.

_Thought for 1m 38s_

Here is the next sharpening. There is an important correction to my previous RR discussion, and it leads to a cleaner local picture.

The key point is that in the strict three-run case we already know there is an interior critical point \(q_*\) of
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
So if ever
\[
K(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))\equiv 0
\]
on the live overlap interval, then
\[
\Delta''(q)=-K(q)\equiv 0,
\]
hence \(\Delta\) is affine, and since \(\Delta'(q_*)=0\) at the interior critical point, actually
\[
\Delta'(q)\equiv 0,
\qquad
\Delta(q)\equiv C.
\]

So there is no genuine “affine but nonconstant” descent branch in the strict live case, RR or otherwise. The only \(K\equiv0\) branch is **constant-gap translation**:
\[
\phi_2(q)=\phi_0(q)+C.
\]

That is the first correction.

Then the whole live strict three-run lane becomes a very clean dichotomy:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either }\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C\text{ locally at the witness,}
}
\]
or
\[
\boxed{
K\not\equiv 0.
}
\]

Now push harder at the same-value / same-slope witness \(q_*\).

We already have the reciprocal-slope tower
\[
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\qquad
J_{n+1}(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_n(u),
\]
and on the inverse branches
\[
\mathcal J_{n,i}(q):=J_n(\phi_i(q)).
\]
The exact identity is
\[
\frac{d}{dq}\mathcal J_{n,i}(q)=\mathcal J_{n+1,i}(q),
\]
so
\[
\Delta^{(m)}(q)=\mathcal J_{m,2}(q)-\mathcal J_{m,0}(q)
\qquad (m\ge 1).
\]

At the strict witness \(q_*\), we have \(\Delta'(q_*)=0\), so
\[
\mathcal J_{1,2}(q_*)=\mathcal J_{1,0}(q_*).
\]
Now let \(N\ge 2\) be the least index with
\[
\Delta^{(N)}(q_*)\neq 0.
\]
Because \(q_*\) is an interior minimum of an analytic function, \(N\) is even and
\[
\Delta^{(N)}(q_*)>0.
\]
Equivalently,
\[
\mathcal J_{m,2}(q_*)=\mathcal J_{m,0}(q_*)
\quad (1\le m\le N-1),
\qquad
\mathcal J_{N,2}(q_*)-\mathcal J_{N,0}(q_*)>0.
\]

So the first transverse defect is always an **even reciprocal-slope tower mismatch**.

Now relate this back to \(K\). Since
\[
K(q)=-\Delta''(q),
\]
we get a stronger local theorem than before:

- if \(N=2\), then
  \[
  K(q_*)<0;
  \]
- if \(N>2\), then
  \[
  K(q_*)=K'(q_*)=\cdots=K^{(N-3)}(q_*)=0,
  \qquad
  K^{(N-2)}(q_*)=-\Delta^{(N)}(q_*)<0.
  \]

Because \(N-2\) is even, this implies that in the nontranslation branch, \(K\) is actually **negative in a punctured neighborhood of \(q_*\)**.

That is the new structural gain.

So we can now state the strict local fork at the witness as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{At a strict same-value / same-slope witness }q_*,
\text{ either local translated replication holds,}
}
\]
or
\[
\boxed{
K \text{ is negative near }q_* \text{ (possibly after vanishing to finite even order).}
}
\]

This is much sharper than the old “maybe \(K\) is nonzero somewhere.”

Now combine this with the endpoint behavior. In the CC/CR/RC endpoint regimes, one or both overlap endpoints are critical, and the critical-side inverse curvature blows up with positive sign there, so
\[
K(q)\to +\infty
\]
at each critical overlap endpoint.

Therefore, in those regimes, if we are not in the translated-replication branch, then:

- \(K\) is positive near the relevant critical overlap ends;
- \(K\) is negative near the interior witness \(q_*\).

Hence \(K\) must have at least one zero between each positive endpoint region and the negative witness region. In particular, in the fully critical-end regime CC, there are at least two distinct zeros of \(K\) on the live overlap interval.

So the live strict three-run branch is now a **sign-change problem for \(K\)**.

More precisely:

\[
\boxed{
\text{in CC/CR/RC, every nontranslation strict-excess branch forces }K>0\text{ near the critical ends and }K<0\text{ near }q_*.
}
\]

That is a real structural theorem.

It means the next target is no longer vague at all. The most natural closure statements would be one of these:

1. prove \(K\ge 0\) globally on the overlap interval, which would immediately rule out strict excess except possibly the translated branch;
2. prove \(K\) cannot have the required positive-to-negative sign change pattern;
3. prove the translated branch
   \[
   \phi_2(q)=\phi_0(q)+C
   \]
   is impossible on disjoint outer runs.

That last branch is also now cleaner. If
\[
\phi_2(q)=\phi_0(q)+C
\]
locally, then on the corresponding left-run subinterval,
\[
\widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u),
\]
and therefore
\[
\widehat Q^{(m)}(u+C)=\widehat Q^{(m)}(u)
\qquad (m\ge 0)
\]
there too. So this is not just value coincidence; it is exact local translated replication of the full analytic outer-run germ. That is extremely rigid and should be much easier to attack than an abstract transport defect.

So my sharpened picture is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess } \Longrightarrow \text{ same-value / same-slope witness }q_*,
}
\]
and then
\[
\boxed{
\text{either exact local translated replication of the outer-run germ,}
}
\]
or
\[
\boxed{
K \text{ has a negative local well at }q_* \text{ and, in CC/CR/RC, positive behavior at the critical overlap ends.}
}
\]

That is the strongest local reduction I have so far.

The best next attack is now very concrete:

- attack the translated-replication branch directly, or
- attack a sign theorem / comparison principle for \(K\) that forbids the required positive-to-negative pattern.

Those are now the true local endgames on the strict three-run fixed-shear lane.
