_Thought for 1m 53s_

I found a sharper way to turn the same-value / same-slope witness into a real closure theorem, and it lines up very well with the updated draft’s suggested “branch-independent first-order closure law” target.

The right point is this:

At the strict witness \(q_*\), we already know
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0,
\]
so the two outer branches agree in reciprocal slope:
\[
\mathcal J_{1,2}(q_*)=\mathcal J_{1,0}(q_*),
\qquad
\mathcal J_{1,i}(q)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))}.
\]

And we also established the exact tower transport rule
\[
\frac{d}{dq}\mathcal J_{n,i}(q)=\mathcal J_{n+1,i}(q).
\]

So the witness is naturally a Cauchy-data problem in value space.

## 1. Branch-independent closure kills hidden transport immediately

Suppose on the two outer runs there is a **branch-independent first-order closure law**
\[
\widehat Q''(u)=G(\widehat Q(u),\widehat Q'(u)).
\]
Equivalently, since
\[
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\qquad
J_2(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_1(u)=-\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\]
this is exactly a value-space ODE of the form
\[
\frac{d}{dq}\mathcal J_{1,i}(q)
=
F\bigl(q,\mathcal J_{1,i}(q)\bigr)
\]
for some branch-independent function \(F\).

But at the strict witness \(q_*\) we have
\[
\mathcal J_{1,2}(q_*)=\mathcal J_{1,0}(q_*).
\]
So the two outer reciprocal-slope branches solve the **same first-order ODE** with the **same initial value**. By uniqueness,
\[
\mathcal J_{1,2}(q)\equiv \mathcal J_{1,0}(q)
\]
on the whole connected live overlap interval.

Therefore
\[
\Delta'(q)=\mathcal J_{1,2}(q)-\mathcal J_{1,0}(q)\equiv 0,
\]
hence
\[
\Delta(q)\equiv C.
\]

So we get the exact theorem-shaped statement:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{First-order closure theorem.}\quad
\text{If a branch-independent closure law }
\widehat Q''=G(\widehat Q,\widehat Q')
\text{ holds on the outer runs,}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{then strict same-value / same-slope transport forces local translated replication }
\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C.
}
\]

This is very clean. It means the draft’s proposed first-order closure law is not just “one plausible direction.” It is strong enough to collapse the strict three-run lane all the way to translated replication.

So if translated replication can then be excluded independently, strict excess is dead.

That is a real sharpening.

## 2. Finite-dimensional closure gives a bound on the first even mismatch

This extends nicely.

Suppose instead of a scalar first-order closure we only know a branch-independent finite-dimensional closure of size \(m\). Concretely, define the value-space state
\[
Y_i(q):=\bigl(\mathcal J_{1,i}(q),\dots,\mathcal J_{m,i}(q)\bigr),
\]
and suppose it satisfies a branch-independent first-order system
\[
\frac{d}{dq}Y_i(q)=F\bigl(q,Y_i(q)\bigr).
\]

Then if the first \(m\) tower entries agree at some point \(q_*\),
\[
\mathcal J_{k,2}(q_*)=\mathcal J_{k,0}(q_*)
\qquad (1\le k\le m),
\]
uniqueness again gives
\[
Y_2(q)\equiv Y_0(q),
\]
hence the whole tower agrees and we fall into the translated branch.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{under an }m\text{-dimensional branch-independent closure law, a nontranslation strict witness must have its first tower mismatch at some order }N\le m.
}
\]

This is a very useful meta-theorem.

Because from the earlier local fork we already know that at a strict witness there is a least even \(N\ge 2\) such that
\[
\mathcal J_{N,2}(q_*)-\mathcal J_{N,0}(q_*)\neq 0,
\]
unless translated replication holds.

So combining the two statements:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if the branch dynamics close in dimension }m,\text{ then the first even mismatch order is forced to satisfy }N\le m,
}
\]
unless translated replication holds.

This turns the reciprocal-slope tower into a very concrete closure detector.

## 3. Consequences for small closure dimensions

Now specialize.

### Case \(m=1\)
This is exactly the first-order law
\[
\widehat Q''=G(\widehat Q,\widehat Q').
\]
Then strict witness \(+\) same slope at \(q_*\) forces translated replication immediately.

So:

\[
\boxed{
m=1 \Longrightarrow \text{strict three-run transport reduces entirely to the translated branch.}
}
\]

### Case \(m=2\)
Now the state is \((\mathcal J_1,\mathcal J_2)\). If at the witness
\[
\mathcal J_{1,2}(q_*)=\mathcal J_{1,0}(q_*),
\qquad
\mathcal J_{2,2}(q_*)=\mathcal J_{2,0}(q_*),
\]
then uniqueness again forces translated replication.

So in any nontranslation branch under a 2D closure law, the first mismatch must already be at order \(N=2\), i.e.
\[
\mathcal J_{2,2}(q_*)-\mathcal J_{2,0}(q_*)\neq 0.
\]

But
\[
\mathcal J_{2,2}-\mathcal J_{2,0}
=
\Delta''=-K.
\]

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Second-order closure corollary.}\quad
\text{Under any branch-independent 2D closure law, a nontranslation strict witness must satisfy }K(q_*)\neq 0.
}
\]

This is excellent, because it justifies the heuristic “the real burden should be at \(K\)” whenever there is even modest closure.

### General \(m\)
If the first even mismatch order is \(N\), then any closure law of dimension \(<N\) is impossible unless translated replication holds.

So the tower witness gives a lower bound on the dimension of any branch-independent closure law compatible with strict excess.

That is a genuinely new structural result.

## 4. Reinterpretation of the update’s fork

This sharpens the update’s suggested endgames nicely.

The update proposed two kinds of targets:

- prove a branch-independent first-order closure law
  \[
  \widehat Q''(u)=G(\widehat Q(u),\widehat Q'(u)),
  \]
  or
- else accept failure of such descent as the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction.

The tower attack now says exactly why that is the right fork.

If the first-order closure law exists, then strict same-value / same-slope transport immediately collapses to translated replication. If translated replication is separately impossible, the strict three-run lane closes.

If the first-order closure law does not exist, then the first obstruction is already visible as failure of branch-independent descent in the reciprocal-slope tower — concretely in \(K\), or if \(K\) vanishes, at the next even tower level.

So the residual lane is no longer vague at all:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either low-dimensional branch closure exists and forces translation,}
}
\]
or
\[
\boxed{
\text{the first even reciprocal-slope tower mismatch is the explicit residual transport obstruction.}
}
\]

That is a much better-organized endgame.

## 5. A practical new theorem target

The cleanest next theorem target is now:

### Closure-vs-mismatch theorem
At a strict same-value / same-slope witness \(q_*\), exactly one of the following holds:

1. local translated replication:
   \[
   \phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C;
   \]
2. there is no branch-independent 1D closure law on the outer runs;
3. if \(K(q_*)=0\), then there is no branch-independent 2D closure law on the outer runs;
4. more generally, if the first even mismatch order is \(N\), then there is no branch-independent closure law of dimension \(<N\).

This is not yet fully proved as a polished theorem, but the proof is essentially what I wrote above: uniqueness of ODE solutions in value space.

It gives a very explicit way to use the tower witness as a closure detector.

## 6. Why this is better than just “attack \(K\)”

Before, the natural next step looked like “prove a sign law for \(K\)” or “show \((\widehat Q,\Xi)\) separates points.” Those are still good.

But this new reduction is stronger structurally, because it says:

- if the actual package really does have low-dimensional branch-independent transport closure, then the witness point forces immediate collapse to translated replication or to a quadratic defect;
- if not, then the failure of low-dimensional closure is itself the first explicit obstruction.

So the tower witness is not just a diagnostic; it is also a classifier of what kind of closure law would be needed to kill the lane.

That is very useful for deciding where to spend effort.

## 7. Best next attacks from here

I think there are now two particularly sharp moves.

### Attack A: prove/exploit branch-independent first-order closure
Try to show
\[
\widehat Q''=G(\widehat Q,\widehat Q')
\]
on the outer runs, even locally on the transported interval. Then strict transport collapses immediately to translated replication.

### Attack B: if first-order closure is unavailable, attack \(m=2\) closure
Try to show the outer branch dynamics close on \((\mathcal J_1,\mathcal J_2)\). Then any nontranslation strict witness must satisfy \(K(q_*)\neq0\), so the whole lane really does reduce to the sign/structure of \(K\).

And if even 2D closure fails, that failure is itself the next explicit residual invariant.

## 8. Bottom line

The new sharpened result is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the strict same-value / same-slope witness is a value-space Cauchy problem for the reciprocal-slope tower;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{any branch-independent closure law of dimension }m\text{ forces the first even mismatch order to satisfy }N\le m,
}
\]
unless translated replication holds.

In particular,

\[
\boxed{
\widehat Q''=G(\widehat Q,\widehat Q')\ \Longrightarrow\ \text{translated replication,}
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
\text{2D closure}\ \Longrightarrow\ \text{either translated replication or }K(q_*)\neq 0.
}
\]

That is the cleanest new mathematical reduction I have from this attack.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:37:10)

_Thought for 3m 13s_

I found a cleaner way to sharpen the strict three-run endgame, and it gives a genuinely stronger local theorem than before.

The key idea is to combine the same-value / same-slope witness with the **smallest possible branch-independent closure** that still sees curvature, namely a 2-dimensional value-space closure on the reciprocal-slope pair
\[
(\mathcal J_1,\mathcal J_2).
\]

This gives a real “quadratic witness” theorem.

## 1. The witness point and the reciprocal-slope pair

On the live overlap interval \(I_q\), let
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2
\]
be the outer inverse branches and
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
At the strict same-value transport witness \(q_*\), we already know
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]
Equivalently, the two branches have the same reciprocal slope:
\[
\mathcal J_{1,2}(q_*)=\mathcal J_{1,0}(q_*),
\qquad
\mathcal J_{1,i}(q)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))}.
\]

Also,
\[
\Delta''(q)=\mathcal J_{2,2}(q)-\mathcal J_{2,0}(q),
\]
and since
\[
\mathcal J_{2,i}(q)=\phi_i''(q)=-\Xi(\phi_i(q)),
\]
we have
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\qquad
K(q):=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))=-\Delta''(q).
\]

So \(K\) is exactly the quadratic mismatch at the witness.

## 2. New theorem: 2D closure forces a quadratic witness

Assume that on the two outer runs there is a **branch-independent 2D value-space closure law** for the reciprocal-slope pair:
\[
\frac{d}{dq}
\begin{pmatrix}
\mathcal J_1\\[2pt]
\mathcal J_2
\end{pmatrix}
=
F\!\left(q,\mathcal J_1,\mathcal J_2\right)
\]
for some branch-independent analytic vector field \(F\).

Then at the witness \(q_*\), there are only two possibilities:

1. local translated replication:
   \[
   \phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C
   \quad\text{near }q_*;
   \]
2. strict quadratic mismatch:
   \[
   K(q_*)\neq 0,
   \qquad\text{equivalently}\qquad
   \Delta''(q_*)\neq 0.
   \]

### Proof
At \(q_*\), we already have
\[
\mathcal J_{1,2}(q_*)=\mathcal J_{1,0}(q_*).
\]
If also
\[
\mathcal J_{2,2}(q_*)=\mathcal J_{2,0}(q_*),
\]
then the two branchwise reciprocal-slope pairs solve the same first-order 2D ODE with the same initial value at \(q_*\). By uniqueness,
\[
(\mathcal J_{1,2},\mathcal J_{2,2})\equiv (\mathcal J_{1,0},\mathcal J_{2,0})
\]
near \(q_*\). In particular
\[
\Delta'(q)=\mathcal J_{1,2}(q)-\mathcal J_{1,0}(q)\equiv 0,
\]
so
\[
\Delta(q)\equiv C
\]
locally, i.e. we are in the translated-replication branch.

Therefore, in the nontranslation branch we must have
\[
\mathcal J_{2,2}(q_*)-\mathcal J_{2,0}(q_*)\neq 0,
\]
which is exactly
\[
\Delta''(q_*)\neq 0
\qquad\Longleftrightarrow\qquad
K(q_*)\neq 0.
\]
∎

So under the very natural 2D closure hypothesis, the whole tower business collapses immediately to the curvature defect \(K\).

This is better than the earlier “some even tower level eventually differs.” It says:

\[
\boxed{
\text{under branch-independent 2D closure, every nontranslation strict witness is already quadratic.}
}
\]

That is a real sharpening.

## 3. Sign of the quadratic witness

Now use the fact that \(q_*\) is an interior minimum of \(\Delta\).

In the nontranslation branch, the theorem above gives
\[
\Delta''(q_*)\neq 0.
\]
But at an interior minimum,
\[
\Delta''(q_*)\ge 0.
\]
Since \(\Delta\) is analytic and not locally constant in the nontranslation branch, we must in fact have
\[
\Delta''(q_*)>0.
\]
Therefore
\[
K(q_*)=-\Delta''(q_*)<0.
\]

So we get the clean corollary:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Under branch-independent 2D closure, every nontranslation strict same-value / same-slope witness satisfies }K(q_*)<0.
}
\]

That is the strongest local sign theorem I have so far.

It says the strict witness always produces a **negative quadratic well** in the defect scalar \(K\), provided the branch dynamics close on \((\mathcal J_1,\mathcal J_2)\).

## 4. Endpoint sign + quadratic witness = forced sign changes of \(K\)

Now combine this with the endpoint asymptotics we already had in the CC/CR/RC regimes.

Near any overlap endpoint where one outer branch approaches a critical point and the other remains regular, we have
\[
K(q)\to +\infty.
\]

So in the CC/CR/RC regimes:

- \(K(q)\) is positive near the critical overlap ends;
- under 2D closure, \(K(q_*)<0\) at the strict witness.

Therefore \(K\) must cross zero.

More precisely:

- in **CR** or **RC**, there is at least one zero of \(K\) between the positive endpoint region and the negative witness region;
- in **CC**, there are at least two distinct zeros of \(K\), one on each side of the negative well.

So under 2D closure, the strict three-run problem becomes a genuine **sign-change problem for \(K\)**.

That is much cleaner than the earlier hidden-state phrasing.

## 5. Why this is mathematically useful

This turns the strict three-run lane into an attackable scalar problem:

- either translated replication occurs;
- or \(K\) has a negative quadratic well at the witness and positive behavior at the critical ends.

So any theorem of the following kinds would now close the lane:

1. a global sign theorem \(K\ge 0\);
2. a maximum principle or comparison principle forbidding such a negative well;
3. a monotonicity theorem showing \(K\) cannot change sign in the required pattern;
4. an exclusion of translated replication.

These are much more concrete than the earlier broad transport-language goals.

## 6. A second sharpening: if translated replication is impossible, 2D closure completely reduces the lane to \(K\)

Suppose we can separately rule out local translated replication of the outer-run germ, i.e.
\[
\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C
\]
cannot happen on disjoint outer runs.

Then under 2D closure, every strict witness must satisfy
\[
K(q_*)<0.
\]

So in that setting the entire strict three-run residual lane is no longer a tower problem at all. It is exactly the problem of controlling the scalar defect \(K\).

That is a very useful reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{translated replication impossible }+\ \text{2D closure}
\Longrightarrow
\text{strict three-run transport is completely detected by }K.
}
\]

## 7. What this says about the updated draft’s “transport closure” target

The update note suggested attacking a branch-independent closure law of the form
\[
\widehat Q''(u)=G(\widehat Q(u),\widehat Q'(u)).
\]
That is a 1D closure law, and it is even stronger than the 2D hypothesis above. Under 1D closure we already knew strict witness forces translated replication.

What I’ve shown now is that you do not need such a strong law to get a decisive local reduction. A branch-independent **2D closure** on \((\mathcal J_1,\mathcal J_2)\) is already enough to force a quadratic witness
\[
K(q_*)<0
\]
in the nontranslation branch.

So the updated closure hierarchy is now:

- **1D closure** \(\Rightarrow\) strict witness collapses directly to translated replication;
- **2D closure** \(\Rightarrow\) nontranslation strict witness is necessarily quadratic and gives \(K(q_*)<0\).

That is a genuinely useful sharpening of the endgame.

## 8. Best next attacks

I think the best next moves are now these.

### Attack A: prove or exploit 2D closure
Try to show that along the outer runs, the reciprocal-slope pair \((\mathcal J_1,\mathcal J_2)\) satisfies a branch-independent first-order system in value space. If that lands, then the strict three-run lane reduces immediately to the sign/structure of \(K\).

### Attack B: prove a sign/comparison theorem for \(K\)
Under 2D closure, the local picture is:
- \(K(q_*)<0\) at the witness;
- \(K>0\) near critical overlap ends in CC/CR/RC.

So try to prove \(K\ge 0\), or at least prove that \(K\) cannot have the required positive-to-negative sign profile.

### Attack C: kill translated replication
If translated replication of the outer-run germ can be ruled out directly, then 2D closure makes the reduction to \(K\) complete.

## 9. Bottom line

The strongest new result from this continuation is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Quadratic witness theorem:}\ 
\text{under branch-independent 2D closure on }(\mathcal J_1,\mathcal J_2),
\text{ every nontranslation strict same-value / same-slope witness satisfies }K(q_*)<0.
}
\]

That is a genuine new theorem-shaped reduction.

It means that once you have even modest branch-independent closure, the strict three-run fixed-shear lane is no longer a mysterious high-order transport problem. It is a concrete scalar sign-change problem for the curvature defect \(K\), plus the separate translated-replication branch.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:38:49)

I’m pushing on the new quadratic-witness theorem now. The most promising move is to see whether the 2D closure law can itself be extracted from the inverse-branch identities, or at least whether the pair \((\mathcal J_1,\mathcal J_2)\) closes branch-independently under one more mild assumption.

_Thought for 6s_

Here is the next push. I think the right way forward is to stop treating “2D closure” as an abstract wish and see exactly what it would mean in the current variables.

Recall on each outer inverse branch \(\phi_i\) we defined
\[
\mathcal J_{1,i}(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))},
\qquad
\mathcal J_{2,i}(q)=\frac{d}{dq}\mathcal J_{1,i}(q)
=-\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_i(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q)))^3}
=-\Xi(\phi_i(q)).
\]
So the branchwise pair
\[
Y_i(q):=(\mathcal J_{1,i}(q),\mathcal J_{2,i}(q))
\]
already satisfies the first half of a 2D first-order system:
\[
\frac{d}{dq}\mathcal J_{1,i}(q)=\mathcal J_{2,i}(q).
\]

Thus the only missing piece for a full 2D branch-independent closure law is an equation for
\[
\frac{d}{dq}\mathcal J_{2,i}(q).
\]

## 1. Exact formula for the third tower entry

Differentiate \(\mathcal J_{2,i}\):
\[
\mathcal J_{3,i}(q)
=
\frac{d}{dq}\mathcal J_{2,i}(q)
=
\frac{d}{dq}\Bigl(-\Xi(\phi_i(q))\Bigr)
=
-\frac{\Xi'(\phi_i(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))}
=
-\mathcal J_{1,i}(q)\,\Xi'(\phi_i(q)).
\]

So the exact 2D closure question is:

> can \(\Xi'(\phi_i(q))\) be written branch-independently as a function of
\[
q,\ \mathcal J_{1,i}(q),\ \mathcal J_{2,i}(q)\ ?
\]

Equivalently, since \(\mathcal J_{2,i}=-\Xi(\phi_i(q))\), can the derivative of \(\Xi\) along a level branch be recovered from \(q\), \(1/\widehat Q'\), and \(\Xi\) itself?

This makes the 2D closure problem very concrete.

## 2. What 2D closure really amounts to

A branch-independent 2D closure law for \(Y_i=(\mathcal J_{1,i},\mathcal J_{2,i})\) is equivalent to the existence of a function \(H\) such that on the outer runs
\[
\Xi'(u)=H\!\left(\widehat Q(u),\,\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\,\Xi(u)\right).
\]
Indeed, then
\[
\frac{d}{dq}
\begin{pmatrix}
\mathcal J_{1,i}\\[2pt]
\mathcal J_{2,i}
\end{pmatrix}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\mathcal J_{2,i}\\[4pt]
-\mathcal J_{1,i}\,H\!\left(q,\mathcal J_{1,i},-\mathcal J_{2,i}\right)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

So the updated closure hierarchy becomes extremely explicit:

- **1D closure**:
  \[
  \widehat Q''=G(\widehat Q,\widehat Q')
  \]
  or equivalently
  \[
  \Xi=F\!\left(\widehat Q,\frac1{\widehat Q'}\right);
  \]

- **2D closure**:
  \[
  \Xi'=H\!\left(\widehat Q,\frac1{\widehat Q'},\Xi\right).
  \]

This is a nice clarification: 2D closure is exactly “the derivative of the curvature observable \(\Xi\) is branch-independent in the \((q,\mathcal J_1,\Xi)\)-variables.”

## 3. Why this is plausible from the current structure

The recent updates already suggest that the residual transport lane should be one-variable and inverse-branch based, and that the real fork is between explicit \(\Xi\)-descent failure and rigidified transport. This 2D closure law is exactly the weakest value-space law that still controls the strict witness quadratically.

In fact, if the package is as quotient-visible as the recent updates suggest, then \(\Xi\) is already the first nontrivial value-space curvature scalar, so asking for \(\Xi'\) to be branch-independent in \((q,\mathcal J_1,\Xi)\) is mathematically very natural. It is the first nontrivial prolongation after \(\mathcal J_1\) and \(\mathcal J_2\).

So this is not just an abstract closure hypothesis anymore; it is the sharpest “next closure” compatible with the known structure.

## 4. Consequence: a fully explicit quadratic witness theorem

Now the earlier quadratic witness theorem can be rewritten in a much cleaner form.

### Explicit quadratic witness theorem
Assume that on the two outer runs there exists a branch-independent function \(H\) such that
\[
\Xi'(u)=H\!\left(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\Xi(u)\right).
\]
Then at any strict same-value / same-slope witness \(q_*\), exactly one of the following holds:

1. local translated replication:
   \[
   \phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C
   \quad\text{near }q_*;
   \]
2. strict quadratic mismatch:
   \[
   K(q_*)<0,
   \qquad
   K(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q)).
   \]

The proof is just the 2D uniqueness argument we already had, but now the hypothesis is expressed in the most concrete possible way.

That is a much nicer theorem target for the paper or for downstream attacks.

## 5. What happens if 2D closure fails

This is also now very precise.

If the 2D closure law fails, then the first genuine residual transport obstruction is exactly the failure of \(\Xi'\) to be determined branch-independently from
\[
\left(\widehat Q,\frac1{\widehat Q'},\Xi\right).
\]

So failure of 2D closure is not amorphous. It means there exist same-value points \(u_0,u_2\) on the outer runs with
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2),\qquad
\frac1{\widehat Q'(u_0)}=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u_2)},\qquad
\Xi(u_0)=\Xi(u_2),
\]
but
\[
\Xi'(u_0)\neq \Xi'(u_2).
\]

That is an explicit third-order same-value transport defect.

So the sharpened fork is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either }\Xi'\text{ closes branch-independently in }(q,\mathcal J_1,\Xi),
}
\]
and then strict witness reduces to translated replication or \(K(q_*)<0\);

or
\[
\boxed{
\text{the first explicit transport obstruction is a same-value defect in }\Xi'.
}
\]

That is much more concrete than anything I had earlier.

## 6. Relation to the reciprocal-slope tower

This also clarifies the tower picture beautifully.

The reciprocal-slope tower differences
\[
D_n(q):=\mathcal J_{n,2}(q)-\mathcal J_{n,0}(q)
\]
are just the value-space transport defects at successive derivatives.

At the strict witness:
\[
D_1(q_*)=0.
\]
If additionally
\[
D_2(q_*)=0,
\]
then the quadratic defect \(K(q_*)\) vanishes.

Under 2D closure, that forces
\[
D_n(q_*)=0\quad\forall n,
\]
hence translated replication.

So the tower is now being cut down by the closure hierarchy exactly as one would hope:
- 1D closure kills everything after \(D_1\);
- 2D closure kills everything after \(D_2\);
- failure of 2D closure means the first unresolved defect is already visible in the next prolongation.

That is a very satisfying structural picture.

## 7. A new possible attack: compare \(\Xi'\) directly at the witness

This suggests a better local target than just “prove a sign theorem for \(K\).”

At a strict witness \(q_*\), there are three increasingly strong possibilities:

1. \(D_1(q_*)=0\) always;
2. maybe \(D_2(q_*)\neq 0\), giving the quadratic witness \(K(q_*)<0\);
3. if \(D_2(q_*)=0\), then either translated replication occurs or the first defect is already
   \[
   D_3(q_*)=\mathcal J_{3,2}(q_*)-\mathcal J_{3,0}(q_*)
   =
   -\mathcal J_{1}(q_*)\bigl(\Xi'(\phi_2(q_*))-\Xi'(\phi_0(q_*))\bigr).
   \]

So the next meaningful local obstruction after \(K\) is exactly the same-value \(\Xi'\)-mismatch.

That gives a very concrete next theorem target:

### Cubic obstruction lemma
If at a strict same-value / same-slope witness \(q_*\) one has \(K(q_*)=0\) and translated replication fails, then
\[
\Xi'(\phi_2(q_*))\neq \Xi'(\phi_0(q_*))
\]
unless branch-independent 2D closure holds.

This is really just a repackaging of the tower argument, but it identifies the next defect scalar explicitly.

## 8. Best next attack from here

I think the best next move is now one of these two.

### Attack A: prove 2D closure directly
Try to show
\[
\Xi'(u)=H\!\left(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\Xi(u)\right)
\]
on the outer runs.

If this works, the strict witness reduces immediately to:
- translated replication, or
- \(K(q_*)<0\).

That would be a huge simplification.

### Attack B: if 2D closure seems too strong, attack the \(\Xi'\)-defect directly
Show that at a same-value / same-slope witness with \(K(q_*)=0\), the next defect
\[
\Xi'(\phi_2(q_*))-\Xi'(\phi_0(q_*))
\]
has a forced sign or cannot vanish.

That would push the explicit scalar obstruction one level deeper in a controlled way.

## 9. Bottom line

The main new finding from this continuation is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{2D closure on the reciprocal-slope pair }(\mathcal J_1,\mathcal J_2)
\text{ is equivalent to the explicit branch-independent law }
\Xi'=H\!\left(\widehat Q,\frac1{\widehat Q'},\Xi\right).
}
\]

And under that law:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every nontranslation strict same-value / same-slope witness is already quadratic, with }K(q_*)<0.
}
\]

If that law fails, the first unresolved defect is no longer abstract hidden transport; it is explicitly a same-value \(\Xi'\)-mismatch.

That is, I think, the sharpest mathematical continuation of the attack so far.
## User (2026-04-22 01:45:16)

Here’s a brief note you can share with all agents:

---

Team — one important draft correction: there was a sign reversal in the downstream strict three-run fixed-shear narrative, and it has now been corrected.

The correct trichotomy is
[
B_0+B_2>B_1 \quad\text{open outer overlap,}
]
[
B_0+B_2=B_1 \quad\text{touching,}
]
[
B_1>B_0+B_2 \quad\text{separation.}
]

So the inverse-branch-gap / (\Delta) / (\Xi) transport setup belongs only to the actual overlap regime
[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
]
not to the separation regime
[
B_1>B_0+B_2.
]

In particular, any argument that starts from outer inverse branches (\phi_0,\phi_2), an overlap interval (\Omega_0\cap\Omega_2), or a gap function
[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)
]
should now be understood as conditional on genuine outer overlap. The transport identities themselves may still be fine once overlap exists; what was wrong was the regime identification.

Practical consequence: if you are using the strict three-run lane, please make sure you are not treating
[
B_1>B_0+B_2
]
as the “live overlap” case. That is the separation case. The live overlap case is
[
B_0+B_2>B_1.
]

This does not damage the broader transport endgame, but it does mean the (\Delta/\Xi) story must be phrased as an overlap-regime analysis, not as something triggered by the opposite inequality.

---

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:45:18)

That correction is important, and it changes the framing in a useful way. I’m going to re-base the attack on the **actual overlap regime** \(B_0+B_2>B_1\), separate out the touching and separation cases cleanly, and then only use the \(\Delta/\Xi\) machinery where open outer overlap really exists.

_Thought for 23s_

Yes — that correction matters, and it improves the attack.

The \(\Delta/\Xi\) machinery was structurally fine, but I had attached it to the wrong inequality. The right place for it is the **open overlap** regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
not the separation regime
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2.
\]

So let me reset the strict three-run analysis correctly and sharpen it from there.

## 1. Correct three-run trichotomy

For the compressed \(+\,-\,+\) three-run picture with endpoint values
\[
d_0<d_1>d_2<d_3,
\]
the outer windows are
\[
\Omega_0=(d_0,d_1),\qquad \Omega_2=(d_2,d_3).
\]
The exact criterion is

\[
\Omega_0\cap\Omega_2\neq\varnothing
\iff
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
with
\[
B_0=d_1-d_0,\qquad
B_1=d_1-d_2,\qquad
B_2=d_3-d_2.
\]

So:

- \(B_0+B_2>B_1\): **open outer overlap**;
- \(B_0+B_2=B_1\): **touching** at one critical value, no open same-value interval;
- \(B_1>B_0+B_2\): **separation**, no outer same-value transport interval.

That means the whole inverse-branch transport lane exists only in the first case.

## 2. What survives in each regime

This immediately reorganizes the lane.

### Separation regime
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2.
\]
There is no open outer overlap interval. So there are no outer inverse branches \(\phi_0,\phi_2\) defined on a common open value interval, and no gap function
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)
\]
to study. So the \(\Delta/\Xi\) transport analysis does **not** belong here at all.

This regime is already the non-overlap/no-reentry side of the quotient-visible picture and should be treated as separation, not transport.

### Touching regime
\[
B_0+B_2=B_1.
\]
The outer windows meet only at a point. Again there is no open transport interval, so no open \(\Delta\)-analysis. This is the sterile touching case.

### Open overlap regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1.
\]
Only here do we get an open common value interval
\[
I_q=\Omega_0\cap\Omega_2=(q_-,q_+)
=
(\max\{d_0,d_2\},\,\min\{d_1,d_3\}),
\]
and only here do the outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:I_q\to\mathbf R_u
\]
exist on the same open set.

So the corrected version is:

\[
\boxed{
\Delta/\Xi\text{ is an overlap-regime analysis, not a separation-regime analysis.}
}
\]

That is the first correction.

## 3. Corrected \(\Delta/\Xi\) transport setup in the overlap regime

Now assume
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1.
\]
Define
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
Then the transport identities remain exactly correct:

\[
\phi_i'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))},
\]
hence
\[
\Delta'(q)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}
-
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
and
\[
\phi_i''(q)
=
-\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_i(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q)))^3}
=
-\Xi(\phi_i(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
Therefore
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\]
and the defect scalar is
\[
K(q):=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))=-\Delta''(q).
\]

So the formulas were fine. The only issue was the regime.

## 4. Corrected witness logic

I need to sharpen something else too: in the overlap regime, an interior critical point of \(\Delta\) is **not automatic in every overlap subcase**. It is automatic only when the endpoint behavior forces a sign change of \(\Delta'\).

Let
\[
q_-=\max\{d_0,d_2\},\qquad q_+=\min\{d_1,d_3\}.
\]
There are four overlap endpoint types, depending on whether the left/right overlap ends come from regular or critical outer endpoints:

- **CC**: \(q_-=d_2,\ q_+=d_1\),
- **CR**: \(q_-=d_2,\ q_+=d_3\),
- **RC**: \(q_-=d_0,\ q_+=d_1\),
- **RR**: \(q_-=d_0,\ q_+=d_3\).

In the CC case, both overlap ends are critical on one of the outer branches, so:
- near \(q_-\), the right outer reciprocal slope blows up and \(\Delta'(q)\to +\infty\);
- near \(q_+\), the left outer reciprocal slope blows up and \(\Delta'(q)\to -\infty\).

So by continuity, \(\Delta'\) has an interior zero. Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{In the CC overlap regime, a same-value / same-slope witness is automatic.}
}
\]

In CR, RC, and RR, that is no longer automatic from endpoint blow-up alone. There may still be a witness, but it needs a separate argument.

That is the second important correction.

## 5. Sharpened local theorem in the CC overlap regime

So the strongest theorem I can now state cleanly is **conditional on CC overlap**, or more generally conditional on the existence of an interior critical point of \(\Delta\).

Assume open overlap and suppose \(q_*\in I_q\) satisfies
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]
Then
\[
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q_*))}
=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q_*))}.
\]
So \(q_*\) is a same-value / same-slope witness.

Now define the reciprocal-slope tower
\[
J_1(u):=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\qquad
J_{n+1}(u):=J_1(u)\partial_u J_n(u),
\]
and along branches
\[
\mathcal J_{n,i}(q):=J_n(\phi_i(q)).
\]
Then
\[
\frac{d}{dq}\mathcal J_{n,i}(q)=\mathcal J_{n+1,i}(q),
\]
and
\[
\Delta^{(m)}(q)=\mathcal J_{m,2}(q)-\mathcal J_{m,0}(q).
\]

So at the witness \(q_*\), exactly one of the following holds:

1. **local translated replication**
   \[
   \phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C
   \quad\text{near }q_*;
   \]
2. there exists a least even \(N\ge 2\) such that
   \[
   \mathcal J_{N,2}(q_*)-\mathcal J_{N,0}(q_*)>0.
   \]

This local fork survives the regime correction completely. It just needs to be stated in the **actual overlap regime**.

## 6. Corrected role of \(K\)

The curvature defect
\[
K(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))
\]
is now properly interpreted as the **quadratic witness**, when the first even mismatch occurs at order \(N=2\).

So in the overlap regime, at a same-value / same-slope witness \(q_*\),

- if \(K(q_*)\neq 0\), then the first transverse defect is already quadratic;
- if \(K(q_*)=0\), then either:
  - translated replication holds, or
  - the first even defect is higher-order in the reciprocal-slope tower.

This remains correct, but it is now correctly tied to overlap, not separation.

## 7. Corrected closure hierarchy

The closure story also survives, with the same regime correction.

### 1D closure
If on the outer runs one has a branch-independent law
\[
\widehat Q''=G(\widehat Q,\widehat Q'),
\]
then at a same-value / same-slope witness \(q_*\), uniqueness in value space forces
\[
\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C
\]
locally.

So in the overlap regime:

\[
\boxed{
\text{1D branch-independent closure }+\text{ witness }\Longrightarrow\text{ translated replication.}
}
\]

### 2D closure
If instead one has the branch-independent 2D closure law
\[
\Xi'(u)=H\!\left(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\Xi(u)\right),
\]
equivalently a branch-independent first-order system for \((\mathcal J_1,\mathcal J_2)\), then at a same-value / same-slope witness \(q_*\), either:

- translated replication holds, or
- the quadratic witness is nonzero:
  \[
  K(q_*)<0.
  \]

So the earlier quadratic-witness theorem is still good — but again it lives in the overlap regime and at an actual witness.

## 8. What the corrected endgame now is

With the sign correction in place, the fixed-shear three-run lane looks like this.

### Separation
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2.
\]
This is already the no-overlap regime. No \(\Delta/\Xi\) transport story here.

### Touching
\[
B_0+B_2=B_1.
\]
Sterile touching. No open transport interval.

### Overlap
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1.
\]
This is the only regime where the inverse-branch gap analysis exists.

Inside overlap, the next split is:

- **CC overlap**: same-value / same-slope witness is automatic;
- **CR/RC/RR overlap**: witness requires a separate argument.

And once a witness exists, the local fork is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either translated replication, or first even reciprocal-slope mismatch.}
}
\]

If a 2D closure law holds, that fork sharpens to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either translated replication, or }K(q_*)<0.
}
\]

That is the corrected and sharpened transport endgame.

## 9. Best next attacks after the correction

There are now three especially clean next moves.

First, attack the **existence of a witness** in the CR/RC/RR overlap regimes. In CC it is automatic, but elsewhere it is not. If one can show overlap already forces a same-value / same-slope witness in all overlap subcases, the whole local tower machinery becomes globally available on the overlap lane.

Second, attack **translated replication**:
\[
\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C.
\]
This remains a very rigid local symmetry of the outer-run germ and looks like the most vulnerable branch.

Third, attack the explicit **2D closure law**
\[
\Xi'(u)=H\!\left(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\Xi(u)\right).
\]
Because if that holds, then every nontranslation witness is already quadratic and detected by \(K\).

## 10. Bottom line

So the corrected structural summary of my lane is:

\[
\boxed{
\Delta/\Xi\text{ belongs only to the open overlap regime }B_0+B_2>B_1.
}
\]

Within that regime:

- CC overlap automatically gives a same-value / same-slope witness;
- at any witness, the exact local fork is:
  \[
  \text{translated replication}\quad\text{or}\quad\text{least even reciprocal-slope mismatch};
  \]
- under branch-independent 2D closure, the nontranslation branch is already quadratic:
  \[
  K(q_*)<0.
  \]

That is the corrected and sharpened continuation of the attack.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:47:20)

I’m going after the cleanest unresolved branch now: **witness existence in the non-CC overlap cases**. If overlap itself already forces a same-value / same-slope witness in CR/RC/RR, then the whole local tower machinery becomes available on the entire overlap regime, not just the CC subcase.

_Thought for 7s_

Here is the next push.

I think the best unresolved local question after the sign correction is exactly this:

> In the open overlap regime
> \[
> B_0+B_2>B_1,
> \]
> do we always get a same-value / same-slope witness
> \[
> \Delta'(q_*)=0
> \]
> on the overlap interval \(I_q=\Omega_0\cap\Omega_2\), or is that only automatic in the CC endpoint configuration?

I pushed that directly. The answer is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{CC gives a witness automatically; CR/RC/RR do not force one by pure endpoint asymptotics alone.}
}
\]

But there is still a useful partial theorem: in every overlap case, the witness question reduces to a very explicit **endpoint reciprocal-slope comparison**.

## 1. Correct endpoint data for \(\Delta'\)

On the overlap interval
\[
I_q=(q_-,q_+)=(\max\{d_0,d_2\},\,\min\{d_1,d_3\}),
\]
we have
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]

Define the outer reciprocal slopes
\[
p_i(q):=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))}>0
\qquad (i=0,2),
\]
so
\[
\Delta'(q)=p_2(q)-p_0(q).
\]

Because each outer run is monotone, each \(p_i\) is continuous on \(I_q\), and:
- as \(q\) approaches a **critical** overlap endpoint on that run, \(p_i(q)\to +\infty\);
- as \(q\) approaches a **regular** overlap endpoint on that run, \(p_i(q)\) tends to a finite positive value.

So the witness problem is equivalent to asking whether the two positive functions \(p_0,p_2\) cross on \(I_q\).

That is the right formulation.

## 2. Exact endpoint behavior by regime

Let me write the one-sided endpoint values of \(\Delta'\) explicitly.

### CC regime
\[
q_-=d_2,\qquad q_+=d_1.
\]
Then:
- at \(q_-\), the right branch is critical, left branch regular:
  \[
  p_2(q)\to+\infty,\qquad p_0(q)\to \text{finite},
  \]
  hence
  \[
  \Delta'(q)\to +\infty \quad (q\downarrow q_-);
  \]
- at \(q_+\), the left branch is critical, right branch regular:
  \[
  p_0(q)\to+\infty,\qquad p_2(q)\to \text{finite},
  \]
  hence
  \[
  \Delta'(q)\to -\infty \quad (q\uparrow q_+).
  \]

So by the IVT, a witness is automatic.

### CR regime
\[
q_-=d_2,\qquad q_+=d_3.
\]
Then:
- at \(q_-\): \(p_2\to+\infty\), \(p_0\) finite, so
  \[
  \Delta'(q)\to +\infty \quad (q\downarrow q_-);
  \]
- at \(q_+\): both \(p_0,p_2\) are finite, so
  \[
  \Delta'(q)\to p_2(d_3^-)-p_0(d_3^-),
  \]
  a finite number of unknown sign.

Thus a witness exists iff that right-end finite limit is negative.

### RC regime
\[
q_-=d_0,\qquad q_+=d_1.
\]
Then:
- at \(q_-\): both \(p_0,p_2\) finite, so
  \[
  \Delta'(q)\to p_2(d_0^+)-p_0(d_0^+),
  \]
  finite of unknown sign;
- at \(q_+\): \(p_0\to+\infty\), \(p_2\) finite, so
  \[
  \Delta'(q)\to -\infty \quad (q\uparrow q_+).
  \]

Thus a witness exists iff the left-end finite limit is positive.

### RR regime
\[
q_-=d_0,\qquad q_+=d_3.
\]
Both ends are regular on both outer branches, so
\[
\Delta'(q)\to \text{finite at both ends}.
\]
No witness is forced by endpoint asymptotics alone.

So the clean theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{CC forces a witness. CR forces one iff }\Delta'(q_+)<0.
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{RC forces one iff }\Delta'(q_-)>0.
\qquad
\text{RR needs a separate interior argument.}
}
\]

That is already a useful sharpening.

## 3. Rewriting the CR/RC endpoint tests geometrically

The finite endpoint tests can be written in a cleaner way.

### CR
At \(q_+=d_3\), the right outer branch hits its regular endpoint \(u_3\), while the left outer branch hits the unique point \(u_{0,3}\in R_0\) with
\[
\widehat Q(u_{0,3})=d_3.
\]
So
\[
\Delta'(q_+^-)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u_3)}
-
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u_{0,3})}.
\]
Thus the witness criterion is
\[
\boxed{
\text{CR witness exists } \Longleftrightarrow \widehat Q'(u_{0,3})>\widehat Q'(u_3).
}
\]
In words: the left outer run must be steeper than the right endpoint at the shared value \(d_3\).

### RC
Similarly, at \(q_-=d_0\), the left outer branch hits its regular endpoint \(u_0\), while the right outer branch hits the unique point \(u_{2,0}\in R_2\) with
\[
\widehat Q(u_{2,0})=d_0.
\]
So
\[
\Delta'(q_-^+)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u_{2,0})}
-
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u_0)}.
\]
Thus the witness criterion is
\[
\boxed{
\text{RC witness exists } \Longleftrightarrow \widehat Q'(u_0)>\widehat Q'(u_{2,0}).
}
\]

These are very concrete endpoint slope comparisons.

So the witness problem in CR/RC is now not vague anymore; it is reduced to comparing the outer-run slopes at the relevant shared endpoint value.

## 4. A useful reformulation via \(\Delta\) itself

There is another structural reformulation.

In every overlap regime, \(\Delta\) is a continuous positive function on \(I_q\) — the right inverse branch lies to the right of the left inverse branch. So a same-value / same-slope witness is exactly an interior extremum of this positive gap.

Thus the witness question becomes:

> does the outer-branch separation \(\Delta(q)\) have an interior extremum as the common value \(q\) runs across the overlap interval?

In CC the answer is yes automatically.
In CR/RC it is yes iff the finite endpoint slope test has the correct sign.
In RR it is a genuine interior geometry question.

That makes RR much more isolated: it is the only case where the witness question is not already reducible to endpoint slope comparison.

## 5. What this means for the transport fork

This helps a lot with the later tower/\(K\)/translated-replication story.

The local transport fork at a witness point remains correct:

\[
\boxed{
\Delta'(q_*)=0
\Longrightarrow
\text{either translated replication, or first even reciprocal-slope mismatch.}
}
\]

What is new is that we now know exactly when that fork is available without extra work:

- always in CC;
- in CR/RC iff the explicit endpoint slope inequality holds;
- in RR only after an additional interior argument.

So the strict overlap lane is no longer one monolithic transport problem. It splits cleanly into:

1. **CC**: automatic witness, hence automatic access to the local fork;
2. **CR/RC**: witness reduced to one explicit endpoint slope comparison;
3. **RR**: truly exceptional, requiring separate analysis.

That is a much cleaner road map.

## 6. Best next attacks by regime

This suggests a better division of labor on the fixed-shear overlap lane.

### CC overlap
No more work is needed to get a witness. The next attack is immediately the local fork:
- kill translated replication, or
- prove 2D closure and get \(K(q_*)<0\).

### CR / RC overlap
The next attack should be **endpoint slope comparison**, not full transport.
You want a theorem of the form:
\[
\widehat Q'(u_{0,3})>\widehat Q'(u_3)
\quad\text{or}\quad
\widehat Q'(u_0)>\widehat Q'(u_{2,0})
\]
from the surrounding three-run geometry.

This is much more concrete than trying to prove existence of an arbitrary witness.

### RR overlap
This remains the genuinely exceptional case. Here the witness question is not controlled by endpoint blow-up, and if a witness exists then we can use the same local tower fork. So RR is now the only place where an independent interior argument is truly needed.

## 7. One more small gain: the endpoint slope comparison is already in value space

The CR/RC tests are nice because they are still fully quotient-visible. They compare \(\widehat Q'\) at two points carrying the same value \(q\), i.e. they compare
\[
p_0(q)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\qquad
p_2(q)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}.
\]
So the same reciprocal-slope transport language still applies. We do not need to introduce any new source-side quantity.

That means a very plausible next theorem target is:

### Same-value endpoint slope comparison theorem
In CR/RC overlap, the reciprocal slope on the branch that terminates regularly at the overlap endpoint is strictly smaller than on the opposite outer branch at the same value.

If that were true, witness existence would follow automatically in all non-RR overlap regimes.

That feels like the right next technical target.

## 8. Bottom line

The sharpened outcome of this continuation is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{CC overlap always gives a same-value / same-slope witness;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{CR/RC overlap reduce witness existence to one explicit same-value endpoint slope comparison;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{RR remains the only genuinely exceptional overlap case needing a separate interior argument.}
}
\]

So the \(\Delta/\Xi\) transport lane is now much better organized:

- CC: automatic local transport fork;
- CR/RC: endpoint slope comparison problem;
- RR: exceptional interior transport problem.

That is the sharpest continuation of the corrected attack I can give right now.
## User (2026-04-22 01:49:48)

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:49:49)

I’m pushing on the CR/RC endpoint-slope problem now. The cleanest hope is to derive witness existence from a monotonicity principle for the reciprocal-slope difference \(p_2-p_0\), or, failing that, to isolate RR as the only truly exceptional overlap case in a theorem-shaped way.

_Thought for 24s_

Here is the next attack. I think the right way to sharpen the overlap lane is to stop asking for a witness “by hand” and instead study the **reciprocal-slope difference**
\[
W(q):=p_2(q)-p_0(q)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}
-
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}
=
\Delta'(q).
\]

Then:

- a same-value / same-slope witness is exactly a zero of \(W\);
- the curvature defect is
  \[
  W'(q)=\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))=-K(q).
  \]

So the witness problem is exactly a one-dimensional sign-change problem for \(W\).

## 1. The overlap regimes in terms of \(W\)

In the open overlap regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
the overlap interval is
\[
I_q=(q_-,q_+)=(\max\{d_0,d_2\},\,\min\{d_1,d_3\}).
\]

The endpoint behavior of \(W=\Delta'\) is:

### CC
\[
q_-=d_2,\qquad q_+=d_1.
\]
Then
\[
W(q)\to +\infty \quad (q\downarrow q_-),
\qquad
W(q)\to -\infty \quad (q\uparrow q_+).
\]
So a witness exists automatically.

### CR
\[
q_-=d_2,\qquad q_+=d_3.
\]
Then
\[
W(q)\to +\infty \quad (q\downarrow q_-),
\]
while
\[
W(q)\to W_+:=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u_3)}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u_{0,3})}
\quad (q\uparrow q_+),
\]
where \(u_3\) is the right outer endpoint and \(u_{0,3}\in R_0\) is the point with \(\widehat Q(u_{0,3})=d_3\).

So a witness exists iff \(W_+<0\).

### RC
\[
q_-=d_0,\qquad q_+=d_1.
\]
Then
\[
W(q)\to W_-:=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u_{2,0})}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u_0)}
\quad (q\downarrow q_-),
\]
and
\[
W(q)\to -\infty \quad (q\uparrow q_+).
\]
So a witness exists iff \(W_->0\).

### RR
\[
q_-=d_0,\qquad q_+=d_3.
\]
Both endpoint limits are finite:
\[
W(q)\to W_- \quad (q\downarrow q_-),\qquad
W(q)\to W_+ \quad (q\uparrow q_+).
\]
So RR is the only regime where endpoint blow-up gives no automatic sign change at all.

This is the clean endpoint reduction.

## 2. A new local theorem if \(K\ge 0\)

Now the key observation: since
\[
W'(q)=-K(q),
\]
any sign information on \(K\) immediately controls \(W\).

If we could prove
\[
K(q)\ge 0 \quad \text{on }I_q,
\]
then
\[
W'(q)\le 0,
\]
so \(W\) is monotone decreasing.

That would have the following consequences:

- in CC, witness existence is automatic anyway;
- in CR, since \(W(q)\to+\infty\) at the left endpoint, there can be at most one witness, and it exists iff \(W_+<0\);
- in RC, similarly there can be at most one witness, and it exists iff \(W_->0\);
- in RR, witness existence reduces to the single endpoint-sign condition
  \[
  W_->0>W_+.
  \]

So under a one-sided sign theorem for \(K\), the entire overlap witness problem becomes an **endpoint test**.

That is already a useful conditional theorem:

\[
\boxed{
K\ge 0 \text{ on }I_q
\Longrightarrow
W=\Delta' \text{ is decreasing on }I_q,
}
\]
hence witness existence reduces to endpoint signs.

This is much cleaner than searching for critical points of \(\Delta\) directly.

## 3. Why the CR/RC endpoint tests are the real next target

This reframes the CR/RC lanes very sharply.

In CR, to get a witness it is enough to prove
\[
W_+<0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\widehat Q'(u_{0,3})>\widehat Q'(u_3).
\]
In RC, it is enough to prove
\[
W_->0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\widehat Q'(u_0)>\widehat Q'(u_{2,0}).
\]

So the actual problem in CR/RC is a **same-value endpoint slope comparison theorem**.

That is much more concrete than “show \(\Delta\) has a critical point.”

It says:

- compare the slope on the regular outer endpoint of one outer run,
- with the slope on the other outer run at the same value.

If the “interior” branch is always steeper at that shared value, then the witness exists.

This is a very clean theorem target.

## 4. A useful reformulation via \(J_1\)

Since
\[
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\]
the endpoint comparison is simply
\[
J_1(u_3)<J_1(u_{0,3})
\qquad \text{(CR)},
\]
or
\[
J_1(u_{2,0})>J_1(u_0)
\qquad \text{(RC)}.
\]

So CR/RC witness existence is equivalent to a **same-value comparison theorem for the first reciprocal-slope observable \(J_1\)** on the two outer runs.

That ties the endpoint problem directly back into the reciprocal-slope transport hierarchy.

So the new sharpened picture is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{CR/RC witness existence is exactly a same-value ordering problem for }J_1.
}
\]

This is a very good reduction, because it lets us use the same transport formalism everywhere.

## 5. A plausible route using the one-vertex gap law

There is a structural reason this comparison might be true.

In CR, the overlap ends at the right outer endpoint value \(d_3\). The right outer run has just traversed the whole run from the middle critical value \(d_2\) up to \(d_3\), while the left outer run is being sampled at an interior point \(u_{0,3}\) below its top critical value \(d_1\). So geometrically, one expects the left outer branch at the shared value \(d_3\) to still be “closer to its vertex” and therefore steeper than the right endpoint branch.

That is heuristic, not theorem, but it suggests the correct same-value ordering:
\[
\widehat Q'(u_{0,3})>\widehat Q'(u_3).
\]

Similarly in RC.

So a plausible next theorem is:

### Same-value outer-slope comparison conjecture
In CR/RC overlap, at the regular overlap endpoint value shared by the two outer runs, the branch that has not yet reached its own outer endpoint is steeper than the branch at its regular endpoint.

Equivalently, the reciprocal slope \(J_1\) is smaller on the interior branch than on the regular endpoint branch.

If true, this would force witness existence in CR/RC.

## 6. RR as the genuinely exceptional regime

This makes RR stand out very clearly.

Even under a monotonicity principle \(K\ge 0\), RR still reduces only to endpoint signs of \(W\), and both endpoint signs are finite and nontrivial. So RR is truly the only overlap case where:

- no blow-up forces a witness,
- and no simple one-vertex endpoint comparison seems automatic.

So the overlap lane is now much more organized:

- **CC**: witness automatic;
- **CR/RC**: witness = same-value endpoint slope comparison;
- **RR**: genuinely exceptional.

That is the cleanest decomposition I have so far.

## 7. One more gain if 2D closure holds

Under the earlier 2D closure law
\[
\Xi'(u)=H\!\left(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\Xi(u)\right),
\]
a nontranslation witness gives
\[
K(q_*)<0.
\]
Hence
\[
W'(q_*)=-K(q_*)>0.
\]

So at the witness, \(W\) crosses upward through zero. That means the corresponding \(\Delta\) has a strict local minimum, which fits the geometric picture perfectly.

More importantly, it shows that under 2D closure the sign of \(K\) at the witness is not arbitrary. The local structure is:
- \(W=0\),
- \(W'>0\),
- i.e. \(\Delta\) has a genuine strict minimum.

So if we can also show \(W\) is monotone decreasing from one end in CR/RC or RR, we get a contradiction. That suggests an even sharper hybrid attack:

- compare endpoint signs of \(W\),
- and combine with local positivity of \(W'\) at a nontranslation witness.

That may be easier than a global sign theorem for \(K\) alone.

## 8. Best next attack from here

The best next move now seems to be:

### Attack same-value endpoint slope comparison in CR/RC
Prove the CR/RC witness criterion directly by showing
\[
\widehat Q'(u_{0,3})>\widehat Q'(u_3)
\quad\text{or}\quad
\widehat Q'(u_0)>\widehat Q'(u_{2,0}).
\]

This would immediately enlarge the “automatic witness” zone from just CC to all non-RR overlap cases.

A good way to try this is to compare \(J_1=1/\widehat Q'\) along the two outer runs at equal value and see whether the middle run geometry forces an ordering.

If that lands, then the full reciprocal-slope tower fork becomes available on all non-RR overlap cases, which would be a big step.

## 9. Bottom line

The sharpened result from this continuation is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the overlap witness problem is a sign-change problem for }W=\Delta'=J_{1,2}-J_{1,0};
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{CC overlap gives a witness automatically;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{CR/RC witness existence is exactly a same-value endpoint slope comparison for }J_1;
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{RR remains the only genuinely exceptional overlap regime.}
}
\]

That is the clearest and most attackable organization of the corrected overlap lane I have so far.

_Thought for 1m 48s_

I found a sign correction in my own last few steps, and it leads to a better attack.

The key point is: in the **overlap** regime, when a same-value / same-slope witness exists, it is a local **maximum** of the gap
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
not a minimum. That flips the sign of the local \(K\)-witness and makes the whole transport picture much more coherent.

## 1. Correct local sign at a witness

Recall
\[
W(q):=\Delta'(q)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}
-
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\qquad
K(q):=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))=-\Delta''(q).
\]

In the overlap cases where a witness exists, the endpoint sign pattern of \(W\) is:

- in **CC**:
  \[
  W(q)\to +\infty \quad (q\downarrow q_-),\qquad
  W(q)\to -\infty \quad (q\uparrow q_+),
  \]
  so \(W\) crosses from positive to negative;

- in **CR** or **RC**, if the endpoint slope comparison gives a witness, the same sign change occurs: \(W\) is positive at one end and negative at the other.

Therefore any witness \(q_*\) furnished by such a sign change is a point where \(\Delta'(q_*)=0\) and \(\Delta\) has a **local maximum**. So the first nonzero derivative of \(\Delta\) at \(q_*\) is even and **negative**.

That means the local fork should be restated as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{At a same-value / same-slope witness }q_*,
\text{ either translated replication holds,}
}
\]
or
\[
\boxed{
\text{there is a least even }N\ge 2\text{ such that }\Delta^{(N)}(q_*)<0.
}
\]

That is the corrected sign version.

## 2. Correct quadratic witness theorem under 2D closure

Now bring back the reciprocal-slope tower
\[
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\qquad
J_{n+1}(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_n(u),
\]
and the branchwise pulls
\[
\mathcal J_{n,i}(q):=J_n(\phi_i(q)).
\]
We still have
\[
\Delta^{(m)}(q)=\mathcal J_{m,2}(q)-\mathcal J_{m,0}(q).
\]

Assume branch-independent 2D closure on the pair \((\mathcal J_1,\mathcal J_2)\), equivalently
\[
\Xi'(u)=H\!\left(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\Xi(u)\right)
\]
on the two outer runs.

Then at a witness \(q_*\), as before, either:
- translated replication holds, or
- the first mismatch occurs already at order \(2\).

But now the sign is corrected:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Correct quadratic witness theorem.}\quad
\text{Under branch-independent 2D closure, every nontranslation same-value / same-slope witness satisfies}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\Delta''(q_*)<0
\qquad\Longleftrightarrow\qquad
K(q_*)>0.
}
\]

So under 2D closure, the witness is a **strict quadratic maximum** of \(\Delta\), and the curvature defect \(K\) is strictly **positive** at the witness.

That is the corrected theorem.

## 3. Why this is actually good news

This sign correction makes the overlap transport story much more coherent than before.

Earlier I had a contradictory picture where \(K\) was positive near critical overlap ends but negative at the witness. That would have forced awkward sign changes.

Now the local witness sign matches the endpoint asymptotics:

- near a critical overlap endpoint, the critical-side inverse curvature blows up and one gets
  \[
  K(q)\to +\infty;
  \]
- at a nontranslation witness under 2D closure,
  \[
  K(q_*)>0.
  \]

So the natural global conjecture is now:

\[
\boxed{
K(q)\ge 0 \quad \text{on the whole overlap interval.}
}
\]

If that were true, then
\[
W'(q)=-K(q)\le 0,
\]
so \(W=\Delta'\) would be monotone decreasing.

That immediately gives a very strong overlap picture:

- in CC, there is a **unique** witness;
- in CR/RC, witness existence reduces exactly to the endpoint slope comparison;
- in RR, witness existence reduces to the finite endpoint sign test
  \[
  W_->0>W_+.
  \]

So the sign correction actually makes a global \(K\ge 0\) theorem look like the right endgame.

## 4. Corrected local normal form at a witness

At a nontranslation witness \(q_*\), under 2D closure, we now have the local expansion
\[
\Delta(q)
=
\Delta(q_*)
-\frac{K(q_*)}{2}(q-q_*)^2
+O((q-q_*)^3),
\qquad
K(q_*)>0.
\]

So the witness is a strict local maximum of the outer gap. Equivalently, the two outer branches move apart before \(q_*\) and then come back together after \(q_*\).

This is the exact geometric picture one should now keep in mind.

## 5. Corrected role of translated replication

The translated branch is still the same:
\[
\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u)
\]
on a nontrivial left-run subinterval.

But now, in overlap, this branch is no longer competing with a negative \(K\)-well; it is competing with a **positive quadratic maximum** branch. That makes the dichotomy cleaner:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either exact local translated replication of the outer-run germ,}
}
\]
or
\[
\boxed{
\text{strict local quadratic maximum with }K(q_*)>0.
}
\]

That is the corrected local transport fork.

## 6. Best next theorem target after the correction

With the sign fixed, the best next attack is now more compelling:

### Positivity theorem for \(K\)
Try to prove
\[
K(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))\ge 0
\qquad (q\in I_q)
\]
on every overlap interval where the \(\Delta/\Xi\) setup applies.

Because then:
- \(W'=-K\le 0\), so \(W\) is decreasing;
- CC gives a unique witness automatically;
- CR/RC witness existence becomes exactly the endpoint slope comparison;
- RR becomes a finite endpoint sign problem.

This is much cleaner than the earlier idea of proving a sign change for \(K\).

## 7. A second good target: CR/RC endpoint slope comparison

With the sign correction, the CR/RC endpoint slope problem is even more natural.

In CR, witness existence is equivalent to
\[
W(q_+^-)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u_3)}
-
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u_{0,3})}
<0,
\]
i.e.
\[
\widehat Q'(u_{0,3})>\widehat Q'(u_3).
\]

In RC, witness existence is equivalent to
\[
\widehat Q'(u_0)>\widehat Q'(u_{2,0}).
\]

So CR/RC are now reduced to the same concrete question:

> at the shared overlap endpoint value, is the “interior” outer branch steeper than the “regular endpoint” outer branch?

That is the clearest next comparison problem I can see.

## 8. Bottom line

The corrected local picture is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{in the actual overlap regime, a same-value / same-slope witness is a local maximum of }\Delta,
}
\]
so under branch-independent 2D closure:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every nontranslation witness satisfies }K(q_*)>0.
}
\]

This makes the most natural global closure target:

\[
\boxed{
K\ge 0 \text{ on the overlap interval.}
}
\]

If that lands, the whole overlap lane becomes a monotonicity problem for
\[
W=\Delta',
\]
and only the translated-replication branch remains separate.

So the next sharp attacks are now:

1. prove \(K\ge 0\) on overlap intervals;
2. prove the CR/RC endpoint slope comparison;
3. kill translated replication.

That is the corrected and sharpened continuation of the attack.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:55:16)

I’m attacking the overlap lane in its corrected form now. The two most promising routes are: derive a direct comparison law that makes \(K(q)\ge 0\) automatic, or derive a one-crossing theorem for \(W=\Delta'\) from the reciprocal-slope dynamics so the witness structure becomes rigid.

_Thought for 27s_

Here is the next attack. I think the cleanest way forward is to stop aiming immediately for a global sign theorem \(K\ge 0\), and instead derive the strongest **one-crossing theorem** for
\[
W(q):=\Delta'(q)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}
-
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}
\]
that the current structure plausibly supports.

Because once \(W\) is known to cross at most once, the whole overlap lane becomes rigid:

- in CC, the witness is automatic and unique;
- in CR/RC, witness existence reduces to one endpoint slope inequality;
- in RR, witness existence reduces to the sign of the two finite endpoint values of \(W\).

So the real target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove }W\text{ has at most one zero on the overlap interval.}
}
\]

That is a better attack than trying to prove \(K\ge 0\) cold.

## 1. Exact differential system for \((W,K)\)

Recall
\[
W(q)=\Delta'(q),\qquad
K(q)=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q)),
\qquad
W'(q)=-K(q).
\]

Now differentiate \(K\). Since
\[
\frac{d}{dq}\Xi(\phi_i(q))
=
\frac{\Xi'(\phi_i(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))}
=
J_1(\phi_i(q))\,\Xi'(\phi_i(q)),
\]
we get
\[
K'(q)
=
\frac{\Xi'(\phi_2(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}
-
\frac{\Xi'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]

So the overlap dynamics always satisfy the exact first-order system
\[
W'=-K,
\qquad
K'=\mathcal E(q),
\]
where
\[
\mathcal E(q):=
\frac{\Xi'(\phi_2(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}
-
\frac{\Xi'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]

This is already useful: witness structure is controlled not by \(K\) alone, but by the **pair** \((W,K)\), with \(\mathcal E\) the next transport defect.

So the right hierarchy is now:
- \(W\): same-value / same-slope defect;
- \(K\): curvature defect;
- \(\mathcal E\): next-order transport defect.

That makes the overlap lane a true scalar transport hierarchy.

## 2. A strong local fact at any nontranslation witness

At any witness \(q_*\) with \(W(q_*)=0\), the corrected sign analysis gives:

- under 2D closure and nontranslation,
  \[
  K(q_*)>0,
  \]
  hence
  \[
  W'(q_*)<0.
  \]

So every nontranslation witness is a **strict simple crossing from \(W>0\) to \(W<0\)**.

That is stronger than “local maximum of \(\Delta\).” It says the witness is a genuine one-way crossing of \(W\).

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{under 2D closure, every nontranslation witness is simple and downward-crossing.}
}
\]

This already almost gives one-crossing, because it prevents tangential or oscillatory re-entry through the same zero.

## 3. Why multiple witnesses would force a forbidden sign pattern

Suppose there are two distinct nontranslation witnesses
\[
q_1<q_2
\]
in the overlap interval.

Since each is a downward crossing, we have:

- just left of \(q_1\), \(W>0\);
- just right of \(q_1\), \(W<0\);
- just left of \(q_2\), \(W>0\);
- just right of \(q_2\), \(W<0\).

So between \(q_1\) and \(q_2\), \(W\) must go from negative back to positive. Therefore \(W\) must have a local minimum somewhere between them at a point \(r\) with
\[
W(r)<0,\qquad W'(r)=0.
\]
But \(W'(r)=0\) means
\[
K(r)=0.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{two nontranslation witnesses force an interior point with }W<0\text{ and }K=0.
}
\]

This is the exact place where one now needs control of the next transport level \(\mathcal E=K'\).

If at every point with \(K=0\) in the overlap regime we had
\[
K'(q)\ge 0,
\]
then \(K\) could cross from positive to negative but not back in the way needed to make \(W\) rise from negative to positive again. More generally, a one-sided sign law on \(K'\) along the zero set of \(K\) would yield one-crossing of \(W\).

This suggests the right next theorem target is not global \(K\ge 0\), but the weaker and more flexible:

### Zero-set sign theorem for \(K\)
At any overlap point where \(K(q)=0\), one has
\[
K'(q)\ge 0
\]
(or the opposite sign, depending on orientation conventions).

That would already force \(W\) to have at most one downward zero.

This is a much more focused target.

## 4. Endpoint structure + downward crossing already solves CC under one-crossing

In CC we know
\[
W(q)\to +\infty \quad (q\downarrow q_-),
\qquad
W(q)\to -\infty \quad (q\uparrow q_+).
\]
So if one can prove “every witness is simple downward and there is at most one,” then CC is finished:

\[
\boxed{
\text{CC overlap has exactly one nontranslation witness.}
}
\]

That would be a very strong structural theorem for the live overlap case.

So the one-crossing attack is not just technically cleaner; it would give a final normal form for the CC overlap lane.

## 5. The right reduced target: monotonicity of \(W\) at its zeros

The full sign theorem \(K\ge 0\) would imply \(W'=-K\le 0\), hence one-crossing immediately. But that is stronger than needed.

What we really need is only:

1. every witness is downward:
   \[
   W(q_*)=0 \Longrightarrow W'(q_*)<0
   \]
   in the nontranslation branch — already supplied by 2D closure;
2. \(W\) cannot return from negative to positive without violating some sign law for \(K\) or \(K'\).

So the new attack target is:

### One-crossing principle for \(W\)
Show that once \(W\) becomes negative in the overlap interval, it cannot become positive again.

This is weaker than \(W\) monotone decreasing globally, but strong enough for everything we want.

## 6. How to attack one-crossing: use the next defect \(\mathcal E\)

The exact system
\[
W'=-K,\qquad K'=\mathcal E
\]
suggests a second-order equation
\[
W''=-\mathcal E.
\]

So a sign/comparison theorem for \(\mathcal E\) would control convexity of \(W\). If, for example, one could prove
\[
\mathcal E(q)\ge 0
\]
on the overlap interval, then
\[
W''(q)\le 0,
\]
so \(W\) is concave. A concave function that crosses downward once cannot cross back upward. That would settle one-crossing immediately.

So the sharpened hierarchy of useful targets is now:

1. strongest: \(K\ge 0\) globally;
2. weaker but sufficient: \(\mathcal E\ge 0\) globally, giving concavity of \(W\);
3. weaker still: a sign law for \(K'\) on the zero set of \(K\);
4. weakest local version: enough sign control to prevent \(W\) from returning positive after a witness.

This is a much better-organized endgame.

## 7. Connection back to closure

Under branch-independent 2D closure, \(\mathcal E\) is not arbitrary. Since
\[
\Xi'(u)=H\!\left(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\Xi(u)\right),
\]
we get
\[
\mathcal E(q)
=
\mathcal J_{1,2}(q)\,
H\!\left(q,\mathcal J_{1,2}(q),-\mathcal J_{2,2}(q)\right)
-
\mathcal J_{1,0}(q)\,
H\!\left(q,\mathcal J_{1,0}(q),-\mathcal J_{2,0}(q)\right).
\]

So at points where \(K=0\) and \(W=0\), the two branches have the same \(\mathcal J_1\) and \(\mathcal J_2\), hence also the same \(\mathcal E\). That means higher-order branching only begins after the witness; there is no immediate hidden jump in the third transport level at the witness itself under 2D closure.

This supports the idea that \(W\) should behave in a one-crossing way, not oscillate wildly.

## 8. Best next attack from here

The best next move is now:

### Attack a concavity or zero-set sign theorem
Try to prove one of the following on the overlap interval:

- \(\mathcal E(q)\ge 0\), so \(W\) is concave;
- or \(K'(q)\ge 0\) whenever \(K(q)=0\);
- or directly: once \(W\le 0\), it cannot become positive again.

Any one of these would convert the overlap witness problem into a rigid one-crossing problem.

That is a cleaner and probably more tractable target than global \(K\ge 0\).

## 9. Bottom line

The main new sharpening is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{under 2D closure, every nontranslation witness is a strict downward zero of }W=\Delta';
}
\]
so the real unresolved issue is not local witness structure anymore, but whether \(W\) can have **more than one** such zero.

And that has now been reduced to a sign/convexity problem for the next defect level:
\[
W'=-K,\qquad W''=-\mathcal E.
\]

So the overlap lane is now best attacked as a **one-crossing problem for \(W\)**, rather than directly as a sign theorem for \(K\).
## User (2026-04-22 01:57:20)

attack!

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:58:05)

_Thought for 37s_

I found a much cleaner attack, and it gives a real theorem.

The key observation is that under the branch-independent 2D closure law, **every nontranslation zero of**
\[
W(q):=\Delta'(q)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}
-
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}
\]
is automatically a **strict downward crossing**. Once you have that, a one-crossing theorem is immediate.

That is much stronger than the earlier “maybe prove \(K\ge 0\)” route.

---

## 1. Setup

We are in the genuine outer-overlap regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
so the outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:I_q\to\mathbf R_u
\]
exist on the open overlap interval
\[
I_q=\Omega_0\cap\Omega_2.
\]

Define
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\qquad
W(q):=\Delta'(q).
\]
Also define
\[
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\qquad
K(q):=\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q)).
\]
Then
\[
W'(q)=\Delta''(q)=-K(q).
\]

Now assume the branch-independent 2D closure law on the reciprocal-slope pair:
\[
\Xi'(u)=H\!\left(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\Xi(u)\right)
\]
on the two outer runs.

Equivalently, the branchwise pairs
\[
Y_i(q):=\left(\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))},\ -\Xi(\phi_i(q))\right)
\]
satisfy the same first-order ODE in \(q\).

---

## 2. Zero classification theorem for \(W\)

Here is the main local theorem.

### Theorem (zero classification under 2D closure)
Let \(q_*\in I_q\) satisfy
\[
W(q_*)=0.
\]
Then exactly one of the following holds:

1. **local translated replication**:
   \[
   \phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C
   \quad\text{near }q_*;
   \]

2. **strict downward crossing**:
   \[
   W'(q_*)<0.
   \]

### Proof
The condition \(W(q_*)=0\) means
\[
\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q_*))}
=
\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q_*))},
\]
so the first reciprocal-slope components agree at \(q_*\).

Now either
\[
K(q_*)=0
\]
or not.

If \(K(q_*)=0\), then
\[
\Xi(\phi_2(q_*))=\Xi(\phi_0(q_*)),
\]
so the two outer branches also agree in the second component of the 2D state \(Y_i\). Since both branchwise states satisfy the same first-order ODE in \(q\), uniqueness gives
\[
Y_2(q)\equiv Y_0(q)
\]
near \(q_*\). In particular
\[
W(q)\equiv 0
\]
near \(q_*\), hence
\[
\Delta(q)\equiv C
\]
near \(q_*\). This is local translated replication.

If \(K(q_*)\neq 0\), then from the earlier witness-sign analysis the nontranslation branch forces
\[
K(q_*)>0,
\]
hence
\[
W'(q_*)=-K(q_*)<0.
\]
So the zero is a strict downward crossing. ∎

This is the cleanest local result so far.

---

## 3. Immediate corollary: one-crossing theorem

Now the global consequence is immediate.

### Corollary (one-crossing theorem)
Assume the 2D closure law above, and exclude the translated-replication branch. Then \(W\) has **at most one zero** on the overlap interval \(I_q\).

### Proof
Every zero of \(W\) is, by the theorem, a strict downward crossing. But a continuous function cannot have two distinct downward crossings: after the first, it is negative immediately to the right; to reach zero again it would have to cross upward somewhere, which is impossible since every zero is downward. ∎

This is the clean global theorem I was trying to get by much more complicated sign/convexity arguments.

So we no longer need a global sign theorem for \(K\) just to control witness multiplicity.

---

## 4. Consequences by overlap regime

This now rigidifies the whole overlap lane.

### CC overlap
In CC we already know
\[
W(q)\to +\infty \quad (q\downarrow q_-),
\qquad
W(q)\to -\infty \quad (q\uparrow q_+).
\]
So \(W\) has at least one zero. By the one-crossing theorem, excluding translated replication, it has **exactly one** zero.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{In CC overlap, under 2D closure and excluding translated replication, there is a unique same-value / same-slope witness.}
}
\]

### CR overlap
In CR we have
\[
W(q)\to +\infty \quad (q\downarrow q_-),
\]
and
\[
W(q)\to W_+ \quad (q\uparrow q_+)
\]
with finite
\[
W_+=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u_3)}-\frac1{\widehat Q'(u_{0,3})}.
\]
So:
- if \(W_+<0\), there is exactly one witness;
- if \(W_+\ge 0\), there is no witness.

### RC overlap
Similarly:
- if
  \[
  W_->0,
  \]
  there is exactly one witness;
- if \(W_-\le 0\), there is no witness.

### RR overlap
Both endpoint limits are finite. So:
- if \(W_->0>W_+\), there is exactly one witness;
- otherwise there is no witness.

So under 2D closure and excluding translated replication, the whole overlap lane reduces to a **finite endpoint sign test**.

That is a huge simplification.

---

## 5. This makes CR/RC endpoint-slope comparison the correct next target

Since one-crossing is now automatic once 2D closure is available, the real problem in CR/RC is no longer “could there be many witnesses?” It is just endpoint sign.

In CR, the existence criterion is
\[
\frac1{\widehat Q'(u_3)}-\frac1{\widehat Q'(u_{0,3})}<0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\widehat Q'(u_{0,3})>\widehat Q'(u_3).
\]

In RC, the criterion is
\[
\widehat Q'(u_0)>\widehat Q'(u_{2,0}).
\]

So the whole CR/RC overlap witness problem is exactly a **same-value endpoint slope comparison theorem** on the two outer runs.

That is much sharper than anything I had earlier.

---

## 6. The translated branch is now the only local loophole

The theorem also tells us something conceptually important.

At a zero of \(W\), there is no third option. Under 2D closure:
- either the outer branches are locally translated copies,
- or the zero is a strict downward crossing.

So all the earlier vague talk about more complicated local witness structure is gone. The only local loophole left is translated replication:
\[
\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C.
\]

And that branch is extremely rigid, since it means
\[
\widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u)
\]
on a nontrivial subinterval of a monotone outer run.

So the overlap endgame is now cleanly split into:

1. kill translated replication;
2. compare endpoint slopes.

That is an excellent reduction.

---

## 7. Strongest practical takeaway

The strongest practical theorem I now believe is:

### Overlap rigidity theorem, conditional form
Assume branch-independent 2D closure on the outer runs and exclude translated replication. Then on any open overlap interval \(I_q\):

- \(W=\Delta'\) has at most one zero;
- CC overlap has exactly one witness;
- CR/RC/RR overlap have either zero or one witness, determined solely by endpoint signs of \(W\).

Equivalently, the entire overlap witness problem becomes a finite endpoint comparison problem.

That is probably the cleanest theorem-shaped reduction on this lane so far.

---

## 8. Best next attacks from here

The best next moves are now very clear.

First, try to prove the 2D closure law
\[
\Xi'(u)=H\!\left(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\Xi(u)\right)
\]
on the outer runs. That would unlock the one-crossing theorem.

Second, attack translated replication directly:
\[
\widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u)
\]
on a monotone outer subinterval should be impossible unless there is some very special symmetry.

Third, attack the CR/RC endpoint slope comparison:
\[
\widehat Q'(u_{0,3})>\widehat Q'(u_3)
\quad\text{or}\quad
\widehat Q'(u_0)>\widehat Q'(u_{2,0}).
\]

If either translated replication is killed or the endpoint comparison is proved, the overlap regime becomes very rigid.

---

## 9. Bottom line

The main new mathematical gain is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Under branch-independent 2D closure, every nontranslation zero of }W=\Delta'
\text{ is a strict downward crossing.}
}
\]

From that:

\[
\boxed{
\text{excluding translated replication, }W\text{ has at most one zero on the overlap interval.}
}
\]

So the corrected overlap transport lane is no longer a messy nonlocal problem. It is now, conditionally, a **one-crossing endpoint-comparison problem**. That is the sharpest continuation of the attack I can honestly give right now.
